The Nature of Logic: Part of Philosophy or a Preamble?
Author introduces the first disputed general issue: whether logic is a part of philosophy or a preamble to it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two competing views on the nature of logic and explain the historical evidence for Aristotle's position as inferred by historians.

Core Idea
The status of logic as part of philosophy or a preamble was a key debate among Islamic logicians.

This passage from the Manahij al-Bahth introduces a fundamental question that arose among Islamic commentators: is logic a branch of philosophy or merely an instrument (preamble) for it? The answer has implications for how we study logic and its relationship to other Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Recognize that the issue is whether logic is a part of philosophy or a preamble prior to it. The passage explicitly presents this dichotomy.
2
Note Aristotle's silence
Aristotle did not give an explicit idea about this issue. Historians rely on his classification of sciences to infer his stance.
3
Understand the inference
Since logic is absent from Aristotle's division of theoretical sciences, historians concluded that he considered logic a preamble, not a part of philosophy.
4
Appreciate the Islamic expansion
This issue entered the logic of Islamic commentators, differing from the Aristotelian heritage. The Peripatetics and Stoics differed on this point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأولى المسائل العامة التي دخلت في منطق الشراح الإسلاميين ولم تكن في تراث أرسطو، واختلف المشاؤون والرواقيون الإسلاميون فيها، هي البحث في طبيعة المنطق : هل هو جزء من الفلسفة أو هو مقدمة سابقة عليها ؟ لم يعط أرسطو فكرة صريحة عن هذه المسائل، ولكن لاحظ المؤرخون أنه ليس ثمة مكان للمنطق في تقسيمه للعلوم النظرية . ومن هنا استنتجوا أن المنطق عند أرسطو ليس جزءاً من الفلسفة ولكن مقدمة فقط لها(١).
And the first of the general issues that entered the logic of the Islamic commentators and was not in Aristotle's heritage, and concerning which the Islamic Peripatetics and Stoics differed, is the investigation into the nature of logic: is it a part of philosophy or is it a preamble prior to it? Aristotle did not give an explicit idea about these issues, but historians have noted that there is no place for logic in his division of the theoretical sciences. From this they concluded that logic for Aristotle is not a part of philosophy but only a preamble to it.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage announces the first general issue in Islamic logic: the status of logic relative to philosophy. The author notes that this issue was not present in Aristotle's own work but was introduced by Islamic commentators. The Peripatetics and Stoics differed on this. Aristotle did not explicitly address it, but historians infer from his classification of sciences that logic is not a part of philosophy (since it is omitted from his division of theoretical sciences) but rather a preamble or tool (organon). This sets the stage for further discussion and disagreement among the Islamic schools.

Key Terms
طبيعة المنطق
مقدمة

C) Aristotle said logic is a tool for all sciences
D) Both B and C

Key Takeaway
The first general issue in Islamic logic concerned whether logic is a part of philosophy or a preamble, a debate introduced by commentators based on an inference from Aristotle's classification of sciences.

The Five Aspects of Studying Words in Islamic Logic
Author justifies word studies in logic and lists five aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the rationale for including word studies in Islamic logic and list the five aspects under which words are examined.

Core Idea
Words in logic are studied as meaning indicators, not as mere linguistic items.

This passage establishes why Islamic logicians dedicated significant attention to words: because concepts (tasawwurat) are defined as apprehensions of simples (mufradat), which are words. The author outlines a fivefold schema for studying words, despite a minority objection from Abu al-Barakat, showing the depth of the Islamic logical tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim about concepts and words
Find the sentence that equates the study of concepts with the study of words. It states that a concept is the apprehension of a simple, and the simple is a word. This justifies the entire enterprise.
The claim is foundational: logic begins with words because it deals with concepts.
2
Understand the justification for word studies
The author says Islamic logicians study words not in themselves but in relation to meanings. This distinguishes their approach from pure linguistics. They expanded beyond Aristotle's Organon.
3
Recognize the dissenting view
Abu al-Barakat al-Baghdadi argued that studying words as meaning indicators belongs to linguistics, not logic. Note that he still included word studies in his own book, showing the strength of the tradition.
His objection is based on subject matter boundaries.
4
List the five aspects
Enumerate them exactly as given: (1) indication of word onto meaning, (2) generality/particularity, (3) simplicity/compound, (4) the word itself, (5) relation of words to meanings.
Memorize these as the framework for subsequent lessons.
5
Distinguish the aspects
Group them logically: aspects 1,2,5 deal with semantics (meaning); aspect 3 deals with structure (syntax); aspect 4 deals with lexical properties (word in itself).
This categorization aids in applying the aspects to Islamic texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التصورات وهي مباحث الألفاظ . وسنعرض الآن لمباحث التصورات على التوالي موضحين تلك العناصر التي ذكرناها على الخصوص . وأول مباحثها كما قلنا هو مبحث الألفاظ . مباحث الألفاظ وجاءت صلة تلك المباحث بالمنطق على العموم وبالتصورات على الخصوص من أن التصور ـ كما يعرفه المناطقة ـ هو إدراك المفرد والمفرد ليس إلا لفظاً ـ وتحت تأثير هذا أخذ الإسلاميون يتدارسون الألفاظ دراسة واسعة لم يبحثها من قبل صاحب الأورجانون ، معللين هذا بأنهم لن يبحثوا في اللفظ في ذاته بل في اللفظ من حيث صلته بالمعاني . هذه فكرة تسود فريق الشراح الإسلاميين جميعهم ، نجدها عند ابن سينا والغزالي كما نجدها عند المتأخرين . ولم يشذ عن ذلك سوى أبي البركات البغدادي ، فقد تنبه أبو البركات إلى أن هذه الأبحاث غريبة عن منطق أرسطو ، فأنكر أن يكون موضوع المنطق « الألفاظ من حيث تدل على المعاني لأن هذا هو علم اللغات » . أما صلة المنطق باللغة فهي صلة عرضية كدخول اللغة في سائر العلوم والصناعات ، ويشبه هذا القول بأن الكتابة من المنطق لأن المنطق ، يكتب في الكتب . ومع أن أبا البركات لا يوافق على اعتبار مباحث الألفاظ من المنطق إلا أنه يبحثها أيضاً في كتابه ، وتبدأ هذه الأبحاث اللفظية ، كما تبدأ كتب المنطق الأخرى ، بالبحث المشهور « دلالة الألفاظ على المعاني »(۱) والبحث في الألفاظ عند المسلمين ينظر اليه من خمسة أوجه : أولاً ـ من ناحية دلالة اللفظ على المعنى . ثانياً ـ من ناحية قسمة اللفظ إلى عموم المعنى وخصوصه . ثالثاً ـ النظر في اللفظ من حيث الإفراد والتركيب . رابعاً ـ النظر في اللفظ نفسه . خامساً ـ نسبة الألفاظ إلى المعاني . (۱) أبو البركات البغدادي . المعتبر ( طبع دائرة المعارف النظامية بالهند ) جـ ١ ص ٦ . ٤٥
Concepts are the studies of words. And we will now present the studies of concepts in order, explaining those elements we mentioned in particular. And the first of its studies, as we said, is the study of words. The study of words: The connection of these studies to logic in general and to concepts in particular came from the fact that the concept — as the logicians define it — is the apprehension of the simple, and the simple is nothing but a word. Under this influence, the Islamic scholars took to studying words extensively, which the author of the Organon had not studied before, justifying this by saying that they would not study the word in itself but the word in terms of its connection to meanings. This idea prevails among all the group of Islamic commentators; we find it with Ibn Sina and al-Ghazali as we find it with the later ones. And no one deviated from that except Abu al-Barakat al-Baghdadi, for Abu al-Barakat realized that these studies are foreign to Aristotle's logic, so he denied that the subject of logic is 'words in terms of their indicating meanings because this is the science of languages.' As for the connection of logic to language, it is an accidental connection, like the entry of language in all sciences and crafts, and this resembles the saying that writing is part of logic because logic is written in books. And although Abu al-Barakat does not agree to consider the studies of words as part of logic, he still discusses them in his book, and these verbal studies begin, as other logic books begin, with the famous study 'The indication of words onto meanings' (1). And the study of words according to the Muslims is viewed from five aspects: First: from the perspective of the indication of the word onto the meaning. Second: from the perspective of the division of the word into generality and particularity of meaning. Third: considering the word in terms of simplicity and composition. Fourth: considering the word itself. Fifth: the relation of words to meanings. (1) Abu al-Barakat al-Baghdadi. Al-Mu'tabar (printed by the Academy of Sciences in India), Vol. 1, p. 6. 45
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This is an introductory passage that sets the stage for the study of words within logic. The author begins by stating that the discussion of concepts (tasawwurat) is essentially the discussion of words, because a concept is defined as the apprehension of a simple (mufrad), and a simple is nothing but a word. This establishes the foundational link between logic and language. The author then notes that Islamic logicians expanded the study of words far beyond what Aristotle (the 'author of the Organon') had done, justifying this by clarifying that they study words not in themselves but in their relation to meanings. This view is widespread among Islamic commentators, from Ibn Sina and al-Ghazali to later scholars. The sole exception is Abu al-Barakat al-Baghdadi, who argued that this belongs to linguistics, not logic; for him, logic's connection to language is merely accidental, like writing's connection to logic. Despite his objection, Abu al-Barakat still discusses words in his own book. The passage then lists the five aspects under which words are studied: (1) indication of word onto meaning, (2) generality and particularity, (3) simplicity and composition, (4) the word itself, and (5) the relation of words to meanings. This enumeration provides the framework for the subsequent detailed discussions.

Key Terms
مباحث الألفاظ
تصور
مفرد

D) Abu al-Salt al-Dani

Key Takeaway
The five aspects of word studies (indication, generality/particularity, simplicity/composition, word itself, relation to meanings) provide the basic framework for all subsequent logical discussions in the Islamic tradition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
List the five aspects of word studies in Islamic logic, and for each aspect, provide an example from usul al-fiqh (e.g., indication: the word 'salat' indicates the prayer; generality: the word 'salat' is used in the Quran both generally and specifically).
?

Meaning in Itself and Its Investigation
Author introduces meaning in itself and its connection to words.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the logician's study of meaning is independent from linguistics and how Islamic commentators expanded the Aristotelian framework.

Core Idea
Meaning is the goal of word study, yet it cannot be defined without words.

In the previous lesson, we considered words as the starting point of logic. Now the author dives deeper into what words point to: meaning. This lesson clarifies the logician's unique perspective on meaning and how Islamic commentators enriched this investigation with non-Aristotelian insights. We will see that meaning, although dependent on words for definition, is studied in itself as a fixed entity.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the logician's focus
The author says the logician only engages with words in terms of their connection to meaning. So meaning is the ultimate aim, not words themselves.
2
Recognize the paradox of defining meaning
The passage states meaning cannot be defined without words. So there is a circular dependency; yet we proceed.
3
Identify the Islamic commentators' contribution
The investigation among Islamic commentators was free from linguistic studies, and they introduced non-Aristotelian elements. Because the nature of meaning itself necessitates this.
4
Locate the non-Aristotelian elements
The author says they will later specify these elements. For now, note that this is a departure from pure Aristotelian logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مبحث المعنى في ذاته : ويتصل بهذا البحث السابق ، مبحث المعاني ، لأن اللفظ كما قلنا لا يشتغل به المنطقي إلا من حيث صلته بالمعنى . فالمعنى من حيث هو ثابت في نفسه هو الغاية من البحث في اللفظ ، مع العلم بأن المعنى تدل عليه الألفاظ ، إذ من البديهي أننا لا نستطيع تعريف المعنى بدون اللفظ . وقد خلا هذا البحث عند الشراح الإسلاميين من الأبحاث اللغوية ، لأن طبيعته نفسها تستلزم هذا الخلو ، إذ هو بحث في المعنى ، في ذاته ودخلت في هذا البحث ، كما هو مقرر ، عناصر غير أرسططاليسية ، وسنحدد مكان تلك العناصر في ثنايا مناقشتنا لهذه المباحث . نسبة المعاني إلى مداركنا : وأول ما نلقاه من تلك المباحث هو نسبة المعاني إلى مداركنا ، أو بمعنى أدق كيفية الحصول على تلك المعاني . إننا نتوصل إلى تلك المعاني عن طريقين : طريق الاحساس وطريق الاستدلال العقلي(١) ، وليس هذا المبحث في الواقع منطقياً في ذاته ، بل هو يتصل بنظرية المعرفة ويعالجه الإسلاميون علاجاً ميتافيزيقياً بحثاً يتصل بأفلاطون من ناحية ، وبالأفلاطونية المحدثة من ناحية أخرى . وليس يهمنا هنا عرض تلك المباحث الميتافيزيقية وبحثها ، ولكن أحب أن أشير إلى أنه يتصل بتقسيم الموجودات إلى محسوسة ومعقولة ، تقسيم آخر يشترك فيه أيضاً المنطق والميتافيزيقا وهو تقسيم الموجودات إلى شخصية معينة ، أي الموجودات الجزئية ، وإلى أمور عامة ، أي الكليات . أما الأولى فنتوصل إليها بالاحساس .
Topic of meaning in itself: This topic of meanings is connected to the previous discussion, because the logician, as we said, only engages with the word in terms of its connection to meaning. The meaning, in itself being fixed, is the goal of investigating the word, knowing that words indicate meanings, since it is obvious that we cannot define meaning without the word. And this investigation among the Islamic commentators was free from linguistic studies, because its nature itself necessitates this freedom, since it is an investigation of meaning in itself. And into this investigation entered, as established, non-Aristotelian elements, and we will determine the place of those elements in the midst of our discussion of these topics. The relation of meanings to our perceptions: And the first of those topics we encounter is the relation of meanings to our perceptions, or more precisely the way of obtaining those meanings. We reach those meanings through two paths: the path of sense and the path of intellectual inference (1). This topic is not in reality logical in itself, but it connects to the theory of knowledge, and the Islamic [scholars] treat it in a purely metaphysical treatment connected to Plato on one side, and to Neoplatonism on the other side. And it is not important here to present and investigate those metaphysical topics, but I would like to indicate that it is connected to the division of existents into sensible and intelligible, another division in which logic and metaphysics also share: the division of existents into specific particulars, i.e., particular existents, and general matters, i.e., universals. As for the former, we reach them through sense.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage introduces the topic of meaning (ma'na) in itself, which is a natural follow-up to the previous discussion of words. The author emphasizes that the logician is concerned with words only insofar as they relate to meaning. Meaning itself, being fixed, is the ultimate aim of investigating words, but it is obvious that meaning cannot be defined without words. The author notes that among the Islamic commentators, this investigation of meaning was free from purely linguistic studies because its nature demands it—it is about meaning itself. Furthermore, non-Aristotelian elements entered this investigation, which the author will later identify. Then the author moves to the relation of meanings to our perceptions (al-madarik). The first issue encountered is how we obtain meanings: there are two paths—sense perception (ihsas) and intellectual inference (istidlal 'aqli). The author comments that this topic is not inherently logical; it belongs to epistemology and is treated metaphysically by Islamic scholars, connecting to Plato and Neoplatonism. However, the author does not delve into those metaphysical details here. Instead, the author points out that this relates to the division of existents into sensible (mahsusat) and intelligible (ma'qulat), and another division shared by logic and metaphysics: the division into particular individuals (juz'iyat) and universal matters (kulliyat). The author notes that particulars are reached through sense. This passage thus establishes the epistemological foundation for the subsequent logical discussions.

Key Terms
معنى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what did the Islamic commentators' investigation of meaning lack?
A) Aristotelian elements
B) Linguistic studies
C) Philosophical depth
D) Connection to words

Key Takeaway
The logician studies meaning in itself, but cannot define it without words; Islamic commentators freed this study from linguistics and added non-Aristotelian dimensions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Explain in your own words why the author says the logician's investigation of meaning is free from linguistic studies. Provide an example from Islamic 'ilm al-kalam (theology) where separating meaning from word is crucial.
?
Identify one non-Aristotelian element that might have entered the investigation of meaning according to the author. (Hint: Think of Platonic or Neoplatonic influences.)

Two Paths to Meaning: Sense and Intellect
Author explains how meanings are related to our perceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between sense perception and intellectual inference as sources of meaning, and identify which type of existence (particular or universal) each yields.

Core Idea
Sense yields particulars; intellect yields universals.

We have learned that meaning is the goal. Now the author asks: how do we actually come to know meanings? This lesson introduces the two fundamental epistemological pathways: sense perception (ihsas) and intellectual inference (istidlal 'aqli). The author also connects these to the division of existents into sensible/intelligible and particular/universal. As we will see, this sets the stage for later discussions about definitions and universals.

Guided Path
1
Recall the two paths
The author says we obtain meanings through sense and intellectual inference. Identify these two paths clearly.
2
Connect paths to types of existents
The passage links sense to sensible things and particulars. Intellect presumably leads to intelligibles and universals, though that is implied here.
3
Note the metaphysical dimension
The author says this topic is not purely logical but connects to epistemology and metaphysics, and is treated by Islamic scholars in a Platonic/Neoplatonic framework.
4
Focus on the logical relevance
Despite the metaphysics, the division into particulars and universals is crucial for logic, as universals are the subject of definitions and syllogisms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مبحث المعنى في ذاته : ويتصل بهذا البحث السابق ، مبحث المعاني ، لأن اللفظ كما قلنا لا يشتغل به المنطقي إلا من حيث صلته بالمعنى . فالمعنى من حيث هو ثابت في نفسه هو الغاية من البحث في اللفظ ، مع العلم بأن المعنى تدل عليه الألفاظ ، إذ من البديهي أننا لا نستطيع تعريف المعنى بدون اللفظ . وقد خلا هذا البحث عند الشراح الإسلاميين من الأبحاث اللغوية ، لأن طبيعته نفسها تستلزم هذا الخلو ، إذ هو بحث في المعنى ، في ذاته ودخلت في هذا البحث ، كما هو مقرر ، عناصر غير أرسططاليسية ، وسنحدد مكان تلك العناصر في ثنايا مناقشتنا لهذه المباحث . نسبة المعاني إلى مداركنا : وأول ما نلقاه من تلك المباحث هو نسبة المعاني إلى مداركنا ، أو بمعنى أدق كيفية الحصول على تلك المعاني . إننا نتوصل إلى تلك المعاني عن طريقين : طريق الاحساس وطريق الاستدلال العقلي(١) ، وليس هذا المبحث في الواقع منطقياً في ذاته ، بل هو يتصل بنظرية المعرفة ويعالجه الإسلاميون علاجاً ميتافيزيقياً بحثاً يتصل بأفلاطون من ناحية ، وبالأفلاطونية المحدثة من ناحية أخرى . وليس يهمنا هنا عرض تلك المباحث الميتافيزيقية وبحثها ، ولكن أحب أن أشير إلى أنه يتصل بتقسيم الموجودات إلى محسوسة ومعقولة ، تقسيم آخر يشترك فيه أيضاً المنطق والميتافيزيقا وهو تقسيم الموجودات إلى شخصية معينة ، أي الموجودات الجزئية ، وإلى أمور عامة ، أي الكليات . أما الأولى فنتوصل إليها بالاحساس .
Topic of meaning in itself: This topic of meanings is connected to the previous discussion, because the logician, as we said, only engages with the word in terms of its connection to meaning. The meaning, in itself being fixed, is the goal of investigating the word, knowing that words indicate meanings, since it is obvious that we cannot define meaning without the word. And this investigation among the Islamic commentators was free from linguistic studies, because its nature itself necessitates this freedom, since it is an investigation of meaning in itself. And into this investigation entered, as established, non-Aristotelian elements, and we will determine the place of those elements in the midst of our discussion of these topics. The relation of meanings to our perceptions: And the first of those topics we encounter is the relation of meanings to our perceptions, or more precisely the way of obtaining those meanings. We reach those meanings through two paths: the path of sense and the path of intellectual inference (1). This topic is not in reality logical in itself, but it connects to the theory of knowledge, and the Islamic [scholars] treat it in a purely metaphysical treatment connected to Plato on one side, and to Neoplatonism on the other side. And it is not important here to present and investigate those metaphysical topics, but I would like to indicate that it is connected to the division of existents into sensible and intelligible, another division in which logic and metaphysics also share: the division of existents into specific particulars, i.e., particular existents, and general matters, i.e., universals. As for the former, we reach them through sense.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage introduces the topic of meaning (ma'na) in itself, which is a natural follow-up to the previous discussion of words. The author emphasizes that the logician is concerned with words only insofar as they relate to meaning. Meaning itself, being fixed, is the ultimate aim of investigating words, but it is obvious that meaning cannot be defined without words. The author notes that among the Islamic commentators, this investigation of meaning was free from purely linguistic studies because its nature demands it—it is about meaning itself. Furthermore, non-Aristotelian elements entered this investigation, which the author will later identify. Then the author moves to the relation of meanings to our perceptions (al-madarik). The first issue encountered is how we obtain meanings: there are two paths—sense perception (ihsas) and intellectual inference (istidlal 'aqli). The author comments that this topic is not inherently logical; it belongs to epistemology and is treated metaphysically by Islamic scholars, connecting to Plato and Neoplatonism. However, the author does not delve into those metaphysical details here. Instead, the author points out that this relates to the division of existents into sensible (mahsusat) and intelligible (ma'qulat), and another division shared by logic and metaphysics: the division into particular individuals (juz'iyat) and universal matters (kulliyat). The author notes that particulars are reached through sense. This passage thus establishes the epistemological foundation for the subsequent logical discussions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Give an example from Islamic jurisprudence where a ruling is derived from a particular event (sensed) vs. a universal principle (intellectually inferred).
?
According to the passage, the topic of the relation of meanings to perceptions is not purely logical. Why does the author still include it in a logic textbook?

Al-Shafi'i's Systematization of Usul al-Fiqh
Author describes al-Shafi'i's systematization of usul al-fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key elements of al-Shafi'i's systematization of usul al-fiqh as described in the passage.

Core Idea
Al-Shafi'i gave usul al-fiqh its first universal law, transforming scattered debates into a disciplined science.

In this passage, the author explains that al-Shafi'i did not invent usul al-fiqh from scratch but refined and organized existing methods. He added rhetorical investigations, expanded principles, and established a universal law for deriving rulings. This lesson walks you through his contributions.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the prior state
Before al-Shafi'i, scholars discussed usul al-fiqh and adduced evidence but lacked a unified system or universal law (qanun kulli).
2
Identify what al-Shafi'i added
Al-Shafi'i analyzed the analogical method, added rhetorical investigations (mabahith bayaniyya), and completed existing principles.
The early Arabs didn't need rhetoric because of natural linguistic mastery, but later generations did.
3
Understand the universal law
According to al-Razi, al-Shafi'i deduced the science of usul al-fiqh and created a qanun kulli for knowing and ranking Sharia proofs.
4
See the result
He built the branches of his school on these principles, systematizing the derivation of rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أصله الفقهي الذي خرج عنه . أما إقامة الفروع على الأصول فهي المحاولة التي قام بها الشافعي .
وصل المنهج إذن إلى الشافعي وكان قد مر عليه تطورات عدة كما رأينا بلغت به حداً كبيراً من النضوج . ولا ينقص من قدر العمل الذي قام به الشافعي . وصول جوهر المنهج القياسي إليه . فقد قام الشافعي بتحليل هذا المنهج وعرضه في صورة منظمة ، وأضاف إليه أبحاثاً كثيرة أهمها المباحث البيانية التي لم يكن الصدر الأول في حاجة إليها ، حين كان الكلام ملكة للعرب . وتوسع في جميع المباحث الأصولية التي عرفت من قبل وأكمل بعضها . وأقام فروع المذهب على الأصول . فالشافعي له في وضع المنهج فضل لا ينكر. يقول الرازي : «كان الناس قبل الشافعي يتكلمون في مسائل أصول الفقه ويستدلون ويعترضون . ولكن ما كان لهم قانون كلي مرجوع اليه في معرفة دلائل الشريعة وفي كيفية معارضتها وترجيحها ـ فاستنبط الشافعي علم أصول الفقه ، ووضع للخلق قانوناً كلياً يرجع في معرفة مراتب أدلة الشرع اليه »(۱) .
ويقول مصطفى عبد الرازق : « كان اتجاه المذاهب الفقهية قبل الشافعي الى جمع المسائل وترتيبها وردها إلى أدلتها التفصيلية خصوصاً عندما تكون دلائلها نصوصاً وأهل الحديث لكثرة اعتمادهم على النص كانوا أكثر تعرضاً لذكر الدلائل من أهل الرأي . فلما جاء الشافعي بمذهبه الجديد كان قد درس المذهبين . ولاحظ ما فيهما من نقص بدا له أن يكمله . وأخذ ينقض بعض التعريفات من ناحية خروجها عن متابعة نظام متحد في طريقة الاستنباط »(۲) وهذه الطريقة طريقة فلسفية بحتة . يقول مصطفى عبد الرازق : « إن هذا الاتجاه من الشافعي هو اتجاه العقل العلمي الذي لا يعني بالجزئيات والفروع فكان تفكيره تفكير من ليس يهتم بالمسائل الجزئية والتفاريع . بل يعني بضبط الإستدلالات التفصيلية بأصول تجمعها . وذلك هو النظر الفلسفي »(۱) وقد دعا كل هذا الى اعتبار الشافعي في العالم الإسلامي وفي الدراسات الإسلامية مقابلاً لأرسطو في العالم الهليني وفي الدراسات اليونانية . بحيث نرى ابن حنبل يعتبره فيلسوفاً «الشافعي فيلسوف في أربعة أشياء ـ في اللغة واختلاف الناس والمعاني والفقه »(۲) ويمكننا أن نقول أنه إذا كان قد وجدت قبل أرسطو مناهج منطقية كالإستقراء السقراطي أو القسمة الأفلاطونية كان لها أثر بالغ في تكوين « الأورجانون » فقد وجد الشافعي قبله مناهج أصولية أثرت في تكوين « الرسالة » .
Its original fiqh from which it emerged. As for establishing branches on the principles, that is the attempt made by al-Shafi'i. Thus the method reached al-Shafi'i, and it had undergone several developments as we saw, reaching a high degree of maturity. The essence of the analogical method having reached him does not diminish the value of the work done by al-Shafi'i. He analyzed this method and presented it in an organized form, adding many investigations to it, the most important being the rhetorical investigations, which the early generation did not need when Arabic speech was a natural faculty for the Arabs. He expanded on all the principles investigations known before and completed some of them. He established the branches of the school on the principles. So al-Shafi'i has an undeniable merit in establishing the method. Al-Razi says: 'Before al-Shafi'i, people used to discuss issues of usul al-fiqh, adduce evidence, and object, but they had no universal law to refer to in knowing the proofs of the Sharia and how to oppose and prefer them. So al-Shafi'i deduced the science of usul al-fiqh and laid down for creation a universal law to refer to in knowing the ranks of legal proofs.' Mustafa Abd al-Raziq says: 'The direction of the juristic schools before al-Shafi'i was to collect issues, arrange them, and refer them to their detailed evidence, especially when their evidence was texts; the hadith folk, due to their heavy reliance on texts, were more prone to mentioning evidence than the people of opinion. When al-Shafi'i came with his new school, he had studied the two schools and observed the deficiency in them that seemed to him to complete. He began to refute some definitions on the basis of their deviation from following a unified system in the method of derivation.' This method is purely philosophical. Mustafa Abd al-Raziq says: 'This orientation of al-Shafi'i is the orientation of the scientific mind that does not concern itself with particulars and branches; his thinking was the thinking of one who does not care about partial issues and subdivisions, but rather cares about controlling detailed inferences by principles that collect them. That is philosophical reflection.' All this led to considering al-Shafi'i in the Islamic world and in Islamic studies as equivalent to Aristotle in the Hellenic world and in Greek studies. So we see Ibn Hanbal considering him a philosopher: 'Al-Shafi'i is a philosopher in four things: language, differences of people, meanings, and fiqh.' And we can say that just as there existed before Aristotle logical methods like Socratic induction and Platonic division that had a profound effect on the formation of the 'Organon', so al-Shafi'i found before him principles methods that influenced the formation of the 'Risala'.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage is a historical and methodological analysis of the development of usul al-fiqh, focusing on the role of Imam al-Shafi'i. The author argues that the method of analogy (qiyas) reached al-Shafi'i after prior development, but al-Shafi'i systematized it. He added new investigations, especially in rhetoric (mabahith bayaniyya), which were unnecessary for earlier Arabs who possessed natural linguistic mastery. He expanded and completed previous usul investigations and built the branches of his school on fundamental principles. The author then quotes al-Razi, who emphasizes that before al-Shafi'i, scholars debated usul without a universal law (qanun kulli); al-Shafi'i created such a law for deducing Sharia rulings. Mustafa Abd al-Raziq's quote notes that pre-Shafi'i schools focused on collecting detailed evidence, but al-Shafi'i synthesized the two approaches and introduced a consistent systematic derivation method. The author characterizes al-Shafi'i's approach as purely philosophical, focused on regulating details through unifying principles. This led to al-Shafi'i being seen as the Islamic counterpart to Aristotle—Ibn Hanbal even called him a philosopher. The author concludes that just as pre-Aristotelian logical methods like Socratic induction and Platonic division influenced the Organon, pre-Shafi'i principles methods influenced his Risala, implying a parallel development.

Key Terms
المنهج القياسي (al-manhaj al-qiyasi): The analogical method, specifically the method of reasoning by analogy (qiyas) used in usul al-fiqh. [Note: In logic, qiyas refers to categorical syllogism; here it is legal analogy.]
قانون كلي (qanun kulli): A universal legal principle that governs the derivation of rulings, as established by al-Shafi'i.
الأورجانون (al-urjānūn): The Organon, the collection of Aristotle's logical works, used as a reference for logical methodology. [Note: Standard term in the Arabic tradition for Aristotle's logical corpus.]
الرسالة (al-Risāla): Al-Shafi'i's foundational work on usul al-fiqh, which systematized the methodology.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what did al-Shafi'i contribute that earlier scholars lacked?
A) A universal law (qanun kulli) for deriving legal proofs
B) The concept of analogy (qiyas) itself
C) The collection of detailed evidence for each ruling
D) The study of hadith as a source

Key Takeaway
Al-Shafi'i's key contribution was creating a systematic, universal method for usul al-fiqh, providing a framework that earlier scholars lacked.

?
In your own words, describe what al-Shafi'i contributed that earlier scholars lacked, using evidence from the passage.
?
Compare al-Shafi'i's contribution to the development of usul al-fiqh with how Aristotle systematized logic in the Organon (as mentioned in the passage).

Al-Shafi'i: The Aristotle of Islamic Jurisprudence
Author describes al-Shafi'i's systematization of usul al-fiqh and compares him to Aristotle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the parallel drawn in the passage between al-Shafi'i and Aristotle regarding their roles in systematizing their respective disciplines.

Core Idea
Just as Aristotle gave logic its formal structure, al-Shafi'i gave usul al-fiqh its systematic methodology.

The passage draws a striking comparison: al-Shafi'i is considered in Islamic studies as equivalent to Aristotle in Greek studies. This parallel is not superficial; both men took pre-existing methods and transformed them into comprehensive systems. This lesson examines that comparison.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base methods
Before Aristotle, there were logical methods like Socratic induction and Platonic division. Before al-Shafi'i, there were pre-existing usul methods.
2
See the systematization
Aristotle compiled these methods into the Organon; al-Shafi'i compiled pre-existing principles methods into the Risala.
3
Note the recognition
Ibn Hanbal called al-Shafi'i a philosopher in four things. Al-Shafi'i was seen as the Islamic counterpart to Aristotle.
4
Reflect on the impact
Both works became foundational in their fields—the Organon for logic, the Risala for usul al-fiqh.

Evaluating Claims of Logical Influence: al-Shafi'i and Aristotelian Logic
Author critiques the claim of Aristotelian influence on al-Shafi'i's Risala.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate historical claims of influence between Islamic and Greek logical traditions by analyzing evidence and counterarguments.

Core Idea
Knowledge of a foreign tradition does not imply adoption; indigenous developments often suffice.

This passage critically examines the claim that Imam al-Shafi'i's methodology in the Risala was directly influenced by Aristotelian logic. You will follow the author's argument for and against this thesis, learning how to weigh historical evidence critically.

Guided Path
1
Identify the thesis under examination
The thesis is that al-Shafi'i's usul methodology was directly influenced by Aristotelian logic. The author initially presents this as a claim to be tested.
The thesis is framed as a historical question: were non-Islamic factors like Aristotelian logic influential?
2
Extract the three reasons in favor of the thesis
The author lists three reasons: (1) logic was already translated and al-Shafi'i studied kalam and astronomy; (2) al-Shafi'i knew Greek; (3) al-Shafi'i viewed qiyas usuli as probable, similar to Aristotle's tamthil.
These are the evidential pillars for the influence claim.
3
Analyze the author's refutation of each reason
The author refutes each: knowledge of logic does not mean adoption (al-Shafi'i knew kalam but didn't engage; mutakallimun knew logic but didn't adopt); knowledge of Greek is not proof; the shared view on probability is insufficient. He also notes that usul methodology was already largely formed before al-Shafi'i.
The refutation hinges on distinguishing between 'awareness' and 'influence'.
4
Evaluate the author's overall conclusion
The author concludes that the evidence is not clear and that al-Shafi'i's contributions are largely indigenous. He cites Mustafa 'Abd al-Raziq to show that logical order in the Risala can be seen as a natural development.
The author does not completely deny any influence but deems the claimed direct influence unproven.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالجزئيات والفروع فكان تفكيره تفكير من ليس يهتم بالمسائل الجزئية والتفاريع . بل يعني بضبط الإستدلالات التفصيلية بأصول تجمعها . وذلك هو النظر الفلسفي »(۱) وقد دعا كل هذا الى اعتبار الشافعي في العالم الإسلامي وفي الدراسات الإسلامية مقابلاً لأرسطو في العالم الهليني وفي الدراسات اليونانية . بحيث نرى ابن حنبل يعتبره فيلسوفاً «الشافعي فيلسوف في أربعة أشياء ـ في اللغة واختلاف الناس والمعاني والفقه »(۲) ويمكننا أن نقول أنه إذا كان قد وجدت قبل أرسطو مناهج منطقية كالإستقراء السقراطي أو القسمة الأفلاطونية كان لها أثر بالغ في تكوين « الأورجانون » فقد وجد الشافعي قبله مناهج أصولية أثرت في تكوين « الرسالة » . ولكن هل كانت هذه المناهج الأصولية السابقة ، هي كل ما دخلت في منهج الشافعي وأثرت عليه . أو بمعنى أضيق ـ هل لم تكن هناك عوامل أخرى غير إسلامية ـ كالمنطق الأرسططاليسي مثلاً أثرت على أصول الشافعي يعاون على فكرة تأثر الشافعي بالمنطق الأرسططاليسي ـ فيما أعتقد ما يأتي . أولاً ـ أن المنطق كان قد نقل إلى العالم الإسلامي قبل عصر الشافعي بكثير . ومن المحتمل أن يكون قد أطلع عليه وخاصة وأنه درس من ناحية علم الكلام كما يعترف هو بنفسه « لو أردت أن أضع على كل مخالف كتاباً كبيراً لفعلت . ولكن ليس الكلام من شأني »(۳) . واشتغل من ناحية أخرى بعلم النجوم . وهذان علمان نظريان لا بد لباحثهما من أن يكون عرف مناهج البحث العلمية التي كانت معروفة في زمنه . ثانياً ـ معرفة الشافعي للغة اليونانية ، على ما يذكر أبو عبد الله الحاكم في كتابه مناقب الشافعي ـ الباب الرابع والعشرون ـ من أن الشافعي كان يقول حين سأله الرشيد عن علمه بالطب : « أعرف ما قالت الروم مثل أرسططاليس ومهراريس وفرفوريوس وجالينوس وبقراط وأسدفليس بلغاتهم »(۱) . ثالثاً ـ إذا لجأنا إلى النصوص نفسها يتبين لنا أن الشافعي يشارك أرسطو في اعتبار القياس الأصولي وهو التمثيل عند أرسطو ظنياً (٢) . غير أن هذه الأسباب التي استخلصتها من تراجم الشافعي أو من مؤلفاته ليست أدلة واضحة على تأثر الرسالة بالمنطق الأرسططاليسي . فمن غير المحتم أن تكون معرفة الشافعي للمنطق الأرسططاليسي ، علة لتأثره به في وضع منهجه . فقد عرف علم الكلام وحججه ومع ذلك فلم يرد عنه أنه تكلم فيه والمتكلمون أنفسهم عرفوا منطق أرسطو ومع ذلك لم يأخذوا به (۳) . والمنهج الأصولي نفسه كان قد تكون الى حد كبير . وكان أغلب ما أضافه إليه الشافعي عناصر بيانية ونقلية خاصة بطرق الإسناد أو عدالة الناقلين أو على العموم مباحث خاصة بالكتاب والسنة . وبجانب هذا كانت هناك طرق عقلية أو مدارك للعقول يلجأ إليها نظار المسلمين وفقهاؤهم . بل وحتى هذه الطرق ، لم يلجأ اليها الشافعي في رسالته . وكان أولى أن يتأثر بها من أي منهج آخر . أما صوغ الشافعي للأصول في منهج عام متصل فقد صدر فيه عن فكر خاص . أما معرفة الشافعي لليونانية - فليست برهاناً واضحاً على دخول المنطق الأرسططاليسي في أصول الشافعي وأسلوب الرسالة وطريقة البحث فيها لا يشعران بوجود أية علاقة بينها وبين أية دراسة أجنبية عن التفكير العربي واللغة العربية حقاً إن في الرسالة نظاماً منطقياً لا شك فيه . يقول الأستاذ مصطفى عبد الرازق إن من مظاهر هذا النظام « الاتجاه المنطقي إلى وضع الحدود والتعاريف أولاً ثم الأخذ في التقسيم مع التمثيل والإستشهاد لكل قسم، وقد يعرض الشافعي لسرد التعاريف المختلفة ليقارن بينها ، وينتهي به التمحيص الى تخير ما يقتضيه منها ـ ومنها أسلوبه في الحوار الجدلي المشبع بصور
In particulars and branches, his thinking was the thinking of one who is not concerned with particular issues and ramifications, but rather aims to regulate detailed inferences by principles that unify them. That is philosophical reflection. And all this led to considering al-Shafi'i in the Islamic world and in Islamic studies as a counterpart to Aristotle in the Hellenic world and in Greek studies, such that we see Ibn Hanbal consider him a philosopher: 'Al-Shafi'i is a philosopher in four things: language, disagreement among people, meanings, and jurisprudence.' And we can say that just as there existed before Aristotle logical methods such as Socratic induction or Platonic division, which had a great influence on the formation of the 'Organon,' so too before al-Shafi'i there existed methodological principles (usul) that influenced the formation of the 'Risala.' But were these earlier methodological principles all that entered into al-Shafi'i's method and influenced him? Or, more narrowly, were there not other non-Islamic factors—such as Aristotelian logic, for example—that influenced al-Shafi'i's principles? Supporting the idea of al-Shafi'i being influenced by Aristotelian logic are, in my opinion, the following. First: logic had been transmitted to the Islamic world long before al-Shafi'i's era, and it is probable that he was acquainted with it, especially since he studied kalam, as he himself admits: 'If I wanted, I would write a large book against every opponent, but kalam is not my concern.' And he also occupied himself with astronomy. These are two theoretical sciences whose researcher must have known the scientific research methods current in his time. Second: al-Shafi'i's knowledge of Greek, as related by Abu 'Abd Allah al-Hakim in his book 'Manaqib al-Shafi'i' (Chapter 24), that al-Shafi'i said when Harun al-Rashid asked him about his knowledge of medicine: 'I know what the Romans said, such as Aristotle, Mahararis, Porphyry, Galen, Hippocrates, and Asclepius, in their languages.' Third: if we turn to the texts themselves, it becomes clear to us that al-Shafi'i shares with Aristotle the view that analogical reasoning (al-qiyas al-usuli) is probable, which is analogy (tamthil) according to Aristotle. However, these reasons that I have extracted from al-Shafi'i's biographies or his works are not clear evidence for the influence of Aristotelian logic on the Risala. For it is not necessary that al-Shafi'i's knowledge of Aristotelian logic was the cause of his being influenced by it in developing his method. He knew kalam and its arguments, yet he is not reported to have spoken about it, and the mutakallimun themselves knew Aristotle's logic but did not adopt it. And the methodological principles (al-manhaj al-usuli) themselves had been largely formed. Most of what al-Shafi'i added to it were elements of exposition and transmission related to methods of chain of transmission or probity of transmitters, or in general topics specific to the Book and the Sunna. Besides this, there were rational methods or intellectual perceptions that Muslim theoreticians and jurists resorted to. But even these methods, al-Shafi'i did not resort to in his Risala; he would have been more likely to be influenced by them than by any other method. As for al-Shafi'i's formulation of the principles into a connected general method, it originated from his own thought. As for al-Shafi'i's knowledge of Greek, it is not clear proof of the entry of Aristotelian logic into his principles; the style of the Risala and its method of research do not indicate any relationship between it and any foreign study of Arab thought and Arabic language. Indeed, there is logical order in the Risala without doubt. Professor Mustafa 'Abd al-Raziq says that among the manifestations of this order are 'the logical tendency to present definitions and delimitations first, then proceed to division with examples and citations for each division, and al-Shafi'i sometimes lists different definitions to compare them, ending after scrutiny with choosing what is required from them—and among them is his style in dialectical dialogue replete with...'
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage is a critical historical analysis by the author, who is examining whether al-Shafi'i's methodological work, the Risala, was influenced by Aristotelian logic. The author first contextualizes al-Shafi'i's status in the Islamic world as a counterpart to Aristotle, even quoting Ibn Hanbal calling him a philosopher. He then draws an analogy: just as earlier Greek methods (Socratic induction, Platonic division) influenced Aristotle's Organon, so earlier Islamic usul methods influenced al-Shafi'i's Risala. The author then asks whether non-Islamic factors like Aristotelian logic also influenced al-Shafi'i, and presents three reasons that might support that thesis: (1) logic was already translated before al-Shafi'i, and he studied kalam and astronomy, so he likely knew it; (2) al-Shafi'i knew Greek, as evidenced by his statement listing Greek physicians; (3) in his texts, al-Shafi'i shares with Aristotle the view that qiyas usuli (legal analogy) is probable, which corresponds to Aristotle's notion of analogy (tamthil). However, the author then refutes these reasons. He argues that knowing logic does not necessitate adopting it; al-Shafi'i knew kalam but did not engage in it, and mutakallimun knew logic but did not adopt it. Moreover, the usul methodology was already largely formed before al-Shafi'i; his main contributions were in transmission and textual matters, not rational methods. Even the rational methods available (like those of the mutakallimun) were not used by al-Shafi'i. Thus, the author concludes that knowledge of Greek is not proof of influence, and the style of the Risala does not show foreign influence. He ends by citing Mustafa 'Abd al-Raziq, who notes that the logical order in the Risala (definitions first, then divisions with examples) is not necessarily Greek but could be indigenous. The passage is thus a careful weighing of evidence against the influence thesis.

Key Terms
الشافعي
المنهج الأصولي
القياس الأصولي
أرسطو
المنطق الأرسططاليسي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a reason the author gives in favor of Aristotelian influence on al-Shafi'i?
A) Al-Shafi'i studied kalam and astronomy.
B) Al-Shafi'i knew Greek.
C) Al-Shafi'i used Aristotelian syllogisms in his Risala.
D) Al-Shafi'i considered qiyas usuli as probable, like Aristotle's tamthil.

Key Takeaway
The evidence for direct influence of Aristotelian logic on al-Shafi'i is insufficient; his method can be understood as a development of indigenous Islamic usul and his own innovative synthesis.

The Independent Development of the Usuli Method
Author summarizes usuli method's development and independence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to trace the historical development of the usuli method and identify its independence from Aristotelian logic before the fifth century.

Core Idea
The usuli method was indigenous, refined by al-Shafi'i, and free from Greek logic until the 5th century.

This passage tracks the evolution of the usuli (principles) method from the earliest Islamic era through al-Shafi'i and beyond. The author emphasizes that this method, used by both jurists and theologians, was not influenced by Aristotelian logic until the fifth century. We'll explore its key features and historical trajectory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the time frame of independence
Note that the method was formed from the first era and continued without Greek influence until the fifth century. The passage states 'في كل هذا لم يتأثر على الإطلاق بمناهج البحث اليونانية'.
2
Recognize the role of al-Shafi'i
Al-Shafi'i established it as a coherent science: 'فأقامه علماً متفق الأجزاء متناسق الأطراف'.
3
Understand the split after al-Shafi'i
The method split into fiqhi usul and kalami usul, both remaining independent of Aristotelian logic.
4
Note the special linguistic induction
Usulis used a special induction (استقراء خاص) to reach subtleties not attained by linguists or grammarians.
This method was part of the independent methodology.
5
Identify the 5th century turning point
In the fifth century, Muslims mixed Aristotelian logic with usul, which the author views as ending creative thinking in logic.
The passage says: 'حتى أتى القرن الخامس فمزج المسلمون المنطق الأرسططاليسي بالأصول'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتي حللت عناصرها في الباب السابق . ولكن سنعرض لهذا المنهج الأصولي الكلامي - لأن المتكلمين يتشاركون فيه ، كما سنرى بعد ـ كما عرف قبل القرن الخامس . ولن أخوض في الأبحاث اللغوية لخروجها عن نطاق هذا البحث ، مع ملاحظة أن المباحث الأصولية اللغوية ليست من نوع علوم اللغة أو النحو العادية . فقد دقق الأصوليون نظرهم في فهم أشياء من كلام العرب لم يتوصل إليها اللغويون أو النحاة . إن كلام العرب متسع وطرق البحث فيه متشعبة . فكتب اللغة تضبط الألفاظ والمعاني الظاهرة دون المعاني الدقيقة التي يتوصل إليها الأصولي باستقراء يزيد على استقراء اللغوي . فهناك إذن دقائق لا يتعرض لها اللغوي ولا تقتضيها صناعة النحو . ولكن يتوصل إليها الأصوليون باستقراء خاص وأدلة خاصة(١) . هذا هو تاريخ المنهج الأصولي - استطعنا أن نتبين من خلاله موقف علماء أصول الفقه من المنطق الأرسططاليسي ، وتكون هذا المنهج منذ العصر الأول واستمر الأصوليون يكملون فيه ويضيفون إلى عناصره جدة في التفكير ، حتى وصل إلى أيدي الشافعي . فأقامه علماً متفق الأجزاء متناسق الأطراف . وفي كل هذا لم يتأثر على الإطلاق بمناهج البحث اليونانية - ثم انقسم إلى قسمين بعد الشافعي : علم الأصول الفقهي وعلم الأصول الكلامي . وفي كلا القسمين لم يتأثر أيضاً بمنطق أرسطو - حتى أتى القرن الخامس فمزج المسلمون المنطق الأرسططاليسي بالأصول ، وبهذا انتهى أو كاد ينتهي تفكير المسلمين المبدع في المنطق . هذا هو موقف علماء أصول الفقه - فقهاء كانوا أو متكلمين . فما هو موقف علماء أصول الدين المتكلمين من المنطق الأرسططاليسي ؟ هذا ما سنبحثه في الفصل القادم . (١) سميت بالكلامية لأن المتأخرين جعلوا المنطق جزءاً من علم الكلام ( مسلم الثبوت جـا ص . ( ١٠ )
And those whose elements were analyzed in the previous chapter. But we will present this usuli-kalami method - because the mutakallimun share in it, as we will see later - as it was known before the fifth century. And I will not delve into linguistic researches because they are outside the scope of this research, noting that the usuli linguistic investigations are not of the type of ordinary language sciences or grammar. The usulis scrutinized their view in understanding things from the speech of the Arabs that the linguists or grammarians did not reach. The speech of the Arabs is vast and the ways of research in it are branched. The language books fix the apparent words and meanings without the precise meanings that the usuli reaches through an induction that exceeds the induction of the linguist. There are therefore subtleties that the linguist does not address and the craft of grammar does not require. But the usulis reach them through special induction and special evidences. This is the history of the usuli method - we were able to discern through it the stance of the scholars of usul al-fiqh towards Aristotelian logic, and this method was formed from the first era and the usulis continued to complete it and add to its elements novelty in thinking, until it reached the hands of al-Shafi'i. So he established it as a science of consistent parts and harmonious aspects. And in all this it was not at all influenced by Greek research methods - then it divided into two sections after al-Shafi'i: the science of fiqhi usul and the science of kalami usul. And in both sections it was also not influenced by Aristotle's logic - until the fifth century came when Muslims mixed Aristotelian logic with usul, and with this the creative thinking of Muslims in logic ended or almost ended. This is the stance of the scholars of usul al-fiqh - whether they were fuqaha' or mutakallimun. So what is the stance of the scholars of usul al-din, the mutakallimun, towards Aristotelian logic? This is what we will research in the next chapter. (1) It was called kalamiya because the later scholars made logic part of the science of kalam (Muslim al-Thubut, vol. 1, p. 10).
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage is a historical narrative summarizing the development of the usuli method and its relationship with Aristotelian logic. The author begins by stating that he will present the usuli-kalami method, which was known before the fifth century. He clarifies that he will not delve into linguistic research because it is outside the scope, but notes that usuli linguistic investigations are distinct from ordinary language or grammar studies. The usuli scholars reached subtleties in understanding Arabic speech that linguists and grammarians did not, through a special inductive process. The author then traces the history: the method was formed from the first Islamic era, continually refined until al-Shafi'i systematized it as a coherent science. Importantly, it was not influenced by Greek research methods. After al-Shafi'i, it split into fiqhi usul and kalami usul, both remaining uninfluenced by Aristotle until the fifth century when Muslims mixed Aristotelian logic with usul, which the author views as ending creative Muslim thinking in logic. The passage ends with a teaser for the next chapter on the stance of mutakallimun. A footnote explains the term 'kalamiya' because later scholars made logic part of kalam.

Key Terms
المنهج الأصولي
المنطق الأرسططاليسي
المتكلمين
أصول الفقه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when did Muslim scholars begin mixing Aristotelian logic with the usuli method?
A) In the first century AH
B) In the time of al-Shafi'i
C) In the fifth century AH
D) Never, they remained independent

Key Takeaway
The usuli method developed independently of Aristotelian logic until the fifth century AH, when mixing occurred.

The Unique Linguistic Induction of the Usulis
Author contrasts usuli linguistic method with ordinary grammar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the usuli method of linguistic induction and how it differs from ordinary linguistic analysis.

Core Idea
Usulis used a special induction to extract legal meanings from Arabic that linguists and grammarians missed.

In this passage, the author highlights that the usuli method involves a deeper analysis of Arabic than standard linguistics. While language books record apparent meanings, usulis derive precise legal meanings through a unique inductive process. This lesson explores that methodological distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim about usuli linguistic work
The passage states that usulis scrutinized and understood things from Arab speech that linguists and grammarians did not reach.
2
Understand the basis of the claim
The speech of Arabs is vast and research paths are many. Language books fix apparent words and meanings, but not precise meanings.
The passage: 'إن كلام العرب متسع وطرق البحث فيه متشعبة'.
3
Contrast ordinary grammar with usuli induction
The craft of grammar does not require the subtleties usulis reach. Usulis use an induction that exceeds that of the linguist.
4
Identify the tools of usuli induction
Usulis employ special induction (استقراء خاص) and special evidences (أدلة خاصة).
This is a key methodological point.
5
Relate this to independence from Greek logic
This linguistic method was part of the indigenous usuli approach, developed independently of Greek logic.
The passage emphasizes no Greek influence.

After al-Shafi'i, it split into fiqhi usul and kalami usul, both remaining uninfluenced by Aristotle until the fifth century when Muslims mixed Aristotelian logic with usul, which the author views as ending creative Muslim thinking in logic. The passage ends with a teaser for the next chapter on the stance of mutakallimun. A footnote explains the term 'kalamiya' because later scholars made logic part of kalam.

Al-Ghazali's Critique of Logicians' Premises and Hint at a Mutakallimun Methodology
Al-Ghazali's critique of logicians' premises and hint at a mutakallimun method.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify al-Ghazali's critique of weak premises and explain how he points to a distinct methodology for mutakallimun.

Core Idea
Al-Ghazali warns that relying on widely accepted premises without investigation leads to contradiction.

In this passage, the author of our text presents al-Ghazali's sharp critique of the logicians' use of weak premises and the mutakallimun's neglect of self-evident truths. Al-Ghazali also hints at a different methodology—one that avoids the pitfalls of blind acceptance. Let's dissect his criticism and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main criticisms al-Ghazali makes
One criticism targets the logicians' reliance on weak premises; the other criticizes the mutakallimun for neglecting self-evident truths and letting doubt creep in due to association with fanatics.
2
Understand the 'theory of the intermediary'
Al-Ghazali refers to a theory that criticizes the principle of non-contradiction, suggesting that some propositions are neither affirmed nor denied—a third possibility.
This theory was circulating among Muslims and challenged the excluded middle.
3
Analyze the problem with widely accepted premises (mashhurat)
Al-Ghazali notes that most syllogisms of the dialecticians rely on premises accepted merely due to fame, not verification. This leads to contradictory results and confusion.
Such premises are not certain and can produce opposing conclusions.
4
Connect al-Ghazali's critique to his proposed methodology
Al-Ghazali implicitly advocates for a special methodology for mutakallimun that depends on certain premises and avoids the blind acceptance of famous opinions.
This methodology would require rigorous verification of premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتوافرها(١) ( وهي ما يسميها صاحب المواقف بالمقدمات الضعيفة )(٢) وما كان يراه في عدم إهتمام المتكلمين بالبديهيات ، وخروجهم في أبحاثهم عنها « فقد يظن بالأوليات أنها ليست أولية فيشكك فيها ولا يتشكك في الأوليات إلا بزوال الذهن عن الفطرة السليمة لمخالطة بعض المتكلمين المتعصبين للمذاهب الفاسدة بمجاححدة الجليات »(٣) ولعل الغزالي يشير إلى تلك النظرية التي كانت تتردد في أوساط المسلمين « نظرية الواسطة » والتي تنقد مبدأ عدم ارتفاع النقيضين أي مبدأ الثالث المرفوع . وفي فقرة أخرى إن « أكثر أقيسة الجدليين من المتكلمين والفقهاء في مجادلاتهم وتصانيفهم مؤلفة من مقدمات مشهورة فيما بينهم سلموها لمجرد الشهرة وذهلوا عن سببها . ولذا نرى أقيستهم تنتج نتائج متناقضة فيتحيرون فيها وتتخبط عقولهم في تنقيحها »(٤) ويلاحظ أن كلمة الجدليين تطلق على طائفة من المتكلمين والأصوليين لم يأخذوا بالمنطق الأرسططاليسي . بل كانت لهم قواعد وأنظمة خاصة في المناظرة والمجادلة . يمكننا أن نستخلص من كلام الغزالي أنه يشير إلى منهاج خاص للمتكلمين .
And their abundance (1) (which the author of al-Mawaqif calls weak premises) (2) and what he saw regarding the lack of interest of the mutakallimun in self-evident truths, and their departure in their investigations from them: 'It may be thought that self-evident propositions are not self-evident, so doubt is cast on them, and doubt is not cast on self-evident propositions except by the removal of the mind from sound disposition due to mixing with some bigoted mutakallimun of corrupt doctrines by denying obvious truths' (3). Perhaps al-Ghazali is referring to that theory which was circulating among Muslims, the 'theory of the intermediary', which criticizes the principle of non-contradiction, i.e., the principle of the excluded middle. And in another paragraph: 'Most of the syllogisms of the dialecticians among the mutakallimun and jurists in their debates and writings are composed of premises widely accepted among them, which they conceded merely due to fame and were heedless of their cause. Hence we see their syllogisms producing contradictory results, so they become confused in them and their minds become perplexed in refining them' (4). It is noted that the word 'dialecticians' (jadalīyīn) is applied to a group of mutakallimun and usuliyyun who did not adopt Aristotelian logic. Rather, they had their own specific rules and systems in disputation and debate. We can deduce from al-Ghazali's words that he indicates a special methodology for the mutakallimun.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage presents al-Ghazali's critique of the logicians' reliance on certain premises, particularly what he calls 'weak premises' as termed by the author of al-Mawaqif. He notes that the mutakallimun often neglected self-evident truths and strayed from them in their research, casting doubt on what should be evident. Al-Ghazali suggests that such doubt arises from a corrupted disposition due to association with fanatical sectarians. The author then connects this to the 'theory of the intermediary' (nazariyyat al-wasita) that criticizes the principle of non-contradiction (the excluded middle). He further quotes al-Ghazali's observation that most syllogisms used by dialecticians (jadalīyīn) among mutakallimun and jurists are composed of widely accepted premises (mashhurat) that they accepted blindly, leading to contradictory conclusions. The author clarifies that 'dialecticians' here refers to those who did not follow Aristotelian logic but had their own systems for debate. Thus, al-Ghazali implicitly points to a distinct methodology for the mutakallimun.

الجدليين
نظرية الواسطة

Key Takeaway
Al-Ghazali's critique reveals the need for methodological rigor in Islamic theology, cautioning against blind acceptance of premises and advocating for a distinct approach based on certainty.

Imam al-Haramayn on the Independence of Islamic Rational Proofs
Quotes Imam al-Haramayn asserting independent arrangement of rational proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Imam al-Haramayn's claim about the arrangement of rational proofs by Muslim imams and explain its significance for an independent Islamic methodology.

Core Idea
Muslim imams had their own rational proof system before adopting Aristotelian logic.

This lesson examines a key statement by Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni, a major Ash'ari theologian. He asserts that Muslim imams arranged rational proofs in a specific order — an order that later scholars transmit and then critique. This claim is used to argue that Muslims had an independent scientific methodology before fully engaging with Greek logic.

Guided Path
1
Locate Imam al-Haramayn's statement
Find the direct quote in Arabic. What exactly does he say about the imams' arrangement of rational proofs?
The quote is: 'رتب أئمتنا أدلة العقول ترتيباً ننقله ثم نبين فساده'
2
Identify the two actions
He mentions two actions: transmitting the order (ننقله) and showing its corruption (نبين فساده). What does this imply about the imams' original arrangement?
The fact that they transmitted it implies its existence and significance; showing its corruption suggests critical engagement, not mere rejection.
3
Connect to the author's argument
The author of Manahij al-Bahth says this proves Muslims had an independent methodology. How does the quote support that?
The author reasons that because Imam al-Haramayn references a distinct order by the imams, it shows a pre-existing method, even if later critiqued.
4
Consider the context of Aristotelian adoption
Note that Imam al-Haramayn himself adopted some Aristotelian logic. How does this strengthen or complicate the claim of independence?
It strengthens by showing that even a scholar influenced by Aristotle acknowledged an earlier indigenous method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ب ـ ما يقوله إمام الحرمين « رتب أئمتنا أدلة العقول ترتيباً ننقله ثم نبين فساده »(٥) وهذا يثبت تماماً أن للمسلمين منهاجاً في البحث العلمي مستقلاً ، لأن إمام الحرمين ـ أخذ إلى حد ما ـ كما بينا في الفصل السابق بالمنطق الأرسططاليسي .
B. What Imam al-Haramayn says: 'Our imams arranged the rational proofs in an order that we transmit and then show its corruption' (5). This entirely proves that the Muslims have an independent methodology in scientific research, because Imam al-Haramayn – to some extent – adopted Aristotelian logic, as we clarified in the previous chapter.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage presents the second piece of evidence for an independent Islamic methodology: the statement of Imam al-Haramayn (Juwayni) that the Muslim imams (theologians) organized rational proofs in a specific order. He says that we (later scholars) transmit this order but then demonstrate its corruption. The author of Manahij Bahth interprets this as proof that Muslims had an independent methodology (manhaj) in research, since Imam al-Haramayn, although he adopted some Aristotelian logic, acknowledges the existence of a distinct arrangement by the imams. This suggests that before the full adoption of Greek logic, Muslim scholars had their own system.

Ibn Taymiyya on a Special Law for Mutakallimun
Ibn Taymiyya's affirmation of a distinct method for mutakallimun

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key figures and methods that constitute the special law (qanun khas) of the mutakallimun according to Ibn Taymiyya.

Core Idea
The mutakallimun possessed their own reasoning method, distinct from Aristotle.

This lesson examines a key passage where Ibn Taymiyya explicitly affirms the existence of a special law (qanun khas) that characterized the mutakallimun. He contrasts it with al-Ghazali's adoption of Aristotelian logic and points to Abu Bakr ibn al-Arabi's alternative method based on earlier kalam authorities. Understanding this helps recognize the independent methodological tradition in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central claim
Ibn Taymiyya states that there is a special law (qanun) that distinguished the mutakallimun. What is this 'law' referring to? It is a method of reasoning, distinct from Aristotelian logic.
The term 'qanun khas' is key.
2
Distinguish the two laws
Al-Ghazali's law is explicitly identified as logic (al-mantiq). Abu Bakr ibn al-Arabi devised a different law based on the method of Abu al-Ma'ali (al-Juwayni) and al-Baqillani. This shows a living tradition of reasoning independent of Aristotle.
Ibn al-Arabi's law is built on prior kalam authorities.
3
Recognize the broader shift
Ibn Taymiyya adds that Muslim theorists (nuzzar) turned away from the logicians' path and adopted 'specific proofs' (adilla khassa). This reinforces the existence of an indigenous Islamic methodology.
These specific proofs are not random; they are systematic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هـ - يقرر ابن تيمية ( ۷۲۹ هـ ) صراحة وجود قانون خاص امتاز به المتكلمون فيقول : « وكان للغزالي قانون هو المنطق - أما أبو بكر بن العربي فقد وضع قانوناً آخر مبنياً على طريقة أبي المعالي ومن قبله كالقاضي أبي بكر الباقلاني »(۳) ويذكر في فقرة أخرى أن نظار المسلمين عدلوا عن طريق المناطقة فقالوا « الطريق هو المرشد إلى المطلوب وهو الموصل إلى المقصود وهو ما يكون العلم ، (٤) وهذا الطريق غير طريق المناطقة بل هي أدلة خاصة ويردد ابن تيمية هذا في مواضع كثيرة من كتبه - كما أن ابن السبكي يردده أيضاً (٥) .
E. Ibn Taymiyya (729 AH) explicitly affirms the existence of a special law (qanun) by which the mutakallimun were distinguished, saying: 'Al-Ghazali had a law which was logic; but Abu Bakr ibn al-Arabi devised another law based on the method of Abu al-Ma'ali and before him, such as Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani' (3). He mentions in another paragraph that the Muslim theorists (nuzzar) turned away from the path of the logicians and said: 'The path (tariq) is what guides to the desired object and leads to the goal, and it is what becomes knowledge' (4). This path is not the path of the logicians, but rather specific proofs. Ibn Taymiyya repeats this in many places of his books, and Ibn al-Subki also repeats it (5).
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth evidence: Ibn Taymiyya's explicit statement that there is a special law (qanun) unique to the mutakallimun. He contrasts al-Ghazali's law (which is logic) with the law of Abu Bakr ibn al-Arabi, which was built on the method of Abu al-Ma'ali al-Juwayni and earlier figures like al-Baqillani. This indicates a distinct tradition of reasoning. Furthermore, Ibn Taymiyya notes that Muslim theorists turned away from the logicians' path and defined 'path' as that which guides to the desired knowledge, which is different from the logicians' method and consists of specific proofs (adilla khassa). Ibn Taymiyya and Ibn al-Subki are both cited as proponents of this view, reinforcing the existence of an independent Islamic methodology.

Key Terms
قانون خاص
أبو بكر بن العربي

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya explicitly confirms that the mutakallimun had their own law of reasoning, distinct from Aristotelian logic, as exemplified by Abu Bakr ibn al-Arabi's method.

Why Focus on ‘Illah? The Author’s Methodological Move
Author states his purpose to compare qiyas to Aristotelian analogy and limits discussion to 'illa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the author limits his study to ‘illah and how he distinguishes usul qiyas from Aristotelian analogy.

Core Idea
The author narrows the analysis to ‘illah because asl, far‘, and hukm fall under other disciplines.

The author sets out his research agenda: to prove that usul qiyas is not Aristotelian analogy but a type of scientific induction. This leads him to forego detailed discussion of the first two pillars (asl and far‘) because their conditions are fiqhi, and of hukm because it is kalami. Instead, he will drill down into ‘illah. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's overarching goal
The author states his aim is to show that usul qiyas is contradictory to Aristotelian analogy and reduce it to a scientific induction.
This frames the whole discussion.
2
Determine which elements he excludes and why
He explicitly says he will not examine the conditions of asl and far‘ (they are fiqhi) nor the hukm (it is kalami).
These exclusions are based on disciplinary boundaries.
3
Identify what remains and how it will be treated
Only ‘illah remains, and he will examine it in detail (tafsili).
This is the core of the method.
4
Assess the logical force of this narrowing
By limiting to ‘illah, the author focuses on the causal element that connects asl and far‘, which he believes is what makes qiyas a form of induction.
This is a deliberate choice to avoid digression.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما كانت غايتنا في هذا البحث هو إثبات مخالفة القياس الأصولي للتمثيل الأرسططاليسي في جوهره ورد هذا القياس إلى نوع من أنواع الإستقراء العلمي الدقيق القائم على التجربة، فاننا لن نبحث فيما وضع الأصوليون للركنين الأولين - الأصل والفرع - من شروط لأن هذه الشروط تعود في معظمها إلى مسائل فقهية - كما أن البحث في الحكم خارج عن نطاق بحثنا هذا . إذ هو بحث كلامي بحت . بقيت العلة وسنبحثها بحثاً تفصيلياً .
And since our aim in this research is to prove the contradiction of usul qiyas to Aristotelian analogy in its essence, and to reduce this qiyas to a type of precise scientific induction based on experience, we will not examine what the usuliyyun established as conditions for the first two pillars—asl and far'—because these conditions mostly return to fiqhi issues; also, the research on hukm is outside the scope of this research, as it is purely kalami. The 'illa remains, and we will examine it in detail.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage justifies the author's decision to focus solely on 'illa. The overall goal is to show that usul qiyas is not Aristotelian analogy but a form of scientific induction. Consequently, discussions of the conditions of asl and far' (which are fiqhi) and the nature of hukm (which is kalami) are excluded. The 'illa is the critical element that will be analyzed in detail.

Key Terms
التمثيل الأرسططاليسي (al-tamthil al-arisṭāṭālīsī): Aristotelian analogy; the author contrasts it with usul qiyas, which he argues is not analogy but induction. [Note: The term is a modern designation; classical texts do not typically refer to 'Aristotelian analogy' explicitly.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The author systematically excludes asl/far‘ conditions (fiqhi) and hukm (kalami) to focus solely on ‘illah, which he believes distinguishes usul qiyas from analogy.

Classification of Evidence in Usul al-Fiqh
Classification of evidence into transmitted and rational

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify evidence into transmitted and rational, and identify the three rational methods the author will discuss.

Core Idea
Transmitted proofs are accepted by authority; rational proofs are discovered by systematic reasoning.

In usul al-fiqh, discovering the 'illa (effective cause) behind a ruling requires evidence. This lesson introduces the two major categories of such evidence: transmitted (naqli) and rational ('aqli). The author will focus on three rational methods that were pioneered by Muslim scholars and later adopted by Europeans. Understanding this classification helps you see how jurists systematically identify causes from revelation and reason.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main categories
The author divides all paths (masalik) or proofs into two sections: transmitted proofs (adilla naqliyya) and rational proofs (adilla 'aqliyya). Note that 'naqli' means based on revelation and transmission, while 'aqli' means based on reason.
The terms 'maslak' and 'dalil' are used interchangeably here.
2
List the transmitted proofs
Under transmitted proofs, the author mentions three: the Quranic text (al-nass), consensus (al-ijma'), and the action of the Prophet (fi'l al-rasul). These are accepted as authoritative sources in Islamic law.
The author will not discuss these in depth, as they fall outside the scope of this research.
3
List the rational proofs
The rational proofs include: sabr wa taqsim, munasaba (suitability), shabah (similarity), tard wa dawran (coextensiveness and coexistence), and tanqih al-manat (refinement of the occasioning factor). The author also mentions 'other weak paths'.
These methods are used to discover the 'illa rationally.
4
Identify the focused methods
The author announces that the research will focus on three specific rational methods: sabr wa taqsim, tard wa dawran, and tanqih al-manat. These are considered the most important manifestations of logical thinking among usulis.
The author also notes that Muslims preceded Europeans in developing these methods.
5
Recognise the historical significance
The passage claims that Muslims discovered these rational methods before Europeans. This underscores the originality and logical sophistication of Islamic legal theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمسالك أو الأدلة تنقسم إلى قسمين: أدلة نقلية وهي النص والإجماع وفعل الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم، وأدلة عقلية وهي السبر والتقسيم والمناسبة والشبه والطرد والدوران وتنقيح المناط ثم طرق أخرى ضعيفة. ولن نبحث في القسم الأول من المسالك فهي تخرج عن بحثنا هنا، وسنقصر بحثنا على بعض الأدلة أو المسالك من القسم الثاني. وهي الأدلة أو الطرق التي سبق المسلمون في التوصل اليها الأوروبيين. وهي في الوقت عينه تعتبر مظهراً من أهم مظاهر الحياة المنطقية لدى الأصوليين. أما المسالك التي سنبحثها فهي مسلك السبر والتقسيم ومسلك الطرد والدوران وتنقيح المناط.
And the paths or proofs are divided into two sections: transmitted proofs, which are the text, consensus, and the action of the Messenger, peace be upon him; and rational proofs, which are sabr and taqsim, munasaba, shabah, tard and dawran, tanqih al-manat, then other weak paths. We will not research the first section of the paths, for they fall outside our research here, and we will restrict our research to some of the proofs or paths from the second section. They are the proofs or paths that the Muslims preceded the Europeans in discovering. And at the same time they are considered one of the most important manifestations of logical life among the usulis. As for the paths we will research, they are the path of sabr and taqsim, the path of tard and dawran, and tanqih al-manat.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage classifies the paths (masalik) or proofs (adilla) used to discover the 'illa (cause) in usul al-fiqh. The author divides them into two major categories: transmitted proofs (adilla naqliyya) which include the Quranic text, consensus, and the actions of the Prophet, and rational proofs (adilla 'aqliyya) which include several specific methods: sabr wa al-taqsim (sweeping and division), munasaba (suitability), shabah (similarity), tard wa dawran (coextensiveness and coexistence), tanqih al-manat (refinement of the occasioning factor), and other weaker methods. The author announces that they will not discuss the transmitted proofs, as they are outside the scope of this research, and will focus instead on the rational proofs, specifically sabr wa al-taqsim, tard wa dawran, and tanqih al-manat. The author also notes that Muslims preceded Europeans in discovering these methods, and that these methods represent an important manifestation of logical thinking among usul scholars. This sets the stage for the detailed discussion of each method.

Key Terms
مسلك
دليل

طرد ودوران
