Qiyas al-Gha'ib 'ala al-Shahid: The Theologian's Analogy
Author defines qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid and relates it to usuli qiyas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three components of qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid and map them to the corresponding usuli qiyas terms.

Core Idea
Qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid is structurally identical to usuli qiyas, with different terminology.

This passage introduces a foundational method of Islamic reasoning used by theologians (mutakallimun) to infer truths about the unseen (gha'ib) from the witnessed (shahid). The author emphasizes that this method is essentially the same as usuli qiyas (analogical reasoning in jurisprudence), but the terms are renamed. Understanding this mapping is crucial for reading works of kalam and seeing the unity of Islamic reasoning across disciplines.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three core components
The passage lists three elements: what is measured upon (al-maqis 'alayhi), what is measured (al-maqis), and the commonality (al-jami'). Locate these terms in the Arabic text.
These are the building blocks of any analogy.
2
Map to usuli qiyas terminology
The author explicitly equates al-maqis 'alayhi with al-asl (original case), al-maqis with al-far' (branch case), and al-jami' with al-'illa (effective cause). Verify this correspondence in the translation.
This mapping shows the conceptual unity between kalam and usul al-fiqh.
3
Note the theologians' addition
The passage ends by noting that theologians add further conditions to the conjunction (al-jam'). This means they impose extra requirements for validating the link between witnessed and unseen.
These conditions are explored in later lessons.
4
Recognize the method's purpose
Qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid is used to argue from observable creation (shahid) to the Creator and His attributes (gha'ib). This is the core of Ash'ari proofs for God's existence and attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفَصْلُ الخَامِس
الطرق الإسلامية الأخرى
الطريق الأول : قياس الغائب على الشاهد
عرضنا في الفصل السابق لأهم طريق من طرق الاستدلالات الإسلامية وهو القياس الأصولي . وسنبحث في هذا الفصل بعض الطرق الأخرى التي استخدمها المسلمون في أبحاثهم وأخذوا بها قبل نهاية القرن الخامس الهجري ولم أستطع مع الاسف الشديد التوصل إلى الكتب التي بحثت هذه الطرق ، وهي كتب الباقلاني وإمام الحرمين لظروف مختلفة ولذلك سنبحثها خلال فقرات باقية في كتب المتأخرين . وقد ذكرنا هذا من قبل . أما أصحاب تلك الطرق فهم الاشاعرة ، ومن المحتمل أن يكون المعتزلة استخدموها أيضاً وهذا الاحتمال يصل إليه ابن رشد « وأما المعتزلة فلم يصل إلينا من كتبهم شيء نقف منه على طرقهم التي سلكوها في هذا المعنى ويشبه أن يكون طرقهم من جنس طرق الأشاعرة »(۱) وأول هذه الطرق هو قياس الغائب على الشاهد(٢) . وهذا القياس هو القياس الأصولي - فالمقيس عليه عند المتكلمين هو الأصل عند الأصوليين - والمقيس هو الفرع - والجامع بين الأصل والفرع أو بين الشاهد والغائب هو العلة عند الأصوليين . ويضيف المتكلمون إلى الجمع
Chapter Five: Other Islamic Methods. The First Method: Qiyas al-Gha'ib 'ala al-Shahid (Reasoning from the Unseen to the Witnessed). We presented in the previous chapter the most important method of Islamic inferences, which is usuli qiyas. And we will discuss in this chapter some other methods that Muslims used in their researches and adopted before the end of the fifth century AH. I was unable, unfortunately, to access the books that discussed these methods, which are the books of al-Baqillani and Imam al-Haramayn, due to various circumstances. Therefore we will discuss them through remaining paragraphs in later books. We have mentioned this before. As for the proponents of these methods, they are the Ash'aris, and it is possible that the Mu'tazila also used them. This possibility is reached by Ibn Rushd: 'As for the Mu'tazila, none of their books have reached us by which we could know their methods that they followed in this meaning, and it seems that their methods are of the same kind as the methods of the Ash'aris.' And the first of these methods is qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid. And this qiyas is the usuli qiyas: for what is measured upon (al-maqis 'alayhi) according to the theologians is the original (al-asl) according to the usulis, and what is measured (al-maqis) is the branch (al-far'), and the commonality between the original and the branch or between the witnessed and the unseen is the cause (al-'illa) according to the usulis. And the theologians add to the conjunction...
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage introduces the first of the 'other Islamic methods' of reasoning after having covered usuli qiyas in the previous chapter. The author begins by noting the difficulty of accessing primary sources from al-Baqillani and Imam al-Haramayn, hence the reliance on later works. He then attributes these methods primarily to the Ash'ari school, with a possible Mu'tazili parallel, citing Ibn Rushd's conjecture. The core definition: qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid is structurally identical to usuli qiyas. The terms are mapped: what the theologians call 'maqis 'alayhi' (that which is measured upon) corresponds to the usuli 'asl' (original); 'maqis' (the measured) corresponds to 'far'' (branch); and the common factor linking original and branch (or witnessed and unseen) is the 'illa (cause/effective reason). The passage ends with a note that theologians add further conditions to the conjunction (al-jam'), which is the process of identifying the common factor. This method is foundational in kalam for arguing from the observable world to the existence and attributes of God.

الفرع
العلة

Key Takeaway
Qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid is structurally identical to usuli qiyas, with different terminology; it is the central method of Ash'ari kalam for reasoning from the witnessed to the unseen.

The Sixth Method: Invalidity of Proof Indicates Invalidity of the Proved
Author defines the sixth method and its two forms with critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the sixth method in Islamic dialectic and distinguish its two forms.

Core Idea
A claim's falsehood cannot be inferred simply from lack of evidence.

This lesson introduces the sixth method of reasoning, which states that if a claim lacks valid evidence, it must be denied. We explore its two formulations, including a refinement by al-Baqillani, and examine the controversy surrounding the necessity of exhaustive enumeration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Principle
State the principle in your own words: the invalidity of evidence for a claim implies the claim's invalidity. Note the two formulations given.
The first formulation is 'what has no evidence must be denied'; the second is al-Baqillani's version.
2
Recognize the Two Stages
The method has two stages: first, seek out and refute all evidence for a claim; second, perform an exhaustive enumeration of all possible proofs and refute each.
The second stage adds explicit enumeration (al-hasr).
3
Compare the Two Forms
The author argues that the two forms are essentially the same, but the second form explicitly includes enumeration. Some theologians considered enumeration an unnecessary addition.
This reflects a methodological debate about the need for exhaustive enumeration.
4
Consider the Consequences
Applying the method indiscriminately can lead to denying necessary truths and proving the infinite, both absurd.
This foreshadows the critique by later scholars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطريق السادس :
بطلان الدليل يؤذن ببطلان المدلول :
«ما لا دليل عليه ـ يجب نفيه »(٢) أو نضعه في الصورة التي يضعها فيها الباقلاني « بطلان الدليل يؤذن ببطلان المدلول »(٣) .
ويتكون هذا الطريق من مرحلتين : المرحلة الأولى أن نتلمس أدلة المثبتين للشيء ، وتثبت كذبها وضعفها بحيث لا نجد دليلاً آخر على ثبوت الشيء سواها . وإما أن نقوم بعملية حصر وجوه الأدلة . ثم نقوم باستقراء دقيق عليها ، ينتهي إلى نفي هذه الوجوه كلها بحيث لا نجد وجوهاً أخرى غيرها .
ومن هنا ، يتبين لنا أن هذه الصورة الثانية هي الأولى بعينها ، إذ تنتهي إلى عدم وجود أية أدلة غير الأدلة المنفية إلا أنها تمتاز عنها بفكرة الحصر . وقد كان بعض المتكلمين يرى في هذه الفكرة زيادة لا مبرر لها ، ولذلك عدل عنها إلى الصورة الأولى . فهي نفي ما لا دليل عليه لأن نفي ما لا دليل عليه ينتج انتفاء الضروريات والنظريات من ناحية وإثبات اللامتناهي من ناحية أخرى ـ وكلاهما محال .
وقد نقد المتأخرون هذا الطريق من ناحية أن المقدمتين الصغرى والكبرى ضعيفتان(٤) .
The sixth method: The invalidity of the evidence indicates the invalidity of the indicated. "What has no evidence for it must be denied" or we place it in the form that al-Baqillani places it: "The invalidity of the evidence indicates the invalidity of the indicated." This method consists of two stages: The first stage is that we seek out the evidence of those who affirm the thing, and we prove its falsehood and weakness, such that we find no other evidence for the existence of the thing besides it. Or we perform a process of enumerating the aspects of evidence. Then we subject them to a precise induction, which ends with negating all these aspects, such that we find no other aspects besides them. From here, it becomes clear to us that this second form is the first itself, since it ends with the non-existence of any evidence other than the negated evidence, except that it is distinguished by the idea of enumeration. Some theologians considered this idea an unnecessary addition, and therefore they turned away from it to the first form. So it is the negation of what has no evidence, because negating what has no evidence results in the negation of necessary and theoretical things on one hand, and the proof of the infinite on the other—both are impossible. The later scholars criticized this method from the aspect that both the minor and major premises are weak.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage defines the sixth method of reasoning in Islamic logic, known as "invalidity of proof indicates invalidity of the proved." The author first states the principle in two formulations: one as "what has no evidence must be denied" and another from al-Baqillani. The method has two stages. In the first, one examines the evidence of those who affirm a proposition and proves it false or weak, leaving no evidence for the proposition. In the second, one performs an exhaustive enumeration of all possible proofs and refutes them. The author notes that these two stages are essentially the same, but the second explicitly adds the idea of exhaustive enumeration (al-hasr). Some theologians found this addition unnecessary and preferred the first form. The author then points out a criticism: applying this method leads to denying necessary and theoretical truths (since they may lack explicit evidence in some sense) and implying the infinite—both impossible. Later scholars critiqued the method because its minor and major premises (the enumeration of all evidence and the claim that all evidence is invalid) are weak. This passage thus both explains the method and highlights its vulnerabilities, essential for understanding its proper application in Islamic dialectic.

Key Terms
بطلان الدليل
بطلان المدلول
الحصر
المقدمتان الصغرى والكبرى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What distinguishes the second form of the sixth method from the first form?
A) It uses exhaustive enumeration.
B) It relies on induction.
C) It was proposed by later scholars.
D) It denies necessary truths.

Key Takeaway
The sixth method is a logical tool, but its application requires caution to avoid absurd conclusions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Read the passage again. Write down the two forms of the sixth method in your own words.
?
2. Find an example from Islamic theology where a claim might be rejected because of lack of evidence. Does the method apply correctly here? Why or why not?

Critique of the Sixth Method: Weakness of Its Premises
Author presents the critique of the sixth method by later scholars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the logical critique of the sixth method and evaluate its validity.

Core Idea
The sixth method fails because its premises cannot be reliably established.

This lesson examines the critique of the sixth method by later scholars. They argued that the method's two premises—that all evidence has been enumerated and that all such evidence is invalid—are inherently weak, leading to unacceptable consequences in theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Critique
The later scholars targeted the sixth method. Find the sentence in the passage that states the critique: 'the later scholars criticized this method from the aspect that both the minor and major premises are weak.'
The minor premise is that all evidence has been enumerated; the major premise is that all enumerated evidence is invalid.
2
Analyze the Minor Premise
The minor premise requires exhaustive enumeration of all evidence. Is this ever possible, especially for complex theological claims? Consider the difficulty of proving that no evidence exists beyond what you have examined.
This premise is difficult to establish with certainty.
3
Analyze the Major Premise
The major premise claims that all enumerated evidence is invalid. Even if you have a list of proofs, proving each one invalid may be subjective or incomplete. What if a valid proof exists but you missed it?
This premise is also weak because proving invalidity of each piece of evidence is challenging.
4
Understand the Implications
The author notes that applying the method leads to denying necessary truths (self-evident propositions) and proving the infinite (an impossible conclusion). This is a reductio ad absurdum of the method.
This shows that the method cannot be universally applied without causing absurdities.
5
Evaluate the Critique
The critique itself is logically sound: it points out the weakness of the premises and shows that the method leads to contradictions. Therefore, the sixth method is not a reliable tool for establishing falsehood.
This does not mean the method is never useful; it means its use is limited to cases where premises are certain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Independence of Islamic Logic: Fitra and Need
Author summarizes the nature of Islamic logical methods and their independence from Aristotle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the concept of fitra (innate disposition) supports the claim that Islamic logical methods were independently developed, not derived from Aristotle.

Core Idea
Islamic logic was born from innate human disposition, not borrowed from Aristotle.

The author asserts that Islamic methods of research, including qiyas usuli, are not imitations of Aristotle. Instead, they arose from the natural disposition (fitra) of Muslims and their practical need for reasoning. This lesson unpacks that claim and its significance for understanding Islamic intellectual independence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's two key claims about Islamic methods
The author states that all methods can be reduced to qiyas usuli and that some resemble Aristotelian forms. But he immediately denies derivation.
2
Understand the role of fitra
Fitra is the innate human disposition that guided Muslims to develop logical forms on their own, without external borrowing.
This concept is central to Islamic theology, often used to explain natural knowledge of God.
3
Contrast with the idea of borrowing from Aristotle
The author explicitly says that similarity does not mean copying. Muslims arrived at similar forms because fitra leads to them, not because they read Aristotle.
4
Relate to Ibn Taymiyya's reinforcement
Ibn Taymiyya will later emphasize that Muslims, through fitra, reached many Aristotelian forms but with more precise Arabic expressions.
This shows continuity in the Islamic logical tradition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذه هي طرق البحث عند المسلمين . وهي تكون مع القياس الأصولي مذهباً منطقياً كاملاً . غير أنه ينبغي أن نلاحظ في هذه الطرق التي عرضناها في هذا الفصل مسألتين : المسألة الأولى - أنه يمكن ردها الى القياس الأصولي ، فهي صورة منه . المسألة الثانية - أن بعض صور هذه الطرق يبدو أنها موجودة في منطق أرسطو . وليس معنى هذا أن المسلمين استمدوها من هذا المنطق . بل وصل إليها المسلمون بفطرتهم ولحاجتهم إلى وضع صور للاستدلال والبحث العلمي . وسيردد ابن تيمية هذه الفكرة ويوضحها . وسيقرر أن المسلمين وصلوا بفطرتهم إلى كثير من صور المنطق الأرسططاليسي ولكن في أساليبهم الخاصة وفي عبارات أدق وأضبط - من حيث صلتها باللغة العربية - من عبارات المنطق الأرسططاليسي وأساليبه . ويضاف إلى رأى ابن تيمية هذا استناد كثير من هذه الطرق إلى طريق القياس الأصولي - وقد رأينا أن هذا القياس يخالف التمثيل الأرسططاليسي مخالفة تامة ويقوم على أساس لا يتفق أي اتفاق مع منطق أرسطو . . . . هذا كله يدعونا إلى القول بأننا أمام منطق يخالف منطق أرسطو في جوهره : منطق يتكون من مبحث في الحد لا يستند على فكرة الماهية وهي جوهر هذا المبحث عند أرسطو . ومن مبحث في الاستدلالات يقوم على التجربة المؤدية إلى اليقين - وهي عنصر لم يعرفها أرسطو . وقد أدى عدم الاعتراف بالمنطق الأرسططاليسي إلى مهاجمة الأساس الذي يقوم عليه هذا المنطق - فقام بعض مفكري الإسلام بنقد مبدأ عدم الجمع بين النقيضين وعدم ارتفاع النقيضين ( مبدأ عدم التناقض ومبدأ الثالث المرفوع ) كما قام من الأشاعرة من نقد مبدأ العلية - وهذا ما سنوضحه في الفصلين القادمين .
These are the methods of research according to Muslims. They, together with qiyas usuli, constitute a complete logical doctrine. However, we should note two matters regarding these methods that we presented in this chapter: The first matter—they can be reduced to qiyas usuli, so they are a form of it. The second matter—some forms of these methods appear to exist in Aristotle's logic. This does not mean that Muslims derived them from this logic. Rather, Muslims arrived at them through their innate disposition (fitra) and their need to formulate forms of reasoning and scientific research. Ibn Taymiyya will reiterate this idea and clarify it. He will affirm that Muslims, through their fitra, arrived at many forms of Aristotelian logic, but in their own methods and in expressions more precise and accurate—in relation to the Arabic language—than the expressions and methods of Aristotelian logic. Added to this opinion of Ibn Taymiyya is the reliance of many of these methods on the path of qiyas usuli—and we have seen that this qiyas completely differs from Aristotelian analogy and is based on a foundation that does not agree with Aristotle's logic at all. All this leads us to say that we are faced with a logic that differs from Aristotle's logic in its essence: a logic consisting of a discussion of definition (hadd) that is not based on the idea of quiddity, which is the essence of this discussion for Aristotle, and a discussion of inferences based on experience leading to certainty—an element that Aristotle did not know. The lack of recognition of Aristotelian logic led to attacking the foundation upon which this logic is based. Some Islamic thinkers criticized the principle of non-conjunction of two opposites and the principle of non-repulsion of two opposites (principle of non-contradiction and the principle of the excluded middle), just as some Ash'aris criticized the principle of causality—and this we will explain in the coming two chapters.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage concludes the chapter on Islamic research methods by synthesizing key insights. The author first states that these methods, together with qiyas usuli (analogical reasoning in usul al-fiqh), form a complete logical system. Two observations follow: first, all methods can be reduced to qiyas usuli; second, some forms resemble Aristotelian logic. However, the author emphatically denies derivation from Aristotle, asserting that Muslims independently developed these methods through their natural disposition (fitra) and practical needs. Ibn Taymiyya's view is invoked: Muslims arrived at many Aristotelian forms through their own genius, but with more precise Arabic formulations. Furthermore, the foundation of qiyas usuli fundamentally differs from Aristotelian analogy (tamthil). The passage culminates in a bold claim: Islamic logic diverges from Aristotelian logic in essence. Its treatment of definition (hadd) does not rely on the notion of quiddity (mahiyya), and its theory of inference is based on empirical certainty, an element foreign to Aristotle. This divergence led to critiques of Aristotelian logical principles, including the principle of non-contradiction and the excluded middle, as well as the principle of causality (by Ash'aris). The author promises to elaborate in subsequent chapters. This passage thus provides a high-level epistemological justification for the distinctiveness of Islamic logic.

Essential Differences: Definition and Empirical Certainty
Author highlights how Islamic logic fundamentally differs from Aristotle's logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish Islamic logic from Aristotelian logic based on two key features: the treatment of definition (hadd) without quiddity and the reliance on empirical certainty in inferences.

Core Idea
Islamic logic redefines definition without quiddity and grounds inference in empirical certainty.

The author claims that Islamic logic differs from Aristotle's in its very essence. This lesson focuses on the two specific differences he highlights: the concept of definition (hadd) is not based on quiddity (mahiyya), and inferences rely on experience that leads to certainty—something Aristotle did not incorporate.

Guided Path
1
Extract the claim about definition (hadd)
The author says Islamic logic's treatment of definition is not based on the idea of quiddity, which is central to Aristotle's approach.
Quiddity (mahiyya) refers to the 'whatness' or essence of a thing.
2
Contrast with Aristotelian definition
Aristotelian definition relies on genus and differentia to capture the essence (quiddity). Islamic logic, especially in usul al-fiqh, defines terms based on observable characteristics (like 'illa).
3
Extract the claim about inferences
Islamic logic bases inferences on experience (tajriba) that yields certainty (yaqin). This empirical element is absent in Aristotle's demonstrative science.
This links to the Islamic emphasis on empirical observation in fields like medicine and astronomy.
4
Understand why these differences matter
These differences show that Islamic logic is not a mere variant of Aristotle's; it has its own epistemology and methodology.
This explains why later scholars critiqued Aristotelian principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

These are the methods of research according to Muslims. They, together with qiyas usuli, constitute a complete logical doctrine. However, we should note two matters regarding these methods that we presented in this chapter: The first matter—they can be reduced to qiyas usuli, so they are a form of it. The second matter—some forms of these methods appear to exist in Aristotle's logic. This does not mean that Muslims derived them from this logic. Rather, Muslims arrived at them through their innate disposition (fitra) and their need to formulate forms of reasoning and scientific research. Ibn Taymiyya will reiterate this idea and clarify it. He will affirm that Muslims, through their fitra, arrived at many forms of Aristotelian logic, but in their own methods and in expressions more precise and accurate—in relation to the Arabic language—than the expressions and methods of Aristotelian logic. Added to this opinion of Ibn Taymiyya is the reliance of many of these methods on the path of qiyas usuli—and we have seen that this qiyas completely differs from Aristotelian analogy and is based on a foundation that does not agree with Aristotle's logic at all. All this leads us to say that we are faced with a logic that differs from Aristotle's logic in its essence: a logic consisting of a discussion of definition (hadd) that is not based on the idea of quiddity, which is the essence of this discussion for Aristotle, and a discussion of inferences based on experience leading to certainty—an element that Aristotle did not know. Critique of Aristotelian Foundations and the Centrality of Qiyas Usuli
Author discusses the critique of Aristotelian principles and implies the foundational role of qiyas usuli.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the Islamic critique of Aristotelian logical principles (non-contradiction, excluded middle, causality) and explain how qiyas usuli serves as the core unifying method of Islamic logic.

Core Idea
Islamic thinkers critiqued Aristotle's principles and centered logic on qiyas usuli, not on Aristotelian syllogistic.

The author notes that the rejection of Aristotelian logic led to critiques of its foundational principles: non-contradiction, excluded middle, and causality. He also earlier stated that all Islamic methods can be reduced to qiyas usuli. This lesson explores these two aspects: the critical stance toward Aristotle and the positive role of qiyas usuli as the core of Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Aristotelian principles that were critiqued
The author mentions the principle of non-contradiction (and excluded middle) and the principle of causality. Some Islamic thinkers rejected these as absolute.
The critique of causality is particularly associated with Ash'ari occasionalism.
2
Understand why these principles were attacked
Because Islamic logic is based on different foundations (definition without quiddity, empirical certainty), the Aristotelian principles were seen as unnecessary or even contradictory to Islamic theology (e.g., occasionalism denies necessary causation).
3
Recall the earlier claim about qiyas usuli
The author stated that all Islamic methods can be reduced to qiyas usuli, making it the central method.
Qiyas usuli is analogy based on 'illa (ratio legis).
4
Connect critique and centrality
By rejecting Aristotelian logic and centering qiyas usuli, Islamic thinkers created a logic that is both independent and tailored to Islamic sciences.
This synthesis shows the coherence of the Islamic logical tradition.

Islamic Logic's Independence from Aristotle
Author concludes that Islamic methods constitute a logic independent of Aristotle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the author's two arguments for the independence of Islamic logic: its origin from al-qiyas al-usuli and its development through fitra.

Core Idea
Islamic logic was born from usul al-fiqh and natural disposition, not Greek translation.

In this passage, the author summarizes why Islamic logical methods are not simply borrowed from Aristotle. He presents two key observations that together establish an independent logical tradition rooted in Islamic scholarship and innate human capacity. Let's walk through his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Locate the first observation
Find where the author states that Islamic methods can be reduced to qiyas usuli. This shows they originate from an indigenous Islamic discipline.
The author says: 'يمكن ردها الى القياس الأصولي، فهي صورة منه'.
2
Locate the second observation
Find where the author argues that Muslims arrived at these methods through their natural disposition (fitra) and need, not by borrowing from Aristotle.
The key phrase: 'بل وصل إليها المسلمون بفطرتهم ولحاجتهم إلى وضع صور للاستدلال'.
3
Identify Ibn Taymiyya's role
The author references Ibn Taymiyya as a proponent of this independence. He will later argue that Muslims independently reached many Aristotelian forms but expressed them more precisely in Arabic.
Ibn Taymiyya's support strengthens the claim of independence.
4
Note the contrast with Aristotle
The author emphasizes that qiyas usuli 'completely contradicts' Aristotelian analogy (tamthil). This marks a fundamental difference.
This reinforces that the logical system is not derivative.

These are the methods of research among the Muslims. They, together with qiyas usuli, constitute a complete logical school. However, it should be noted regarding these methods that we have presented in this chapter two issues: The first issue - that they can be reduced to qiyas usuli, so they are a form of it. The second issue - that some forms of these methods appear to be present in Aristotle's logic. But this does not mean that the Muslims derived them from this logic. Rather, the Muslims arrived at them through their natural disposition and their need to establish forms of reasoning and scientific research. Ibn Taymiyya will reiterate this idea and clarify it. He will affirm that the Muslims, through their natural disposition, arrived at many forms of Aristotelian logic, but in their own methods and in more precise and accurate expressions - in terms of their relation to the Arabic language - than the expressions of Aristotelian logic and its methods. Added to this opinion of Ibn Taymiyya is the reliance of many of these methods on the method of qiyas usuli - and we have seen that this qiyas completely contradicts Aristotelian analogy and is based on a foundation that does not agree in any way with Aristotle's logic. ... All of this leads us to say that we are faced with a logic that contradicts Aristotle's logic in its essence: a logic consisting of a discussion of definition (hadd) that is not based on the idea of essence (mahiyya), which is the core of this discussion for Aristotle. And a discussion of inferences based on experience leading to certainty - an element that Aristotle did not know. The failure to recognize Aristotelian logic led to attacking the foundation upon which this logic is based - so some Muslim thinkers criticized the principle of non-conjunction of contradictories and non-removal of contradictories (the principle of non-contradiction and the principle of the excluded middle), and some Ash'aris criticized the principle of causality - and this we will clarify in the coming two chapters.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage is a concluding summary of the previous chapter's discussion on Islamic methods of research. The author asserts that these methods, when combined with qiyas usuli (analogical reasoning in usul al-fiqh), form a complete logical system. He then raises two important observations. First, these methods can be reduced to qiyas usuli, meaning they are essentially forms of it. Second, although some forms resemble Aristotelian logic, the Muslims did not borrow them from Aristotle; rather, they arrived at them through their natural disposition (fitra) and practical need. The author references Ibn Taymiyya, who will later argue that Muslims independently reached many Aristotelian logical forms but expressed them more precisely in Arabic. This independence is further supported by the fact that many of these methods are based on qiyas usuli, which fundamentally differs from Aristotelian analogy (tamthil). The author concludes that Islamic logic differs from Aristotle's in essence: its theory of definition (hadd) does not rely on the concept of essence (mahiyya), and its theory of inference is grounded in experience leading to certainty, an element absent in Aristotle. This rejection of Aristotelian logic led to criticism of its foundational principles, namely the law of non-contradiction and the law of excluded middle, as well as the principle of causality by Ash'ari theologians. These critiques will be detailed in the following chapters.

Key Takeaway

Core Differences Between Islamic and Aristotelian Logic
Author contrasts Islamic logic's definition and inference with Aristotle's.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify two fundamental differences between Islamic and Aristotelian logic as presented in the passage: the basis of definition and the basis of inference.

Core Idea
Islamic logic rejects essence-based definitions and embraces empirical certainty, diverging from Aristotle's framework.

After establishing independence, the author specifies how Islamic logic fundamentally differs from Aristotle's. These differences are not superficial but concern the core of what logic is about: how we define things and how we make inferences. Let's extract these differences.

Guided Path
1
Find the author's statement on definition (hadd)
Look for where the author describes Islamic logic's treatment of definition. He says it is not based on the idea of essence (mahiyya), which is central to Aristotle's approach.
The key phrase: 'مبحث في الحد لا يستند على فكرة الماهية'.
2
Contrast with Aristotle's approach
The author explicitly states that for Aristotle, the concept of essence (mahiyya) is the core (جوهر) of his discussion on definition. Islamic logic does not rely on this.
This shows a fundamental divergence in the theory of definition.
3
Find the author's statement on inference (istidlal)
Look for where the author describes Islamic logic's approach to inference. He says it is based on experience (tajriba) leading to certainty (yaqin).
The key phrase: 'يقوم على التجربة المؤدية إلى اليقين'.
4
Note Aristotle's omission
The author claims that this element (experience leading to certainty) was not known to Aristotle. This is a second core difference.
This implies that Aristotelian logic does not incorporate empirical verification as a basis for certainty.

هذه هي طرق البحث عند المسلمين . وهي تكون مع القياس الأصولي مذهباً منطقياً كاملاً . غير أنه ينبغي أن نلاحظ في هذه الطرق التي عرضناها في هذا الفصل مسألتين : المسألة الأولى - أنه يمكن ردها الى القياس الأصولي ، فهي صورة منه . المسألة الثانية - أن بعض صور هذه الطرق يبدو أنها موجودة في منطق أرسطو . وليس معنى هذا أن المسلمين استمدوها من هذا المنطق . بل وصل إليها المسلمون بفطرتهم ولحاجتهم إلى وضع صور للاستدلال والبحث العلمي . وسيردد ابن تيمية هذه الفكرة ويوضحها . وسيقرر أن المسلمين وصلوا بفطرتهم إلى كثير من صور المنطق الأرسططاليسي ولكن في أساليبهم الخاصة وفي عبارات أدق وأضبط - من حيث صلتها باللغة العربية - من عبارات المنطق الأرسططاليسي وأساليبه . ويضاف إلى رأى ابن تيمية هذا استناد كثير من هذه الطرق إلى طريق القياس الأصولي - وقد رأينا أن هذا القياس يخالف التمثيل الأرسططاليسي مخالفة تامة ويقوم على أساس لا يتفق أي اتفاق مع منطق أرسطو . . . . هذا كله يدعونا إلى القول بأننا أمام منطق يخالف منطق أرسطو في جوهره : منطق يتكون من مبحث في الحد لا يستند على فكرة الماهية وهي جوهر هذا المبحث عند أرسطو . ومن مبحث في الاستدلالات يقوم على التجربة المؤدية إلى اليقين - وهي عنصر لم يعرفها أرسطو . وقد أدى عدم الاعتراف بالمنطق الأرسططاليسي إلى مهاجمة الأساس الذي يقوم عليه هذا المنطق - فقام بعض مفكري الإسلام بنقد مبدأ عدم الجمع بين النقيضين وعدم ارتفاع النقيضين ( مبدأ عدم التناقض ومبدأ الثالث المرفوع ) كما قام من الأشاعرة من نقد مبدأ العلية - وهذا ما سنوضحه في الفصلين القادمين .
These are the methods of research among the Muslims. They, together with qiyas usuli, constitute a complete logical school. However, it should be noted regarding these methods that we have presented in this chapter two issues: The first issue - that they can be reduced to qiyas usuli, so they are a form of it. The second issue - that some forms of these methods appear to be present in Aristotle's logic. But this does not mean that the Muslims derived them from this logic. Rather, the Muslims arrived at them through their natural disposition and their need to establish forms of reasoning and scientific research. Ibn Taymiyya will reiterate this idea and clarify it. He will affirm that the Muslims, through their natural disposition, arrived at many forms of Aristotelian logic, but in their own methods and in more precise and accurate expressions - in terms of their relation to the Arabic language - than the expressions of Aristotelian logic and its methods. Added to this opinion of Ibn Taymiyya is the reliance of many of these methods on the method of qiyas usuli - and we have seen that this qiyas completely contradicts Aristotelian analogy and is based on a foundation that does not agree in any way with Aristotle's logic. ... All of this leads us to say that we are faced with a logic that contradicts Aristotle's logic in its essence: a logic consisting of a discussion of definition (hadd) that is not based on the idea of essence (mahiyya), which is the core of this discussion for Aristotle. And a discussion of inferences based on experience leading to certainty - an element that Aristotle did not know. The failure to recognize Aristotelian logic led to attacking the foundation upon which this logic is based - so some Muslim thinkers criticized the principle of non-conjunction of contradictories and non-removal of contradictories (the principle of non-contradiction and the principle of the excluded middle), and some Ash'aris criticized the principle of causality - and this we will clarify in the coming two chapters.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage is a concluding summary of the previous chapter's discussion on Islamic methods of research. The author asserts that these methods, when combined with qiyas usuli (analogical reasoning in usul al-fiqh), form a complete logical system. He then raises two important observations. First, these methods can be reduced to qiyas usuli, meaning they are essentially forms of it. Second, although some forms resemble Aristotelian logic, the Muslims did not borrow them from Aristotle; rather, they arrived at them through their natural disposition (fitra) and practical need. The author references Ibn Taymiyya, who will later argue that Muslims independently reached many Aristotelian logical forms but expressed them more precisely in Arabic. This independence is further supported by the fact that many of these methods are based on qiyas usuli, which fundamentally differs from Aristotelian analogy (tamthil). The author concludes that Islamic logic differs from Aristotle's in essence: its theory of definition (hadd) does not rely on the concept of essence (mahiyya), and its theory of inference is grounded in experience leading to certainty, an element absent in Aristotle. This rejection of Aristotelian logic led to criticism of its foundational principles, namely the law of non-contradiction and the law of excluded middle, as well as the principle of causality by Ash'ari theologians. These critiques will be detailed in the following chapters.

Islamic logic grounds definition and inference differently: definitions are not tied to essences, and inferences rely on empirical certainty.

Muslim Criticism of Aristotelian Principles
Author notes Muslim thinkers' criticism of Aristotelian principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the three Aristotelian principles that Muslim thinkers criticized according to the passage: non-contradiction, excluded middle, and causality.

Core Idea
Some Muslim thinkers attacked the principles of non-contradiction, excluded middle, and causality, challenging Aristotle's foundation.

The author concludes by noting that the rejection of Aristotelian logic led to direct attacks on its most basic principles. Muslim thinkers, particularly Ibn Taymiyya and Ash'ari theologians, questioned the law of non-contradiction, the law of excluded middle, and the principle of causality. This lesson explores these critiques.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first two principles
Find where the author mentions 'mabda' 'adam al-jam' bayn al-naqidayn' (non-contradiction) and 'mabda' 'adam irtifa' al-naqidayn' (excluded middle).
These are the two principles explicitly named in the passage.
2
Identify the third principle
Find where the author mentions 'mabda' al-'illiyya' (principle of causality), which is criticized by some Ash'aris.
This is the third principle attacked.
3
Note the groups involved
The passage says 'some Muslim thinkers' criticized the first two, and 'some Ash'aris' criticized causality.
This distinguishes between general Muslim thinkers and a specific theological school.
4
Understand the context
The author says these attacks resulted from 'failure to recognize Aristotelian logic' (عدم الاعتراف بالمنطق الأرسططاليسي). This implies that the critiques were part of a broader rejection.
The following chapters will elaborate on these critiques.

The Twofold Nature of Ibn Taymiyya's Logical Critique
Methodological roadmap for analyzing Ibn Taymiyya's critique

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the destructive and constructive aspects of Ibn Taymiyya's critique and map them onto the three sections of Aristotelian logic: definition, proposition, and syllogism.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya's critique is not just rejection; it builds a Quranic alternative.

This lesson introduces the author's methodology for studying Ibn Taymiyya's critique of Aristotelian logic. Because Ibn Taymiyya's logical writings are complex and repetitive, the author proposes a clear framework: separating the critique into two aspects—destructive (refutation) and constructive (alternative). This framework will be applied to three core subjects: definition, proposition, and syllogism. Understanding this structure will help you navigate Ibn Taymiyya's arguments and appreciate his original contributions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two overarching aspects
In the passage, the author divides Ibn Taymiyya's critique into a 'destructive aspect' (al-janib al-hadim) and a 'constructive aspect' (al-janib al-insha'i). The destructive aspect refutes Aristotelian doctrines; the constructive aspect proposes Islamic alternatives.
Destructive does not mean mere negation; it is a critical dismantling.
2
Recognize the three sections of Aristotelian logic
The destructive and constructive aspects are applied to three specific subjects: definition (al-hadd), proposition (al-qadiyya), and syllogism (al-qiyas). These correspond to the three main chapters of the study.
These are the classical topics of Aristotelian logic that Ibn Taymiyya targeted.
3
Understand the two positions within the destructive aspect
Within the destructive aspect of definition (and similarly for syllogism), Ibn Taymiyya critiques two positions: the negative position (al-maqam al-salib) that 'the required concept is not attained except by definition', and the positive position (al-maqam al-mujib) that 'definition gives knowledge of concepts'.
These positions are the claims of Aristotelian logicians that Ibn Taymiyya refutes.
4
Locate the sources for the constructive aspect
The author extracts Ibn Taymiyya's constructive views from various works: 'Muwafaqat Sarih al-Ma'qul li Sahih al-Manqul', 'Minhaj al-Sunna', 'Majmu' al-Rasa'il al-Kubra', 'al-Sab'iniyya', and 'al-'Aqida al-Isfahaniyya'. These provide Quranic forms of inference.
These are key texts for understanding Ibn Taymiyya's positive project.
5
Synthesize the overall plan
Combine the two aspects and three sections: The upcoming chapters will treat definition (Chapter 2), proposition (Chapter 3), and syllogism (Chapter 4), each broken into destructive and constructive parts. This grid structures the entire study.
This step reinforces the big picture before diving into details.

CHAPTER TWO: Critique of Aristotelian Logic according to Ibn Taymiyya - Critique of the Subject of Definition. We said in the previous chapter that we will divide Ibn Taymiyya's critique of Aristotelian logic into three sections: a section in which Ibn Taymiyya critiques definition, a section in which he critiques the proposition, and a section in which he critiques the syllogism. And we said that we will present under each of these sections two aspects of Ibn Taymiyya's thinking - the first aspect is the destructive, and the second is the constructive. The first aspect includes what Ibn Taymiyya called the negative and positive positions, and the second aspect includes Ibn Taymiyya's views on Quranic logic, sometimes extracted from these two previous positions and from other books by Ibn Taymiyya. And we will discuss in this chapter the first section of Ibn Taymiyya's critique, i.e., the critique of the Aristotelian definition. And the critique of the Aristotelian definition is divided, as we said, into two aspects: 1 - The destructive aspect. 2 - The constructive aspect. 1 - The destructive aspect: The critique of the subject of Aristotelian definition. This aspect is divided into two sections or positions: a negative position and a positive position. The negative position is 'the required concept is not attained except by definition' and the positive position is 'definition gives knowledge of concepts, and we can discuss each of these two
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage serves as a methodological introduction, outlining the author's approach to studying Ibn Taymiyya's critique of Aristotelian logic. The author first notes that the complexity and repetition of issues in Ibn Taymiyya's logical thought necessitate a clear methodology. The methodology proposed is to divide Ibn Taymiyya's critique into two overarching aspects: a destructive aspect (al-janib al-hadim) and a constructive aspect (al-janib al-insha'i). In the destructive aspect, the author will present Ibn Taymiyya's critique of two positions—negative and positive—concerning definition (al-hadd) and syllogism (al-qiyas). In the constructive aspect, the author will extract Ibn Taymiyya's positive views on Islamic definition and Quranic forms of inference from various works. The author then states that the coming chapters will follow this plan: Chapter one on definition, chapter two on proposition, and chapter three on syllogism, each covering both destructive and constructive aspects. The passage then introduces Chapter Two specifically, which focuses on the critique of Aristotelian definition. It reiterates the three-section division (definition, proposition, syllogism) and the two-aspect division (destructive and constructive). For the destructive aspect of definition, it is further split into two 'maqams' (positions): a negative maqam (the claim that the required concept is not attained except by definition) and a positive maqam (the claim that definition gives knowledge of concepts). The author intends to discuss each. This passage is essentially a roadmap for the reader, explaining the structure and scope of the study that follows.

Ibn Taymiyya's Empirical Epistemology: Why Medical Experience Trumps Syllogisms
Ibn Taymiyya argues for the primacy of empirical experience over syllogistic reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that concrete particulars and repeated empirical experience provide more certain knowledge than syllogistic logic.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya argues that only repeated empirical observation, especially in medicine, yields genuine knowledge.

In this passage, a modern scholar presents Ibn Taymiyya's radical epistemological stance. Ibn Taymiyya rejects the Aristotelian syllogism as a source of real knowledge, instead championing experiential knowledge gained through repeated observation, particularly in medicine. This lesson unpacks his argument and its implications for Islamic methodology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main epistemological claim
Find where Ibn Taymiyya affirms knowledge of particulars (al-muʿayyanāt) and denies that syllogistic logic attains anything real.
Look for the contrast between al-manṭiq al-qiyāsī and al-tajriba.
2
Extract the positive argument for experience
Locate the reasons Ibn Taymiyya gives for why experience (tajriba) is closer to truth, especially medical experience.
He mentions participation of many physicians, abundance of plants, and agreement of experimenters.
3
Analyze the medical example
Read the direct quote from Ibn Taymiyya about physicians' experiments. Identify the specific reasons he gives for their reliability.
Contrast this with philosophical claims that lack such practical verification.
4
Contrast the two paths
The author notes a difference between the Greek path and the Muslim path. Explain how each path relates to civilization and how Ibn Taymiyya's call for Islamic empirical logic fits.
Consider the civilizational implications of methodological choice.

إن ابن تيمية يؤمن بالمعينات، بالجزئيات، ويرى أن المنطق القياسي لا يصل إلى شيء واقعي . وينتهي إلى القول بأن التجربة وحدها هي أقرب إلى الحقيقة مما ينتجه الفلاسفة بقياس . وتجربة الأطباء هي أسد التجارب يقول « إن ما يدعى في تجارب الاطباء من الأدوية أقرب إلى التسليم من تجاربهم الرصدية وذلك لاشتراك كثير من الأطباء في تجربة الدواء الواحد ، وأيضاً فلكثرة وجود ما يدعى فيه التجربة من النبات ، وتواطؤ المجربين له وليس كذلك ما يدعونه من فلسفتهم : فإنا رأينا تجارب الأطباء على غير منوالهم . وعلمنا صدقها كثيراً . . . والأطباء في تجاربهم أسد حالاً منهم ، لأن القوم إنما يذكرون دواءاً موجوداً يمكن تجربته كثيراً لوجوده ، ولكثرة المحتاجين إليه ممن يصيبه مما يقال : إن ذلك الدواء يؤثر فيه شفاء(۲) . وليس هناك في الحقيقة من تكلم - فيما قبل العصور الحديثة - بما تكلم به ابن تيمية . لقد وصل حقاً إلى أوج الدرج في فلسفة المنهج التجريبي ، بنقده للمنطق اليوناني القياسي وبدعوته إلى المنطق الإسلامي التجريبي ، وعبر عن روح الحضارة الإسلامية الحقة في عصر الإنهيار الحضاري الإسلامي الذي عاش فيه . لقد تنبه ابن تيمية - إلى ما تنبه اليه الاقدمون من نظار المسلمين من اختلاف الطريقين - طريق اليونان وطريق المسلمين - في العلم ، وأن كل طريق من هذين الطريقين قد أقام حضارة مباينة للحضارة الأخرى . وأن كل طريق منهما كان منهج حضارة معينة ، فلما قامت دعوة بعض المسلمين إلى استخدام هذا المنطق ومزجه بعلوم المسلمين ، صرخ ابن تيمية صرخته ، وقد (۱) ابن تيمية : الرد ص ٣٧٦ - ٣٧٧ . (۲) ابن تيمية : الرد ص ۳۹۳ .
Ibn Taymiyya believes in concrete individuals, in particulars, and holds that logical syllogism does not attain anything real. He concludes that experience alone is closer to truth than what the philosophers produce through syllogism. The experience of physicians is the most reliable of experiences. He says: 'What is claimed in the experiments of physicians concerning medicines is closer to acceptance than their observational experiments, due to the participation of many physicians in the experiment of a single medicine, and also due to the abundance of what is claimed in the experiment from plants, and the agreement of the experimenters. It is not so with what they claim from their philosophy: for we have seen the experiments of physicians on a different basis, and we have known their truth much... The physicians in their experiments are more correct than them, because the people only mention an existing medicine that can be experimented upon many times due to its existence, and due to the abundance of those in need of it who are afflicted with what it is said that medicine affects as cure.' There is truly no one who spoke – before modern times – what Ibn Taymiyya spoke. He indeed reached the pinnacle in the philosophy of the empirical method, by his critique of Greek logical syllogism and his call for Islamic empirical logic, and he expressed the spirit of true Islamic civilization in the era of Islamic civilizational decline in which he lived. Ibn Taymiyya noticed – what earlier Muslim theorists noticed – the difference between the two paths: the path of the Greeks and the path of the Muslims in knowledge, and that each of these two paths established a civilization distinct from the other. And that each of them was the method of a particular civilization. So when the call of some Muslims to use this logic and mix it with Islamic sciences arose, Ibn Taymiyya cried out his cry, and (1) Ibn Taymiyya: al-Radd pp. 376-377. (2) Ibn Taymiyya: al-Radd p. 393.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Taymiyya's epistemological position: he affirms knowledge of concrete particulars (al-muʿayyanāt, al-juzʾiyyāt) and denies that formal logical syllogism (al-manṭiq al-qiyāsī) can attain real knowledge. He argues that experience (tajriba) alone is closer to truth. He particularly praises medical experience (tajribat al-aṭibbāʾ) as the most reliable type of experience. He quotes Ibn Taymiyya directly: medical experiments on medicines are more trustworthy than 'observational experiments' (tajāribuhum al-raṣdiyya) of philosophers because many physicians share the same experiment, the substances are abundant, and experimenters agree on results. In contrast, philosophical claims lack such consensus and practical verification. The author then comments that Ibn Taymiyya's thought reached the apex of empirical methodology before modern times, critiquing Greek syllogistic logic and calling for an Islamic empirical logic (al-manṭiq al-islāmī al-tajrībī). He embodied the spirit of true Islamic civilization during its decline. Finally, the author notes that Ibn Taymiyya recognized a fundamental difference between the Greek path and the Muslim path in knowledge. Each path produced a distinct civilization. Therefore, when some Muslims advocated mixing Greek logic with Islamic sciences, Ibn Taymiyya raised his voice in protest.

المعينات (al-muʿayyanāt)
المنطق القياسي (al-manṭiq al-qiyāsī)
التجربة (al-tajriba)
المنطق الإسلامي التجريبي (al-manṭiq al-islāmī al-tajrībī)

Ibn Taymiyya's empirical epistemology emphasizes that real knowledge comes from repeated observation of particulars, not from abstract syllogisms.

Ibn Taymiyya's Constructive Alternative: Al-Mizan as a Quantitative Scale
Author explains Ibn Taymiyya's constructive alternative: al-mizan as quantitative scale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two interpretations of al-mizan (justice vs. rational analogy) and explain how quantity serves as the foundation for Ibn Taymiyya's alternative method of inference.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya's mizan turns logical inference into a quantitative science grounded in the Qur'an.

In this passage, we see the constructive side of Ibn Taymiyya's critique of Aristotelian logic. He does not merely demolish; he builds an alternative method of inference rooted in the Qur'anic concept of al-mizan (the balance). This lesson unpacks how Ibn Taymiyya reinterprets al-mizan as a quantitative tool for comparing similarities and differences, applicable to both physical measurement and legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two interpretations of al-mizan
Find in the passage where the author explains the meaning of al-mizan. First, it is justice (‘adl), the means by which matters are weighed. Second, it is the proverbs struck (al-amthal al-madruiba) and rational analogies that combine similarities and separate differences. Note that both are rooted in the Qur'an.
These interpretations come from different exegetical traditions.
2
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's claim that al-mizan is quantitative
Locate the sentence where Ibn Taymiyya states that the balance is quantitative (kami) and based on quantity (kamm). Understand that for him, quantity is the material (madda) upon which the balance works.
3
Analyze the physical examples given
The passage gives examples: dirhams and heavy bodies (weight), measuring vessels (volume), time measurement via shadows, and lengths/widths/depths. In each case, a common amount (qadr mushtarak) is established to determine equality. See how the mizan operates as a tool for comparison.
These examples illustrate the quantitative foundation of the mizan in the natural sciences.
4
Consider the application to jurisprudence
The passage ends by hinting at applying the mizan in fiqh (jurisprudence) and legal matters (shar‘iyat). How would a quantitative scale help in determining similarities between legal cases? Think about qiyas (analogical reasoning) where a common ‘illah (effective cause) serves as the 'common measure'.
Ibn Taymiyya's mizan provides a rational framework for legal analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجانب الإنشائي لنقد ابن تيمية لمباحث الإستدلال أو صور الإستدلال التيمي لم يكن ابن تيمية هادماً فقط، كان يهدم جانباً، ليبني جانباً. فعل هذا في مبحث الحد، كما فعل هذا في مبحث القضايا. ويفعل نفس الشيء في مبحث الإستدلال. يرى ابن تيمية أن القرآن ـ وهو كتاب الوجود عند المسلمين ـ هو الذي يمدنا بصور الإستدلال أو بمعنى أدق يقدم لنا « الميزان » والميزان عند كثير من المفسرين هو العدل وهو ما تزن به الأمور، وهو ما يعرف به العدل. وفي تفسير آخر هو الأمثال المضروبة ـ والأقيسة العقلية التي تجمع بين المتماثلات وتفرق بين المختلفات(۱). أو ما يعرف به تماثل المتماثلات من الصفات والمقادير؛ وكذلك ما يعرف به من اختلاف المختلفات منها(۲). وابن تيمية يرى أن هذا الميزان كمي، يستند على الكم. ويعطي أمثلة واضحة في أن الكم هو مادة الميزان، أو المادة التي يعمل عليها الميزان، إن معرفة أن هذه الدراهم أو غيرها من الأجسام الثقيلة بقدر هذه تعرف بموازينها، وكذلك معرفة أن هذا الكيل مثل هذا يعرف بميزانه ـ وهو المكاييل. وكذلك قياس الزمان ـ إن معرفة أن هذا الزمان مثل هذا الزمان يعرف بموازينه التي نقدر بها الأوقات ونقيس به الظلال، كما نعرف به ما في مجاري المياه من كمية الماء والرمال. وكذلك نعرف به الأطوال والأعراض والأعماق ونضع به بين كل متماثلين قدراً مشتركاً يعرف به أن أحدهما مثل الآخر، فالميزان إذن أداة عقلية في علوم الكم البحتة، بل يبدو أنه عند ابن تيمية كمي أولاً وبالذات. فإذا انتقلنا إلى تطبيق الميزان في الفقهيات والشرعيات فنعرف الفروع (۱) ابن تيمية : الرد ص ۳۳۳ . (۲) نفس المصدر ص ۳۷۱ .
The constructive aspect of Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the inquiries of inference, or the Taymiyyan forms of inference. Ibn Taymiyya was not only a demolisher; he would demolish one side to build another. He did this in the topic of definition, as he did in the topic of propositions. And he does the same in the topic of inference. Ibn Taymiyya sees that the Qur'an - which is the book of existence for Muslims - is what provides us with forms of inference, or more precisely, presents to us 'the balance' (al-mizan). And the balance according to many exegetes is justice, and it is that by which matters are weighed, and that by which justice is known. In another interpretation, it is the proverbs struck - and the rational analogies that combine similarities and separate differences (1). Or that by which the similarity of similar things in attributes and measures is known; and likewise that by which the difference of different things from them is known (2). And Ibn Taymiyya sees that this balance is quantitative, based on quantity. And he gives clear examples that quantity is the material of the balance, or the material upon which the balance works. Indeed, knowing that these dirhams or other heavy bodies equal this measure is known by their scales, and likewise knowing that this measure is like that measure is known by its balance - which is the measuring vessels. And likewise the measurement of time - indeed, knowing that this time is like that time is known by its measures by which we estimate times and measure shadows, as we know by it the quantity of water and sand in water channels. And likewise we know by it lengths, widths, and depths, and we place by it between every two similar things a common amount by which it is known that one is like the other. So the balance is therefore a rational tool in the pure quantitative sciences. Rather, it appears that for Ibn Taymiyya it is quantitative primarily and essentially. Then if we move to applying the balance in jurisprudence and legal matters, we know the branches. (1) Ibn Taymiyya: al-Radd p. 333. (2) Same source p. 371.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage presents the constructive side of Ibn Taymiyya's critique, focusing on his alternative method of inference based on the Qur'anic concept of 'al-mizan' (the balance). The author begins by noting that Ibn Taymiyya was not merely destructive; he would demolish one aspect to build another, as seen in his works on definition and propositions, and now in inference. Ibn Taymiyya's central claim is that the Qur'an provides forms of inference, specifically the 'mizan'. The term is explained in two ways: first, as justice ('adl), the means by which matters are weighed and justice is known; second, as 'proverbs struck' (amthal madruiba) and rational analogies (aqisa 'aqliyya) that combine similar things and separate different things. The mizan is thus a tool for recognizing similarity and difference in attributes and measures. Crucially, Ibn Taymiyya sees this mizan as quantitative, based on quantity (kamm). He provides examples: measuring weight (dirhams, heavy bodies) with scales, measuring volume (kayl) with measuring vessels, measuring time with shadows and estimative units, and measuring lengths, widths, depths in water channels. In each case, a common amount is established to determine equality. Thus, the mizan is a rational tool for the pure quantitative sciences, and for Ibn Taymiyya it is primarily quantitative. The passage concludes by hinting at its application to fiqh (jurisprudence) and shar'i (legal) matters, where the mizan would be used to infer rulings for branches (furu') based on principles.

Key Terms
الميزان
الكم
الأمثال المضروبة

Ibn Taymiyya's al-mizan is a Qur'anically grounded, quantitative method for inference that establishes common measures to compare similarities and differences, applicable to both natural and legal sciences.

Ibn Taymiyya's Two Rational Methods
Author summarizes Ibn Taymiyya's innovative critique and his two rational methods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain Ibn Taymiyya's two rational methods for logical inquiry and their foundations in the Qur'an.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya replaces Aristotelian syllogism with two Qur'anic rational methods: the law of entailment and the distinction between types of names.

In this passage, the author concludes the discussion of Ibn Taymiyya's critique by highlighting its innovative, positive dimension. Ibn Taymiyya did not merely reject Aristotelian logic; he offered constructive alternatives grounded in deep logical insights and derived from the Qur'an. We will now explore his two rational methods and understand how they serve as a framework for Islamic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the context of the two methods
The passage states that each of the two methods 'is based on one of the deepest logical ideas.' Note that these methods are presented as alternatives to Aristotelian syllogistic, not as mere criticism.
2
Analyze the first method: law of talazum
The first method returns to the 'law of correlation and concomitance in occurrence' (qānūn al-talāzum wa al-takhalluf). This principle involves establishing necessary connections between propositions based on their co-occurrence or mutual exclusion.
Ibn Taymiyya uses this to construct proofs without relying on the middle term of categorical syllogisms.
3
Analyze the second method: distinction of names
The second method involves a precise differentiation between mushakkik (equivocal) and mutawati' (univocal) names, as well as between general univocal and specific univocal. This linguistic analysis is crucial for avoiding fallacies and clarifying definitions.
This method connects logic directly to Arabic linguistic theory.
4
Connect to Ibn Taymiyya's overall aim
The passage concludes that these methods realize Ibn Taymiyya's idea that Muslims have unique methods of inquiry derived from the Qur'an. Reflect on how these methods are more flexible and context-sensitive than Aristotelian forms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بهذا ينتهي الجانب الموجب من نقد ابن تيمية للقياس الأرسططاليسي ونستطيع الآن أن نلمس في هذا النقد طرافة وابتكاراً . فهو لم يقف عند حد نقد الشكاك . بل وضع هو أيضاً نقوداً عدة ، صدر فيها عن فكر خاص ، بحيث يبدو أثر الشكاك في هذا الفصل ضئيلاً باهتاً . حقاً إنه تأثر بهم من ناحية وبالرواقية من ناحية في نقده الطويل للقضية الكلية ، وخاصة حين حاول أن يطبق البرهان على مباحث الوجود . ودخل بهذا في أبحاث ميتافيزيقية كالوجود وأقسامه والماهيات ووجودها ، فأنكر الكليات ولم يستبق إلى الجزئيات المحسوسة . ولكن لا نجد شيئاً من نقده لاقتصار البرهان على مقدمتين أو لحصر صور الإستدلال في ثلاثة في التراث الهليني . أما ارتباط عناصر هذا النقد التفصيلي بنقده العام للمنطق فارتباط وثيق . وفي إيجاز يقدم لنا ابن تيمية في هذا الفصل مبحثاً كاملاً متفق العناصر ، متناسق الأجزاء . محاولاً أن يثبت به أن المنطق ليس مخالفاً فحسب لصحيح المنقول بل لصريح المعقول . أما عن الطرق العقلية التي وضعها لكي يستخدمها النظار ، فقد رأينا أن كلاً منهما يقوم على فكرة من أعمق الأفكار المنطقية . فالطريق الأول يعود كما قلنا إلى قانون التلازم والتخلف في الوقوع . والطريق الثاني يعود إلى فهم وتفريق دقيق بين الأسماء المشككة والمتواطئة أو بين المتواطئة العامة والمتواطئة الخاصة . وبهذا حقق ابن تيمية فكرته القائلة بأن للمسلمين طرقاً خاصة في البحث ، مستمدة من القرآن وقائمة عليه . وأخيراً ترك ابن تيمية بما كتبه عن المنطق تراثاً علمياً لا يقدر . (۱) ابن تيمية : الرد ص ١٥٤ إلى ١٥٧ - ٣٥٠ .
Thus ends the positive aspect of Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the Aristotelian syllogism. We can now perceive in this critique novelty and innovation. He did not stop at the level of the skeptics' critique. Rather, he himself advanced several critiques, proceeding from a unique thought, such that the influence of the skeptics in this chapter appears faint and faded. Indeed, he was influenced by them on one hand and by the Stoics on the other in his lengthy critique of the universal proposition, especially when he attempted to apply demonstration to topics of existence. He thereby entered into metaphysical discussions such as existence and its divisions, essences and their existence, denying universals and not confining himself to sensible particulars. But we do not find anything of his critique concerning the restriction of demonstration to two premises or the limitation of inferential forms to three in the Hellenic heritage. Rather, the connection of the elements of this detailed critique to his general critique of logic is strong. In summary, Ibn Taymiyya presents in this chapter a complete, coherent, and consistent investigation, attempting to prove thereby that logic is not only contrary to sound transmitted knowledge but also to clear reason. As for the rational methods he formulated for the use of theorists, we have seen that each of them is based on one of the deepest logical ideas. The first method returns, as we said, to the law of correlation and concomitance in occurrence. The second method returns to a precise understanding and differentiation between mushakkik and mutawati' names, or between general mutawati' and specific mutawati'. By this, Ibn Taymiyya realized his idea that Muslims have unique methods of inquiry, derived from the Qur'an and founded upon it. Finally, Ibn Taymiyya left behind, through his writings on logic, an invaluable scholarly legacy. (1) Ibn Taymiyya: al-Radd, pp. 154-157 - 350.
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage concludes the discussion of Ibn Taymiyya's critique by highlighting its positive and innovative aspects. The author notes that Ibn Taymiyya went beyond the skeptics, influenced by Stoic logic in his critique of universal propositions, but he did not attack the number of premises or figures in Aristotelian demonstration. His detailed critique is coherent. Two rational methods are summarized: the first based on the principle of talazum (entailment) and the second on the distinction between mushakkik and mutawati' names. These methods demonstrate Ibn Taymiyya's claim that Muslims have a unique logical methodology rooted in the Qur'an.

الأسماء المشككة
الأسماء المتواطئة

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's constructive alternative to Aristotelian logic consists of two Qur'an-based rational methods: the law of talazum and the precise analysis of equivocal versus univocal names.

Two Paths to Knowledge: Logical vs Intuitive Method
Author contrasts logical method with Sufi intuitive method

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the logical method (al-manhaj al-mantiqi) from the intuitive method (al-manhaj al-dhawqi) as described in the source passage.

Core Idea
Reason and spiritual taste are two distinct paths to truth—one discursive, the other experiential.

This lesson introduces you to two fundamentally different approaches to knowledge that existed in classical Islamic thought: the rational, logical method of theologians and philosophers, and the intuitive, experiential method of Sufis. The source passage contrasts them and explains how each arrived at its claims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two methods named
Find the Arabic phrases for 'logical method' (المنهج المنطقي) and 'intuitive method' (المنهج الذوقي) in the passage. Note how they are introduced as contrasting.
2
Extract the description of each
What does the passage say about how each method operates? For the logical method, it is based on 'aql (reason). For the intuitive method, it involves Qur'anic recitation, worship, seclusion, and culminates in dhawq (taste) and hads (intuition).
3
Compare the epistemic foundations
One uses reason and rational inference; the other uses direct experiential knowledge. The passage highlights the 'vast gap' between them. How does this contrast reflect different views on what counts as knowledge?
4
Locate the historical turning point
The passage mentions al-Ghazali's merging of logic with Islamic sciences and the jurists' resistance. This sets the stage for the Sufis' alternative. Note that the Sufis rejected all forms of logical thought, whether Greek or Islamic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفَصْلُ الأوّل
المنهج المنطقي والمنهج الذوقي
ذكرنا في الأبواب السابقة موقف علماء المسلمين من المنطق الأرسططاليسي ووصلنا إلى أن هؤلاء المسلمين لم يأخذوا بهذا المنطق من ناحية ، ووضعوا منطقاً آخر يخالفه من ناحية أخرى . واستمر موقفهم هذا حتى نهاية القرن الخامس . وفي نهاية هذا القرن مزج الغزالي المنطق بعلوم المسلمين . ولكن الفقهاء لم يوافقوا على هذه الحركة ، وقاوموها مقاومة شديدة . وسنحاول في هذا الفصل أن نبين منهج مفكرين آخرين عاشوا في العالم الإسلامي ولم يقبلوا شيئاً من صور التفكير المنطقية التي ذكرناها يونانية كانت أو إسلامية وهم الصوفية .
يتبين للباحث منذ بدء تكون الصوفية ، البون الشاسع بين منهجهم في المعرفة ، ومنهج المتكلمين والمناطقة ، فبينما كان مناهج هؤلاء الأخيرين يستند على العقل ، قدم الصوفية منهجاً جديداً ، أسموه أولاً ـ الاستنباط القرآني ، أن يردد الصوفي القرآن ، مستغرقاً فيه ، حتى تنفتح له المعاني الإلهية . ثم ينتقل إلى معاناة العبادة ، والخلوة والتردد بين المقامات الصوفية والأحوال حتى تنقدح المعرفة الإلهية فيه أو تلقى فيه ، فيتذوقها فإذا كان منهج المتكلمين والفلاسفة هو العقل ، فقد انتهى موقف الصوفية إلى الذوق والحدس ، ولسنا نخوض في هذين الطريقين ، إن البحث يكون أدخل في نطاق نظرية المعرفة الإبستمولوجيا ـ وإنما نبحث فيه من ناحية تأديته للصوفية
The First Chapter: The Logical Method and the Intuitive Method. In the previous sections we mentioned the stance of Muslim scholars regarding Aristotelian logic, and we concluded that these Muslims, on the one hand, did not adopt this logic, and on the other hand, they established another logic contrary to it. Their position continued until the end of the fifth century. At the end of this century, al-Ghazali merged logic with the Islamic sciences. But the jurists did not approve of this movement and resisted it strongly. In this chapter we will attempt to explain the method of other thinkers who lived in the Islamic world and did not accept any of the forms of logical thought we mentioned, whether Greek or Islamic, namely the Sufis. It becomes clear to the researcher, from the beginning of the formation of Sufism, the vast gap between their method of knowledge and that of the theologians and logicians. While the methods of the latter relied on reason, the Sufis presented a new method, which they first called Qur'anic deduction: the Sufi recites the Qur'an, absorbed in it, until divine meanings open to him. Then he moves to the practice of worship, seclusion, and oscillation between Sufi stations and states until divine knowledge is kindled in him or cast into him, so he tastes it. If the method of the theologians and philosophers is reason, then the position of the Sufis ended with taste and intuition. We do not delve into these two paths; that research is more within the scope of epistemology. Rather, we investigate it from the perspective of its delivery to the Sufis.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage is an introductory section from a chapter that contrasts the logical method (المنهج المنطقي) with the intuitive method (المنهج الذوقي). The author begins by summarizing earlier discussions about Muslim scholars' attitudes toward Aristotelian logic: they rejected it and formulated an alternative logic, a stance that persisted until the end of the fifth Islamic century. Then al-Ghazali attempted to integrate logic with Islamic sciences, which met with strong resistance from jurists. The focus of this chapter, however, is on a different group: the Sufis, who rejected all forms of logical reasoning, whether Greek or Islamic. The author points out the fundamental difference between the rational methods of theologians and logicians and the Sufi approach. The Sufis first used 'Qur'anic deduction' (الاستنباط القرآني), which involves deep absorption in recitation until divine meanings are revealed. Then they engage in worship, seclusion, and moving through spiritual stations until knowledge is directly experienced or 'tasted' (الذوق). Ultimately, the Sufi method relies on taste and intuition (الحدس) rather than reason. The author clarifies that the chapter does not fully explore these epistemological questions but rather examines how this method delivers the Sufis to their goals.

Key Terms
المنهج المنطقي
المنهج الذوقي
الاستنباط القرآني
الذوق
الحدس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what did the Sufis replace rational methods with?
A) Aristotelian syllogisms
B) Qur'anic deduction and taste/intuition
C) Juridical analogy (qiyas)
D) Philosophical demonstration (burhan)

Key Takeaway
The logical method relies on reason and discursive inference, while the intuitive method relies on Qur'anic immersion, spiritual practice, and direct taste—two distinct epistemological approaches in Islam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Based on the passage, list three characteristics of the logical method and three characteristics of the intuitive method. Use the Arabic terms where possible.
?
Find one example from Islamic history where a scholar argued that rational proof is superior to spiritual experience, and one where a Sufi argued the opposite. (You may use general knowledge of Islamic thought.)

Method of Learning

The Sufi Method: From Qur'anic Recitation to Spiritual Taste
Author contrasts logical method with Sufi intuitive method

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the three-step process of the Sufi method as outlined in the passage: istinbat qur'ani, dhawq, and hads.

Core Idea
The Sufi path to knowledge begins with Qur'anic immersion, passes through spiritual practice, and culminates in direct tasting.

The source passage outlines a distinctive method that the Sufis proposed as an alternative to rational logic. It starts with deep recitation of the Qur'an, moves to worship and seclusion, and ends with the direct experience of divine truths—what they call 'taste' and 'intuition'. This lesson unpacks each stage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial practice
Find the term 'istinbat qur'ani' (الاستنباط القرآني). What does it involve? The passage says the Sufi recites the Qur'an, absorbed in it, until divine meanings open. This is not mere recitation but a contemplative immersion.
2
Follow the progression
After Qur'anic recitation, the Sufi moves to worship, seclusion, and oscillation between spiritual stations (maqamat) and states (ahwal). This active practice prepares the heart for knowledge.
3
Identify the final stage
The ultimate goal is dhawq (taste) and hads (intuition). These terms denote direct, experiential knowledge that is not attained through reasoning but is 'kindled' or 'cast' into the Sufi.
4
Reflect on the author's stance
The author says the chapter will investigate the method 'from the perspective of its delivery to the Sufis'—meaning they will not evaluate its truth but explain how it functions. Is the author neutral? Does he seem to favor one method?

Source Text

المنهج المنطقي والمنهج الذوقي

Method of Learning
