Al-Ghazali's Classification of Methods of Inquiry
Al-Ghazali classifies methods of inquiry into speculation and taste with sub-branches.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe al-Ghazali's division of inquiry into speculation and taste and identify the four sub-methods associated with each branch.

Core Idea
Al-Ghazali divides all inquiry into speculation and taste, each with philosophical and theological branches.

In this passage, al-Ghazali presents a sweeping classification of all methods of inquiry available to scholars. He reduces them to two fundamental paths: rational speculation (naẓar) and direct spiritual taste (dhawq). Each path then splits into two, giving us four distinct approaches: the Peripatetic philosophers, the theologians (mutakallimun), the Illuminationists (ishrāqiyun), and the Sufis. Understanding this taxonomy helps us position different Islamic intellectual traditions in relation to one another.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary division
The passage divides all investigation into two categories: speculation (naẓar) and taste (dhawq). Note that these are mutually exclusive and exhaustive according to al-Ghazali.
2
List the sub-branches of speculation
Speculation is further divided into the method of the Peripatetic philosophers (Greek logic) and the method of the theologians (Islamic logic). Identify the source books for each.
3
List the sub-branches of taste
Taste is divided into the method of the Illuminationist philosophers and the method of the Sufis. Note their respective guarantors (books).
4
Trace the historical reference
The commentator notes that this classification is a precise history of research methods among Muslims. The author of Abjad al-Ulum confirms this division. How does this relate to the distinction between Greek logic and Islamic logic?
5
Apply the classification
Give an example: A mutakallim like al-Ash'ari uses the method of speculation via theology, while a Sufi like al-Hallaj uses the method of taste.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تقسيم الغزالي فيرى أن هذا البحث لا يخلو إما أن يكون على طريق النظر أو على طريق الذوق . فالأول إما على قانون فلاسفة المشائين ، فالمتكفل له كتب الحكمة ، أي أننا نعرفه من كتب الفلاسفة أو على قانون المتكلمين . والثاني إما على قانون فلاسفة الإشراقيين ، فالمتكفل له حكمة الإشراق ونحوه(۱) أو على قانون الصوفية واصطلاحهم والمتكفل له لكتب التصوف . وهذا في الواقع تأريخ دقيق لطرق البحث عند المسلمين . فهذه الطرق يقسمها صاحب أبجد العلوم إلى قسمين طريق النظر وطريق الذوق . وينقسم طريق النظر إلى قسمين - طريق الفلاسفة أي المنطق اليوناني - وطريق المتكلمين أي المنطق الإسلامي . وينقسم الطريق الأخير إلى قسمين : طريق الإشراق ، وطريق التصوف .
Al-Ghazali's division states that this investigation is either by way of speculation or by way of taste. The first is either according to the method of the Peripatetic philosophers, so the guarantor for it is the books of wisdom, i.e., we know it from the books of philosophers, or according to the method of the theologians. The second is either according to the method of the Illuminationist philosophers, so the guarantor for it is the philosophy of illumination and similar (1), or according to the method of the Sufis and their terminology, and the guarantor for it is the books of Sufism. This is in reality a precise history of the methods of research among the Muslims. So these methods are divided by the author of Abjad al-'Ulum into two parts: the way of speculation and the way of taste. The way of speculation is divided into two parts: the way of the philosophers, i.e., Greek logic, and the way of the theologians, i.e., Islamic logic. The last path is divided into two parts: the way of illumination and the way of Sufism.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage presents al-Ghazali's classification of methods of inquiry. It begins by stating that every investigation falls into one of two categories: speculation (naẓar) or taste (dhawq). The speculative path is further divided into two sub-paths: the method of the Peripatetic philosophers (flāsifat al-mashshā'iyīn), whose books are the source, and the method of the theologians (mutakallimīn). The taste path is also divided: the method of the Illuminationist philosophers (flāsifat al-ishrāqiyīn), whose source is the philosophy of illumination, and the method of the Sufis (ṣūfiyya), whose source is the books of Sufism. The author then notes that this division is a precise historical account of research methods among Muslims, referencing the classification by the author of Abjad al-'Ulūm. The way of speculation is identified with Greek logic (manṭiq yūnānī) and the way of the theologians with Islamic logic (manṭiq islāmī). The taste path branches into illumination and Sufism. This classification sets the stage for later discussions on the relationship between these methods.

Key Terms
طريق النظر
طريق الذوق
فلاسفة المشائين
طريق المتكلمين
طريق الإشراق
طريق التصوف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Al-Ghazali's division of inquiry into naẓar and dhawq provides a foundational taxonomy for understanding Islamic epistemological methods.

Method of Learning

The Two Paths: Inference and Witnessing
Tashköprüzade on two ways: inference and witnessing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two paths (inference and witnessing) and explain how they can converge, as described by Tashköprüzade.

Core Idea
Knowledge can be reached either by rational inference or divine witness; the perfect seeker combines both.

Tashköprüzade opens a chapter on the relationship between speculation and purification, presenting two fundamental paths to truth. The first is the way of inference (istidlāl), the path of the deeply-rooted scholars; the second is the way of witnessing (mushāhada), the path of the truthful. Importantly, these paths can merge, producing a seeker who unites both inference and witnessing, knowledge and gnosis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two paths
Locate the Arabic phrases for 'way of inference' and 'way of witnessing'. Note the ranks associated with each (scholars vs. truthful).
The path of inference is rational deduction; witnessing is spiritual intuition.
2
Analyze the convergence
Tashköprüzade says each path may lead to the other, so the practitioner becomes 'a collection of the two seas'. Identify the dualities used: inference/witnessing, knowledge/gnosis, testimony/unseen.
This shows that the two ways are not mutually exclusive but complementary.
3
Distinguish the two types of seekers
The text describes two types: (a) those who start from knowledge/witness (speculation) and reach gnosis; (b) those who start from the unseen (divine attraction) and then reach intellectual wisdom.
The second type begins with taste (dhawq) and ends with intellectual wisdom.
4
Apply the concept to an Islamic scholar
Identify a scholar (e.g., al-Ghazali) who combined both paths. Explain how his works (e.g., Ihya' 'Ulum al-Din) display both rational argument and spiritual insight.
This helps situate the theoretical distinction in a concrete example.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقرر طاش كبري زادة فصلاً في « النسبة بين طريق النظر وطريق التصفية » ويقرر أن هناك طريقين - الأول منهما طريق الإستدلال - والثاني طريق المشاهدة (٢) . والأول درجة العلماء الراسخين والثاني درجة الصديقين . ثم إن كلا من الطريقين قد ينتهي إلى الآخر ، فيكون صاحبه مجمعاً للبحرين ، أي يجمع بين الاستدلال والمشاهدة ، أو العلم والعرفان ، أو الشهادة والغيب . فالسالكون إلى الحق إذن مع كثرة الطرق وتعددها نوعان : أحدهما ما يبتدىء من طريق العلم إلى العرفان ، ومن طريق الشهادة إلى الغيب . وثانيهما - ما ينجلي الحق له بالجذبة الإلهية ، فيبتدىء من الغيب ، ثم ينكشف له عالم الشهادة ، أي يبدأ بالذوق وينتهي إلى الحكمة العقلية . ثم يقرر طاش كبري زادة أن العلوم منحصرة في أن للأشياء وجوداً في أربع مراتب - في الأعيان وفي الأذهان وفي العبارة والكتابة « أما العلوم المتعلقة في الأول من حيث حالها في نفس الأمر هي العلوم الحقيقية التي لا تتبدل باختلاف الأزمان وتجدد الملل والأديان » أي هي العلم الإلهي وهذه تسمى علوماً حكمية إن جرى الباحث عن أحوالها فيها على مقتضى عقله . وعلوماً شرعية ان بحث فيها على قانون الإسلام أي هي الفلسفة وعلم الكلام والعلوم المتعلقة بالثانية هي العلوم الآلية المعنوية كالمنطق ونحوه ، والعلوم المتعلقة بالأخيرين هي العلوم الآلية اللفظية أو الخطية ، وهذه هي العلوم المعتبرة في ديننا هذا لورود شريعتنا على لسان العرب وعلى كتابه ، ثم إن الثلاثة الأخيرة من هذه الأنواع لا سبيل إلى تحصيلها إلا الكسب بالنظر . وأما النوع الأول أي العلم الإلهي فقد يتحصل بالنظر ، وقد يتحصل بالتصفية ، فهذا التقسيم إذن يعود في آخر الأمر إلى تقسيم الطريق الى نظرية وصوفية أو ذوقية .
And Tashköprüzade devotes a chapter on 'the relationship between the way of speculation and the way of purification' and states that there are two ways: the first of them is the way of inference, and the second is the way of witnessing (2). The first is the rank of deeply-rooted scholars, and the second is the rank of the truthful. Then each of the two ways may lead to the other, so its practitioner becomes a collection of the two seas, meaning he combines inference and witnessing, or knowledge and gnosis, or testimony and the unseen. So the seekers of truth, despite the multitude and diversity of ways, are two types: one of them begins from the way of knowledge to gnosis, and from the way of testimony to the unseen. The second is he to whom the truth reveals itself by divine attraction, so he begins from the unseen, then the world of testimony is disclosed to him, meaning he begins with taste and ends with intellectual wisdom. Then Tashköprüzade states that the sciences are confined to the fact that things have existence in four ranks: in external objects, in minds, in expression, and in writing. 'As for the sciences related to the first, in terms of their state in themselves, they are the true sciences that do not change with difference of times and renewal of creeds and religions' i.e., they are divine knowledge, and these are called philosophical sciences if the researcher investigates their states according to his intellect, and religious sciences if he investigates them according to Islamic law, i.e., philosophy and theology (kalam). The sciences related to the second are the instrumental conceptual sciences like logic and the like. The sciences related to the latter two are the instrumental verbal or written sciences, and these are the sciences considered in our religion because our law came in the tongue of the Arabs and in His book. Then the last three of these types cannot be acquired except by acquisition through speculation. As for the first type, i.e., divine knowledge, it may be acquired through speculation and may be acquired through purification. So this division ultimately returns to dividing the path into speculative and mystical or taste.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage presents Tashköprüzade's synthesis of the two paths. He introduces two ways: inference (istidlāl) and witnessing (mushāhada). The first is the rank of deeply-rooted scholars (ʿulamāʾ rāsikhīn), the second of the truthful (ṣiddīqīn). Importantly, the two ways can converge, so that a practitioner combines both inference and witnessing, knowledge and gnosis, testimony and the unseen. Seekers of truth are of two types: those who begin with knowledge (speculation) and reach gnosis, and those who begin with the unseen (divine attraction) and then reach intellectual wisdom. Tashköprüzade then presents a classification of sciences based on four existential ranks: external objects (aʿyān), minds (adhān), expression (ʿibāra), and writing (kitāba). The sciences related to the first rank are the true sciences (al-ʿulūm al-ḥaqīqiyya) that do not change; they are divine knowledge (al-ʿilm al-ilāhī). These are called philosophical sciences (ʿulūm ḥikmiyya) if investigated by intellect, and religious sciences (ʿulūm sharʿiyya) if investigated according to Islamic law (including philosophy and kalām). The sciences related to the second rank are instrumental conceptual sciences (al-ʿulūm al-āliyya al-maʿnawiyya) like logic. The sciences related to the last two ranks are instrumental verbal/written sciences (al-ʿulūm al-āliyya al-lafẓiyya aw al-khaṭṭiyya). The latter three types (instrumental and religious/philosophical?) are acquired only through speculation (kashf bi al-naẓar), while divine knowledge can be acquired either through speculation or through purification. Thus the division ultimately returns to the speculative versus mystical/taste dichotomy.

Key Terms
طريق الاستدلال
طريق المشاهدة
الصديقين
الجذبة الإلهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They are mutually exclusive and cannot be combined.
B) Each may lead to the other, so a practitioner can combine both.
C) Witnessing is superior and makes inference obsolete.
D) Inference is only for beginners, witnessing for advanced.

Key Takeaway
Tashköprüzade presents inference and witnessing as complementary paths that can be united in the same seeker, reflecting a synthesis of reason and spiritual experience.

The Four Existential Ranks of Sciences
Tashköprüzade's four existential ranks classification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given science according to Tashköprüzade's four ranks of existence (external objects, minds, expression, writing).

Core Idea
Every science corresponds to one of four existential orders: external, mental, verbal, or written.

Tashköprüzade provides a metaphysical classification of sciences based on the levels at which things exist. He asserts that everything has existence in four ranks: in external objects (a'yān), in minds (adhān), in expression (ʿibāra), and in writing (kitāba). Sciences are then grouped according to which rank they primarily concern. This framework is foundational for understanding the organization of Islamic knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Name the four ranks
From the Arabic, list the four ranks: في الأعيان (in external objects), في الأذهان (in minds), في العبارة (in expression/utterance), في الكتابة (in writing).
These are ascending degrees of abstraction: from concrete objects to mental concepts to spoken words to written symbols.
2
Match sciences to ranks
Identify which sciences belong to each rank according to Tashköprüzade: true sciences (divine knowledge) related to the first rank; instrumental conceptual sciences (e.g., logic) to the second; instrumental verbal/written sciences to the last two.
The passage explicitly assigns the 'first' rank to true sciences, the 'second' to instrumental conceptual, and the 'last two' to verbal/written.
3
Apply the schema to a specific science
Take a science like fiqh (jurisprudence) and determine its primary rank. Fiqh deals with rulings that exist in minds (as abstract legal norms), expressed in speech (fatwas, books), and written in texts. However, its ultimate subject (Allah's law) is in the first rank? Consider the subtlety.
Encourage nuanced application: some sciences may span multiple ranks but have a primary focus.
4
Contrast with other classification systems
Compare Tashköprüzade's four ranks to al-Farabi's enumeration of sciences or Ibn Sina's division. Notice that Tashköprüzade adds the dimension of existence levels, not just subject matter.
This shows the novelty of Tashköprüzade's approach.

Acquisition of True Sciences: Speculation and Purification
Tashköprüzade on acquisition methods: speculation vs. purification for divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why divine knowledge can be acquired through both speculation and purification, while other sciences require only speculation, according to Tashköprüzade.

Core Idea
Divine knowledge alone is accessible through both rational inquiry and spiritual purification; all other sciences depend solely on speculative effort.

After classifying the sciences by existential ranks, Tashköprüzade addresses the method of acquisition. He states that the last three types of sciences (instrumental and the true sciences when investigated by reason or revelation) can only be acquired through speculative effort (al-kasb bi al-naẓar). However, divine knowledge (al-ʿilm al-ilāhī) is unique: it can be obtained either through speculation or through spiritual purification (taṣfiya). This observation ultimately leads back to his earlier division of the path into speculative and mystical/taste (dhawqī).

Guided Path
1
Identify which sciences require only speculation
In the passage, locate the phrase 'لا سبيل إلى تحصيلها إلا الكسب بالنظر' (cannot be acquired except by acquisition through speculation). This applies to the three types: instrumental conceptual, instrumental verbal/written, and the true sciences when studied as philosophical/religious sciences.
These include logic, grammar, calligraphy, philosophy, and kalām as traditionally taught.
2
Note the exception for divine knowledge
The text says 'أما النوع الأول أي العلم الإلهي فقد يتحصل بالنظر، وقد يتحصل بالتصفية' (the first type, i.e., divine knowledge, may be acquired through speculation and may be acquired through purification).
Divine knowledge is al-ʿilm al-ilāhī, the knowledge of God and ultimate realities.
3
Relate back to the two paths
Tashköprüzade concludes that this classification returns to the division of the path into speculative and mystical/taste. Connect the method of speculation (naẓar) to the way of inference, and purification (taṣfiya) to the way of witnessing.
This shows the unity of his overall argument: the paths are reflected in the acquisition methods of the highest science.
4
Apply to a concrete example
Take a specific issue in theology, such as knowledge of God's attributes. The speculative path uses rational arguments (like the kalām proofs). The purification path involves spiritual practices (dhikr, contemplation) leading to direct unveiling. Identify which method a particular scholar (e.g., Ibn 'Arabi vs. al-Ash'ari) emphasizes.
This demonstrates that both approaches are legitimate within the Islamic tradition.

Classification of Sciences and Two Epistemological Paths
Author classifies sciences and introduces two paths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the sciences according to the author's scheme and identify the two epistemological paths to divine science.

Core Idea
Divine science can be reached by reason or purification, creating two conflicting paths.

The author begins by categorizing the Islamic sciences into four types. Three of these—philosophy, theology, and the instrumental sciences—can only be acquired through rational inquiry (nazar). But the highest science, divine science ('ilm ilahi), has two possible avenues: rational inquiry or spiritual purification (tasfiya). This bifurcation sets up a fundamental epistemological divide between the rationalists and the Sufis, each rejecting the other's method.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four types of sciences
From the passage, list the four categories: legal sciences investigated according to Islam (philosophy and theology), instrumental conceptual sciences (logic), instrumental verbal/written sciences, and divine science.
The first three are grouped as 'earned through nazar', while the fourth has two paths.
2
Group the sciences by method of acquisition
Which sciences can only be obtained by nazar? Which can be obtained either by nazar or tasfiya? Note that the author says the first type (divine science) can be obtained by either.
3
Recognize the two general epistemological paths
The division of sciences ultimately reduces to two paths: the rational path (nazariyya) and the mystical/intuitive path (sufiyya or dhawqiyya). The two paths are mutually exclusive in the eyes of their adherents.
Each side denies the validity of the other's method.
4
Understand the implications for knowledge claims
This classification is not just taxonomy; it determines what counts as valid knowledge. Rationalists accept only knowledge gained through nazar; Sufis accept only knowledge from tasfiya.
This sets up the debate detailed in the rest of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلوماً شرعية ان بحث فيها على قانون الإسلام أي هي الفلسفة وعلم الكلام والعلوم المتعلقة بالثانية هي العلوم الآلية المعنوية كالمنطق ونحوه ، والعلوم المتعلقة بالأخيرين هي العلوم الآلية اللفظية أو الخطية ، وهذه هي العلوم المعتبرة في ديننا هذا لورود شريعتنا على لسان العرب وعلى كتابه ، ثم إن الثلاثة الأخيرة من هذه الأنواع لا سبيل إلى تحصيلها إلا الكسب بالنظر . وأما النوع الأول أي العلم الإلهي فقد يتحصل بالنظر ، وقد يتحصل بالتصفية ، فهذا التقسيم إذن يعود في آخر الأمر إلى تقسيم الطريق الى نظرية وصوفية أو ذوقية . ولم يقبل الصوفية الطريق الأول وأنكروا كل الطرق التي تقوم على النظر ، كما أن أهل النظر أنكروا طريق الصوفية . لأن طريق التصفية صعب الوصول ، لأن محو العلائق كما يتطلب التصوف إلى حد يؤدي إلى انكشاف المعارف متعذر ، بل قريب من الممتنع ، وان أفضى الى القصد ، أي إلى علم فثباته أبعد منه . ذلك لأنه تجربة ذاتية لا تقوم على قانون عام مسلم به « ثم إن أدنى وسواس وخاطر يمحو ما حصل ، ويقطع ما وصل »(۱) وبهذا يفسرون نتائج التصوف تفسيراً مرضياً بحتاً patholopique (وهو رأي يذهب إليه بعض علماء النفس المحدثين ) فيقولون « إنه قد يفسد المزاج ويختلط العقل في أثناء تلك المجاهدات الصعبة والرياضات الشاقة » . ويرد الصوفية على هذا بأن العلوم الحاصلة بالنظر مشوبة بأحكام الوهم ، وغير خالصة من عمل الخيال ، وأن المعتزلة تلجأ في أحكامها إلى قياس الغائب على الشاهد ، وتنتهي أقيستهم هذه إلى أخطاء شنيعة وضلال ظاهر . وعلى العكس من هذا طريق التصوف ، إنه تصفية روحية والفيض من بين العلوم الحقيقية هو الطريق الوحيد للعلم وبواسطته تنكشف علوم إلهية ومعارف ربانية «ويرد عليهم وارد إلهام هو حديث عهد بربه»(۲) وأما أن طريق التصوف صعب الوصول فلا يقدح في قوة اليقين وصحة العلم . . . أما التفسير المرضي للتصوف باعتباره إختلالاً في المزاج ، فإن المتصوفة قلما يحدث لهم هذا لأنهم كما أنهم أطباء النفوس والأرواح ، كذلك عارفون بأحوال البدن والأشباح ، فالرياضة على ما شرطوه من الأحوال ، أمان من العناء والإختلال » . أما ما يحدث لبعض المتصوفة من شذوذ أو اضطراب عقلي ، فإنه نشأ عن شذوذ واضطراب أصلي في النفوس قبل البدء في الطريق .
And [the] legal sciences if investigated according to the law of Islam, i.e., philosophy and theology, and the sciences related to the second are the instrumental conceptual sciences like logic and its like, and the sciences related to the last two are the instrumental verbal or written sciences, and these are the sciences considered in our religion because our Sharia came in the tongue of the Arabs and in His book. Then the last three of these types can only be acquired by earning through rational inquiry. As for the first type, i.e., divine science, it may be attained by rational inquiry and may be attained by purification. This division thus ultimately returns to dividing the path into rational and mystical or intuitive. The Sufis did not accept the first path and denied all paths based on rational inquiry, just as the rationalists denied the Sufi path. Because the path of purification is difficult to reach, for erasing attachments as required by Sufism to the point of leading to the disclosure of knowledge is impossible, rather close to impossible, and if it leads to the goal, i.e., to knowledge, then its stability is more distant. That is because it is a subjective experience not based on a general accepted law. 'Then the slightest whisper and thought erases what was obtained and cuts what was reached.' And by this they explain the results of Sufism purely pathologically (a view held by some modern psychologists). They say: 'It may spoil the temperament and confuse the mind during those difficult strivings and strenuous exercises.' The Sufis reply to this that the sciences obtained by rational inquiry are mixed with the judgments of illusion and not free from the work of imagination, and that the Mu'tazilites resort in their judgments to analogizing the unseen based on the seen, and their analogies end in monstrous errors and manifest misguidance. On the contrary, the path of Sufism is spiritual purification, and effusion among the true sciences is the only path to knowledge, and through it divine sciences and lordly cognitions are disclosed. 'A divine inspiration that is a recent covenant with its Lord comes upon them.' As for the fact that the path of Sufism is difficult to reach, this does not detract from the strength of certainty or the correctness of knowledge. As for the pathological explanation of Sufism as a disorder of temperament, the Sufis rarely experience this because they, as they are physicians of souls and spirits, are also knowledgeable about the conditions of bodies and physical forms. Spiritual exercise, according to the conditions they stipulate, is a guarantee against hardship and disorder. As for what happens to some Sufis in terms of abnormality or mental disturbance, it arises from an original abnormality and disturbance in souls before beginning the path.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological comparison and critique between two methodological approaches to knowledge: the rationalist path (nazar) and the mystical path (tasawwuf/tasfiya). The author begins by classifying sciences: the last three types (presumably logic, language sciences, and written sciences) can only be acquired through rational inquiry (kasb bi-nazar). The first type, divine science ('ilm ilahi), can be obtained either by rational inquiry or by purification (tasfiya). This bifurcation leads to two general paths: theoretical/rational and mystical/intuitive. The Sufis reject the rationalist path, and the rationalists reject the Sufi path. The rationalists argue that the Sufi path is extremely difficult: erasing attachments is nearly impossible, and even if knowledge is attained, it is unstable because it is a subjective experience with no universal law. They cite that a mere whisper can erase what was gained, and they provide a pathological explanation: strenuous spiritual exercises may cause mental imbalance. The Sufis counter that rationalist knowledge is tainted by illusion and imagination; they specifically criticize the Mu'tazilites for applying analogy from the witnessed to the unseen, leading to errors. In contrast, the Sufi path is pure spiritual purification and direct effusion from God, yielding true divine sciences. The objection that the path is difficult does not affect the certainty of its results; the difficulty does not undermine the correctness. As for the rationalists' pathological explanation, the Sufis respond that they themselves are physicians of souls and spirits, and their regulated spiritual exercises are a safeguard against disorder. Any mental disturbance occurring to some Sufis is due to pre-existing disturbance in their souls before embarking on the path.

Key Terms
نظر
تصفية
علم إلهي

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following sciences can ONLY be acquired through rational inquiry (nazar)?
A) Divine science ('ilm ilahi)
B) Philosophy and theology
C) Instrumental sciences (logic, etc.)
D) Both B and C

Key Takeaway
The classification of sciences into those requiring nazar and those admitting tasfiya reveals a fundamental epistemological divide between rational and mystical paths.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using the author's classification, categorize the following sciences: 'ilm al-kalam (theology), 'ilm al-manṭiq (logic), 'ilm al-taṣawwuf (Sufism). Which type does each belong to, and which path (nazar, tasfiya, or both) leads to it according to the author?

Method of Learning
advanced
7 min
Rationalist Critique of Sufism and the Sufi Rebuttal
Author details mutual critiques between rationalists and sufis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the rationalist critique of the Sufi path and the Sufi response regarding the nature and stability of knowledge.

Core Idea
Rationalists: Sufi knowledge is subjective and unstable; Sufis: rational knowledge is tainted by imagination.

After establishing the two paths, the author presents the mutual critiques. The rationalists argue that the Sufi path is nearly impossible—it requires erasing all attachments, and even if knowledge is attained, it is fleeting and subjective. They even offer a pathological explanation, claiming spiritual exercises can cause mental disturbance. The Sufis counter that rational inquiry is never pure: it is mixed with illusions (wahm) and imagination (khayal). They specifically target the Mu‘tazilites, who rely on analogizing the unseen from the seen (qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid), leading to errors. In contrast, the Sufi path yields directly inspired divine knowledge that is certain.

Guided Path
1
Extract the rationalist critique of tasfiya
Find the three rationalist objections: (1) The path is extremely difficult, erasing attachments is nearly impossible. (2) Even if knowledge is obtained, it is unstable—any whisper can erase it. (3) The strenuous exercises can cause mental imbalance (pathological explanation).
The rationalists argue from accessibility and reliability.
2
Identify the Sufi rebuttal regarding rationalist knowledge
The Sufis claim that knowledge from nazar is 'mixed with judgments of illusion and not free from work of imagination.' They give a concrete example: the Mu‘tazilites' use of qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid leads to monstrous errors and manifest misguidance.
This is an epistemological critique: reason alone cannot purify knowledge of subjective influences.
3
Understand the Sufi defense against the stability objection
The Sufis respond that difficulty of attainment does not affect certainty. Regarding the pathological explanation, they argue that true Sufis are physicians of souls and bodies; their regulated exercises are a protection, not a cause of disorder. Any mental disturbance among some Sufis is due to pre-existing abnormality, not the path itself.
The Sufis shift the blame from the method to the practitioner.
4
Map the debate structure
The passage presents a clear give-and-take: rationalist objection, Sufi counter-objection, further rationalist point, Sufi reply. Identify each turn.
This is a classic jadal exchange, though not formalized.

Suhrawardi's Dual Stance on Logic
Author describes Suhrawardi's dual stance and the dhawq origin of Ishraqi logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe Suhrawardi's dual stance on Aristotelian logic and contrast it with the typical Sufi rejection of theoretical thought.

Core Idea
Suhrawardi accepts logic as spiritual exercise yet creates a condensed logic from mystical taste.

Suhrawardi's approach to logic is unique among Sufis. While most Sufis reject theoretical reasoning, Suhrawardi embraces Aristotelian logic as a discipline for Ishraqi mystics. However, he also claims to have derived a new, concise logic through direct mystical taste (dhawq). This lesson unpacks his dual position and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify Suhrawardi's two positions
In the passage, the author states Suhrawardi has a dual position: he accepts logic in general and considers it one of the exercises of Ishraqi mystics, but then he places a second new logic or tries to summarize it. Locate these in the Arabic.
The first position is acceptance, the second is innovation.
2
Compare with other Sufis
Note that the author says Suhrawardi contradicts the Sufis who did not accept any form of theoretical thinking. Understand that this makes Suhrawardi an outlier.
Most Sufis prioritize spiritual experience over rational discourse.
3
Analyze the claim of dhawq
Suhrawardi declares he arrived at this logic by means of dhawq (taste), not through thought. Then he later sought rational proof. Highlight this sequence: mystical origin, then intellectual justification.
The author expresses doubt about this claim, calling it an intellectual attempt nonetheless.
4
Recognize the author's skepticism
The author says 'despite the contradiction in this designation and our doubt about the validity of what Suhrawardi says'. This shows critical engagement with Suhrawardi's narrative.
This skepticism is part of the scholarly tradition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلنا إن للسهروردي تجاه المنطق الأرسططاليسي موقفاً مزدوجاً ـ فهو يقبل المنطق عامة ، ويعتبره إحدى رياضات المتصوفة الإشراقية ثم يضع منطقاً جديداً ثانياً ، أو يحاول أن يختصر هذا المنطق ثانياً . وقلنا إنه بهذا يخالف الصوفية الذين لم يقبلوا أي صورة من صور التفكير النظري . وإن كان السهروردي نفسه يصرح بأنه توصل إلى ابتكار هذا المنطق بواسطة الذوق ، ويسمي كثيراً من أبحاثه الجديدة ضوابط إشراقية . فيقرر أنه في حكمة الإشراق يبدأ على سياق يبتني على الذوق والكشف ومشاهدة الأنوار بخلاف سياق المشائين الذي يبتني على البحث الصرف . ويصف هذا السياق الصوفي بأنه « سياق آخر وطريق أقرب من تلك الطريقة وأنظم وأضبط وأقل أتعاباً في التحصيل »(۱) ويقرر في فقرة أخرى أنه سيجعل الآلة الواقية للفكر ، أي المنطق مختصرة مضبوطة بضوابط قليلة العدد كثيرة الفوائد(۲) . ويشرح الشارح هذا المنطق الإشراقي بأنه موجز حذفت منه الفروع الكثيرة القليلة الاستعمال ، وأن السهروردي بين فيه أشياء كانت في طريقتهم غير محصلة ولا مهذبة ، ويصف السهروردي كيفية حصوله على هذا المنطق الموجز فيقول « لم يحصل لي أولاً بالفكر بل كان حصوله بأمر آخر ـ ثم طلبت الحجة عليه » (١) أي أنه حصل عليه بالذوق ، ثم حاول البرهنة عليه نظرياً . وهذا ما يجعلنا نسميه منطقاً إشراقياً ، بالرغم مما في هذه التسمية من تناقض ومن شكنا في صحة ما يقرره السهروردي أنه قد حصل عليه بالذوق أو بالإشراق . إنه محاولة عقلية بلا شك . وينقسم هذا المنطق الإشراقي عند السهروردي إلى : مبحث التعريف الإشراقي ومبحث القضايا الإشراقية . ومبحث القياس الإشراقي .
We said that Suhrawardi has a dual position towards Aristotelian logic: he accepts logic in general and considers it one of the exercises of Ishraqi mystics, then he places a second new logic, or tries to summarize this logic a second time. And we said that by this he contradicts the Sufis who did not accept any form of theoretical thinking. Although Suhrawardi himself declares that he arrived at the invention of this logic by means of taste (dhawq), and he calls many of his new investigations Ishraqi principles. He states that in the Wisdom of Illumination he begins with a context based on taste, unveiling, and contemplation of lights, unlike the context of the Peripatetics which is based on purely discursive investigation. And he describes this mystical context as 'another context and a path closer than that method, more organized, more precise, and less laborious in acquisition' (1). And he states in another paragraph that he will make the instrument protecting thought, i.e., logic, concise and precise with few principles but many benefits (2). And the commentator explains this Ishraqi logic as an abridgment from which many rarely used branches are removed, and that Suhrawardi clarified in it things that in their method were not attained nor refined. And Suhrawardi describes how he obtained this concise logic, saying: 'It did not come to me first through thought, but its acquisition was through another matter — then I sought the proof for it' (1), i.e., he obtained it through taste, then he tried to prove it theoretically. And this leads us to call it Ishraqi logic, despite the contradiction in this designation and our doubt about the validity of what Suhrawardi says that he obtained it through taste or illumination. It is undoubtedly an intellectual attempt. And this Ishraqi logic of Suhrawardi is divided into: the topic of Ishraqi definition, the topic of Ishraqi propositions, and the topic of Ishraqi syllogism.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage introduces Suhrawardi's approach to logic, highlighting his dual attitude: he accepts Aristotelian logic as a spiritual exercise for Ishraqi mystics, but then creates a new, condensed logic. The author notes that this distinguishes Suhrawardi from other Sufis who reject theoretical thought entirely. Suhrawardi claims that his new logic came through mystical taste (dhawq), not intellectual reasoning, and he later sought to prove it rationally. The commentator explains that this Ishraqi logic is an abridged version, removing rarely used branches and refining concepts. The passage then states the division of this logic into three parts: definition, propositions, and syllogism. The author expresses skepticism about Suhrawardi's claim of dhawq, asserting that it is nonetheless an intellectual endeavor.

Key Terms
المنطق الإشراقي (al-mantiq al-ishraqi): Suhrawardi's condensed, intuitive logic derived from mystical taste (dhawq) as opposed to Aristotelian discursive logic. [Note: No significant shift: term is specific to Suhrawardi.]
الذوق (al-dhawq): Mystical taste or direct intuitive experience, contrasted with rational thought (al-fikr). [Note: In Sufi texts, dhawq is often used similarly; here it is specifically the source of logical principles.]

The Three Branches of Ishraqi Logic
Author describes the division of Ishraqi logic into three topics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list and explain the three main branches of Suhrawardi's Ishraqi logic as described in the passage.

Core Idea
Ishraqi logic is divided into definition, propositions, and syllogism, mirroring Peripatetic structure but with intuitive foundations.

Suhrawardi's Ishraqi logic is not a monolithic discipline; it is structured into three distinct branches: definition, propositions, and syllogism. This division parallels the traditional Peripatetic organization of logic, but Suhrawardi claims each branch is derived from mystical insight rather than pure reason. In this lesson, we explore each branch and its significance within the Ishraqi framework.

Guided Path
1
Locate the three divisions
Read the final sentence of the passage: 'And this Ishraqi logic of Suhrawardi is divided into: the topic of Ishraqi definition, the topic of Ishraqi propositions, and the topic of Ishraqi syllogism.' Identify each part.
The Arabic terms: التعريف الإشراقي, القضايا الإشراقية, القياس الإشراقي.
2
Define each branch
Based on your knowledge of logic, recall that definition (ta'rif) deals with defining terms, propositions (qadaya) deal with statements, and syllogism (qiyas) deals with inference. The passage does not elaborate further, but this is standard.
Suhrawardi's 'Ishraqi' versions likely emphasize intuitive grasp over discursive reasoning.
3
Relate to Peripatetic logic
Note that the same threefold division (definition, proposition, syllogism) exists in Aristotelian logic. The difference is the source: Peripatetic logic relies on rational demonstration, Ishraqi logic on dhawq.
This parallel suggests Suhrawardi is reorganizing, not rejecting, traditional logical topics.
4
Reflect on the structure's purpose
Consider why Suhrawardi kept this structure. It likely provides a familiar framework for students transitioning from Peripatetic to Ishraqi thought, while infusing it with intuitive content.
The commentator mentioned removal of rare branches, implying the structure remains but is streamlined.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلنا إن للسهروردي تجاه المنطق الأرسططاليسي موقفاً مزدوجاً ـ فهو يقبل المنطق عامة ، ويعتبره إحدى رياضات المتصوفة الإشراقية ثم يضع منطقاً جديداً ثانياً ، أو يحاول أن يختصر هذا المنطق ثانياً . وقلنا إنه بهذا يخالف الصوفية الذين لم يقبلوا أي صورة من صور التفكير النظري . وإن كان السهروردي نفسه يصرح بأنه توصل إلى ابتكار هذا المنطق بواسطة الذوق ، ويسمي كثيراً من أبحاثه الجديدة ضوابط إشراقية . فيقرر أنه في حكمة الإشراق يبدأ على سياق يبتني على الذوق والكشف ومشاهدة الأنوار بخلاف سياق المشائين الذي يبتني على البحث الصرف . ويصف هذا السياق الصوفي بأنه « سياق آخر وطريق أقرب من تلك الطريقة وأنظم وأضبط وأقل أتعاباً في التحصيل »(۱) ويقرر في فقرة أخرى أنه سيجعل الآلة الواقية للفكر ، أي المنطق مختصرة مضبوطة بضوابط قليلة العدد كثيرة الفوائد(۲) . ويشرح الشارح هذا المنطق الإشراقي بأنه موجز حذفت منه الفروع الكثيرة القليلة الاستعمال ، وأن السهروردي بين فيه أشياء كانت في طريقتهم غير محصلة ولا مهذبة ، ويصف السهروردي كيفية حصوله على هذا المنطق الموجز فيقول « لم يحصل لي أولاً بالفكر بل كان حصوله بأمر آخر ـ ثم طلبت الحجة عليه » (١) أي أنه حصل عليه بالذوق ، ثم حاول البرهنة عليه نظرياً . وهذا ما يجعلنا نسميه منطقاً إشراقياً ، بالرغم مما في هذه التسمية من تناقض ومن شكنا في صحة ما يقرره السهروردي أنه قد حصل عليه بالذوق أو بالإشراق . إنه محاولة عقلية بلا شك . وينقسم هذا المنطق الإشراقي عند السهروردي إلى : مبحث التعريف الإشراقي ومبحث القضايا الإشراقية . ومبحث القياس الإشراقي .
We said that Suhrawardi has a dual position towards Aristotelian logic: he accepts logic in general and considers it one of the exercises of Ishraqi mystics, then he places a second new logic, or tries to summarize this logic a second time. And we said that by this he contradicts the Sufis who did not accept any form of theoretical thinking. Although Suhrawardi himself declares that he arrived at the invention of this logic by means of taste (dhawq), and he calls many of his new investigations Ishraqi principles. He states that in the Wisdom of Illumination he begins with a context based on taste, unveiling, and contemplation of lights, unlike the context of the Peripatetics which is based on purely discursive investigation. And he describes this mystical context as 'another context and a path closer than that method, more organized, more precise, and less laborious in acquisition' (1). And he states in another paragraph that he will make the instrument protecting thought, i.e., logic, concise and precise with few principles but many benefits (2). And the commentator explains this Ishraqi logic as an abridgment from which many rarely used branches are removed, and that Suhrawardi clarified in it things that in their method were not attained nor refined. And Suhrawardi describes how he obtained this concise logic, saying: 'It did not come to me first through thought, but its acquisition was through another matter — then I sought the proof for it' (1), i.e., he obtained it through taste, then he tried to prove it theoretically. And this leads us to call it Ishraqi logic, despite the contradiction in this designation and our doubt about the validity of what Suhrawardi says that he obtained it through taste or illumination. It is undoubtedly an intellectual attempt. And this Ishraqi logic of Suhrawardi is divided into: the topic of Ishraqi definition, the topic of Ishraqi propositions, and the topic of Ishraqi syllogism.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage introduces Suhrawardi's approach to logic, highlighting his dual attitude: he accepts Aristotelian logic as a spiritual exercise for Ishraqi mystics, but then creates a new, condensed logic. The author notes that this distinguishes Suhrawardi from other Sufis who reject theoretical thought entirely. Suhrawardi claims that his new logic came through mystical taste (dhawq), not intellectual reasoning, and he later sought to prove it rationally. The commentator explains that this Ishraqi logic is an abridged version, removing rarely used branches and refining concepts. The passage then states the division of this logic into three parts: definition, propositions, and syllogism. The author expresses skepticism about Suhrawardi's claim of dhawq, asserting that it is nonetheless an intellectual endeavor.

Key Terms
المنطق الإشراقي (al-mantiq al-ishraqi): Suhrawardi's condensed, intuitive logic derived from mystical taste (dhawq) as opposed to Aristotelian discursive logic. [Note: No significant shift: term is specific to Suhrawardi.]
التعريف الإشراقي (al-ta'rif al-ishraqi): Ishraqi definition, the branch dealing with defining terms through intuitive insight. [Note: Not used elsewhere; specific to this classification.]
القضايا الإشراقية (al-qadaya al-ishraqiyya): Ishraqi propositions, the branch dealing with statements derived from mystical unveiling. [Note: Not used elsewhere; specific to this classification.]
القياس الإشراقي (al-qiyas al-ishraqi): Ishraqi syllogism, the branch dealing with inference based on intuitive knowledge. [Note: Not used elsewhere; specific to this classification.]

Three Migrations of Knowledge
Historical overview of knowledge transmission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three major sources of pre-Islamic scientific knowledge transmitted to the Islamic world and describe the main characteristic of each.

Core Idea
The Islamic world inherited empirical data from India and Persia, and a theoretical framework from Greece.

The author describes how scientific knowledge flowed into the Islamic world through three distinct migrations: Greek, Persian, and Indian. This historical backdrop is essential for understanding how Muslim scholars later developed a creative methodology by synthesizing these traditions. Al-Biruni's comparative work will serve as a key example.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three migrations
In the Arabic passage, find the three migrations mentioned: Greek Alexandrian library to Baghdad, Persian science, and Indian knowledge.
2
Note the main contribution of each
The Greeks contributed a theory of demonstration (burhan) and a logical framework. The Persians and Indians contributed empirical data and specialized sciences (medicine, astronomy, mathematics).
3
Understand the timeline
The first migration began when the medical council moved from Alexandria to Antioch, then to Harran and Marw, finally reaching Baghdad under the Abbasids.
This was accompanied by translation efforts and the establishment of the House of Wisdom.
4
Reflect on the significance for Islamic methodology
The Muslims did not simply copy; they combined the Greek theoretical framework with empirical data from other cultures. This synthesis is the focus of the author's book.

الفَصْلُ الأوّل
انتقال العلم إلى العالم الإسلامي
لسنا نحاول في هذا الفصل تأريخ انتقال العلوم الكيميائية والطبيعية والطبية والرياضية إلى العالم الإسلامي . إنني أؤخر هذا التأريخ لبحث مستقل شامل ، أرجو أن أقوم به قريباً إن شاء الله . ومن المؤكد أن حركة انتقال التراث العلمي قبل الإسلام - وبخاصة التراث اليوناني - إلى العالم الإسلامي قد حظي بعناية جيل من جبابرة الباحثين ، وقد حاولوا توضيح هذا الانتقال طرقه وأساليبه ورجاله . وكان بحث لوكلير عن - تاريخ الطب عند العرب - مثالاً يحتذى في دقة البحث واحاطته بالموضوع ، كما قام براون أيضاً بالكتابة في نفس الموضوع ، ثم تتابعت الأبحاث في مختلف النطاقات ، وفي أقسام العلم الأخرى من طبيعية وكيميائية ورياضية . وبالرغم من دقة ما كتب ، ومعرفة الباحثين المتعددين لمصادر أبحاثهم معرفة تامة ، فان مرور السنين يكشف لنا عن وثائق جديدة تزيد البحث خصوبة وتدعو إلى الكتابة فيه مرة بل مرات أخرى .
أما نحن في هذا الكتاب الذي بين أيدينا فاننا نؤرخ لمنهج المسلمين الإبداعي ، ولا نتعرض لهذا الإنتقال إلا في صورة عامة ، محاولين فقط أن نستخرج منه بعض نماذج المنهج لدى بعض العلماء التطبيقيين .
لقد ذهب كثيرون من مؤرخي العلم ان العلم العربي لم يبدأ ، إلا حين انتقل مجلس التعليم الطبي والعلمي من الاسكندرية في عهد الخليفة الأموي عمر بن عبد العزيز إلى انطاكية ومنها إلى حران ومنها إلى مرو متخذاً رحلة طويلة انتهت إلى بغداد ، ومن ثم بدأت المجامع العلمية في بغداد حركة الترجمة من ناحية ، والبحث العلمي من ناحية أخرى . وقد استدعى خلفاء بني العباس هؤلاء التراجمة إلى قصورهم ، وبني المأمون بعد ذلك بيت الحكمة ؛ وبدأ البحث التجريبي في مختلف العلوم .
نستطيع إذن أن نقول : إن تاريخ العلم عند العرب يبدأ بهجرة المكتبة اليونانية الاسكندرانية إلى بغداد ، ثم أعقبتها هجرة علمية أخرى من بلاد الأعاجم - فارس القديمة ، تحمل إلى العالم العربي - علم إيران - ثم هجرة ثالثة أتت من الهند والسند تحمل الكثير من آراء الهنود في الطب والفلك والرياضيات، وسنرى فيما بعد عالماً كبيراً هو البيروني يتكلم في كتابه «تحقيق ما للهند من مقولة » عن علوم الهند . وكان البيروني - فيما نعلم - عالماً ممحصاً وذا منهج مقارن ، وقد قام بمقارنة كل تلك العلوم الهندية التي وصل إلى معرفتها معرفة تكاد تكون تامة ، من طب وفلك ورياضيات بتراث اليونان العلمي ، ثم قارن كل هذا بما عند المسلمين . ولقد وصل البيروني إلى النتائج الخطيرة الآتية : أنه كان لدى الهنود علم جزئي كبير ، على درجة من التقدم ، لكن لا يربطه رباط علمي أو منهجي ، أبحاث متناثرة في الطب والرياضيات والفلكيات والطبيعيات، ولكن خالية من الإطار المنهجي الرائع . وكان لدى اليونان - على العكس من هذا - نظرية العلم ، نظرية البرهان . فبينما لا نجد لدى اليونان هذا العدد الجديد من أبحاث الهنود في مختلف العلوم الجزئية ، التي كان الهنود سادتها ، لا نجد لدى الهنود نظرية في العلم ، في البرهان دون المقدمات اليقينية ، قمة الفكر اليوناني . وسمة الحضارة اليونانية كلها . وكان الهنود يقفون منثالين متعجبين مسحورين ، حين يعرض لهم هذه النظرية الأخيرة ، بل إنهم نسبوه إلى السحر.
Chapter One: Transmission of Science to the Islamic World. We are not trying in this chapter to date the transmission of chemical, natural, medical, and mathematical sciences to the Islamic world. I defer this dating to a separate comprehensive research, which I hope to undertake soon, God willing. It is certain that the movement of transmission of pre-Islamic scientific heritage—especially the Greek heritage—to the Islamic world has received the attention of a generation of giant researchers, who have tried to clarify this transmission: its paths, methods, and men. Leclerc's research on 'History of Medicine among the Arabs' was an exemplary model in precision and comprehensiveness, and Browne also wrote on the same subject. Then research followed in various domains and other divisions of science: natural, chemical, and mathematical. Despite the precision of what was written and the thorough knowledge of the numerous researchers of their sources, the passage of time reveals new documents that enrich research and call for writing on it once and even multiple times. As for us in this book before us, we are chronicling the creative methodology of the Muslims, and we do not address this transmission except in a general manner, trying only to extract from it some models of methodology from some applied scientists. Many historians of science have held that Arab science did not begin until the medical and scientific teaching council moved from Alexandria during the reign of the Umayyad caliph Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz to Antioch, then to Harran, then to Marw, taking a long journey that ended in Baghdad. Then the scientific assemblies in Baghdad began the translation movement on one hand, and scientific research on the other. The Abbasid caliphs summoned these translators to their palaces, and al-Ma'mun later built the House of Wisdom; experimental research began in various sciences. We can therefore say: The history of science among the Arabs begins with the migration of the Greek Alexandrian library to Baghdad, then it was followed by another scientific migration from the lands of the non-Arabs—ancient Persia—carrying Iranian science to the Arab world, then a third migration came from India and Sindh carrying many of the Indians' opinions in medicine, astronomy, and mathematics. Later we will see a great scholar, al-Biruni, speaking in his book 'Tahqiq ma li al-Hind min maqula' about the sciences of India. Al-Biruni was—as far as we know—a thorough scholar with a comparative methodology. He compared all those Indian sciences that he came to know almost completely—medicine, astronomy, and mathematics—with the Greek scientific heritage, then compared all this with what the Muslims had. Al-Biruni reached the following serious conclusions: The Indians had a large partial science, at a degree of advancement, but no scientific or methodological link bound it; scattered research in medicine, mathematics, astronomy, and natural sciences, but lacking the magnificent methodological framework. The Greeks, on the contrary, had a theory of science, a theory of demonstration (burhan). While we do not find among the Greeks this new number of the Indians' researches in various partial sciences (which the Indians mastered), we do not find among the Indians a theory of science, of demonstration based on certain premises—the pinnacle of Greek thought and a feature of the entire Greek civilization. The Indians stood amazed and awestruck when this latter theory was presented to them; indeed they attributed it to magic.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage is a historical introduction from the book 'Manahij Bahth' (Methods of Research), setting the stage for a discussion of the Muslims' creative methodology. The author begins by stating that this chapter is not a detailed chronological account of how the chemical, natural, medical, and mathematical sciences were transmitted to the Islamic world. He postpones that for a future independent study. He acknowledges the work of earlier scholars like Leclerc and Browne, who have thoroughly studied the transmission of Greek science, but notes that new documents continue to emerge, prompting further writing. The author clarifies that his book focuses on chronicling the Muslims' own methodological creativity, only touching on the transmission of science generally to extract models of methodology from applied scientists.

Next, the author presents a common narrative among historians: Arab science began when the medical and scientific teaching council moved from Alexandria (during the Umayyad caliph Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz) to Antioch, then to Harran, then to Marw, and finally to Baghdad. This journey coincided with the Abbasid caliphs sponsoring translation and the establishment of the House of Wisdom by al-Ma'mun, leading to experimental research.

The author then summarizes three major migrations of knowledge to the Islamic world: first, the Greek Alexandrian library to Baghdad; second, Persian (Iranian) science; third, Indian knowledge in medicine, astronomy, and mathematics. This sets the stage for discussing al-Biruni, a comparative scholar who studied Indian sciences thoroughly. Al-Biruni's crucial conclusion was that Indians had extensive empirical knowledge in various fields but lacked an overarching methodological framework—a theory of science and demonstration (burhan). The Greeks, on the other hand, had this theory but less empirical data. The Indians were reportedly amazed by this Greek theory of demonstration, even attributing it to magic. This contrast underscores the significance of the Greek logical heritage (especially the theory of demonstration) which the Muslims would later integrate with empirical observations to develop their own distinctive methodology.

العلم الجزئي
المنهج
البرهان

Key Takeaway

Al-Burhan: Theory vs. Data
Al-Biruni's comparative analysis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the distinction between partial sciences (al-‘ilm al-juz’i) and the theory of demonstration (al-burhan) as illustrated by al-Biruni, and recognize the significance of this distinction for Islamic methodology.

Core Idea
The Greeks had a theory of proof but less data; the Indians had data but no theory; the Muslims combined both.

Al-Biruni's comparative analysis revealed a fundamental contrast: the Indians possessed extensive empirical research in many fields but lacked a unified theoretical framework, while the Greeks had a sophisticated theory of demonstration (al-burhan) but fewer specific empirical findings. This insight is central to understanding what the Muslims later developed: a creative methodology that integrated both.

Guided Path
1
Identify al-Biruni's two objects of comparison
He compared Indian sciences (medicine, astronomy, mathematics) with Greek scientific heritage, then with what the Muslims had.
2
Describe the Indian condition according to al-Biruni
Indians had al-‘ilm al-juz’i: large partial sciences, advanced but scattered, lacking a methodological framework.
The author uses the term 'magnificent methodological framework' to describe what was missing.
3
Describe the Greek condition
Greeks had a theory of science, a theory of demonstration (burhan) based on certain premises. This is described as the pinnacle of Greek thought.
4
Synthesize the implications for Islamic science
The Muslims inherited both: the Greek theoretical framework and the empirical data from India and Persia. Their creative methodology emerged from combining these.

لسنا نحاول في هذا الفصل تأريخ انتقال العلوم الكيميائية والطبيعية والطبية والرياضية إلى العالم الإسلامي . إنني أؤخر هذا التأريخ لبحث مستقل شامل ، أرجو أن أقوم به قريباً إن شاء الله . ومن المؤكد أن حركة انتقال التراث العلمي قبل الإسلام - وبخاصة التراث اليوناني - إلى العالم الإسلامي قد حظي بعناية جيل من جبابرة الباحثين ، وقد حاولوا توضيح هذا الانتقال طرقه وأساليبه ورجاله . وكان بحث لوكلير عن - تاريخ الطب عند العرب - مثالاً يحتذى في دقة البحث واحاطته بالموضوع ، كما قام براون أيضاً بالكتابة في نفس الموضوع ، ثم تتابعت الأبحاث في مختلف النطاقات ، وفي أقسام العلم الأخرى من طبيعية وكيميائية ورياضية . وبالرغم من دقة ما كتب ، ومعرفة الباحثين المتعددين لمصادر أبحاثهم معرفة تامة ، فان مرور السنين يكشف لنا عن وثائق جديدة تزيد البحث خصوبة وتدعو إلى الكتابة فيه مرة بل مرات أخرى .
أما نحن في هذا الكتاب الذي بين أيدينا فاننا نؤرخ لمنهج المسلمين الإبداعي ، ولا نتعرض لهذا الإنتقال إلا في صورة عامة ، محاولين فقط أن نستخرج منه بعض نماذج المنهج لدى بعض العلماء التطبيقيين .
لقد ذهب كثيرون من مؤرخي العلم ان العلم العربي لم يبدأ ، إلا حين انتقل مجلس التعليم الطبي والعلمي من الاسكندرية في عهد الخليفة الأموي عمر بن عبد العزيز إلى انطاكية ومنها إلى حران ومنها إلى مرو متخذاً رحلة طويلة انتهت إلى بغداد ، ومن ثم بدأت المجامع العلمية في بغداد حركة الترجمة من ناحية ، والبحث العلمي من ناحية أخرى . وقد استدعى خلفاء بني العباس هؤلاء التراجمة إلى قصورهم ، وبني المأمون بعد ذلك بيت الحكمة ؛ وبدأ البحث التجريبي في مختلف العلوم .
نستطيع إذن أن نقول : إن تاريخ العلم عند العرب يبدأ بهجرة المكتبة اليونانية الاسكندرانية إلى بغداد ، ثم أعقبتها هجرة علمية أخرى من بلاد الأعاجم - فارس القديمة ، تحمل إلى العالم العربي - علم إيران - ثم هجرة ثالثة أتت من الهند والسند تحمل الكثير من آراء الهنود في الطب والفلك والرياضيات، وسنرى فيما بعد عالماً كبيراً هو البيروني يتكلم في كتابه «تحقيق ما للهند من مقولة » عن علوم الهند . وكان البيروني - فيما نعلم - عالماً ممحصاً وذا منهج مقارن ، وقد قام بمقارنة كل تلك العلوم الهندية التي وصل إلى معرفتها معرفة تكاد تكون تامة ، من طب وفلك ورياضيات بتراث اليونان العلمي ، ثم قارن كل هذا بما عند المسلمين . ولقد وصل البيروني إلى النتائج الخطيرة الآتية : أنه كان لدى الهنود علم جزئي كبير ، على درجة من التقدم ، لكن لا يربطه رباط علمي أو منهجي ، أبحاث متناثرة في الطب والرياضيات والفلكيات والطبيعيات، ولكن خالية من الإطار المنهجي الرائع . وكان لدى اليونان - على العكس من هذا - نظرية العلم ، نظرية البرهان . فبينما لا نجد لدى اليونان هذا العدد الجديد من أبحاث الهنود في مختلف العلوم الجزئية ، التي كان الهنود سادتها ، لا نجد لدى الهنود نظرية في العلم ، في البرهان دون المقدمات اليقينية ، قمة الفكر اليوناني . وسمة الحضارة اليونانية كلها . وكان الهنود يقفون منثالين متعجبين مسحورين ، حين يعرض لهم هذه النظرية الأخيرة ، بل إنهم نسبوه إلى السحر.
Chapter One: Transmission of Science to the Islamic World. We are not trying in this chapter to date the transmission of chemical, natural, medical, and mathematical sciences to the Islamic world. I defer this dating to a separate comprehensive research, which I hope to undertake soon, God willing. It is certain that the movement of transmission of pre-Islamic scientific heritage—especially the Greek heritage—to the Islamic world has received the attention of a generation of giant researchers, who have tried to clarify this transmission: its paths, methods, and men. Leclerc's research on 'History of Medicine among the Arabs' was an exemplary model in precision and comprehensiveness, and Browne also wrote on the same subject. Then research followed in various domains and other divisions of science: natural, chemical, and mathematical. Despite the precision of what was written and the thorough knowledge of the numerous researchers of their sources, the passage of time reveals new documents that enrich research and call for writing on it once and even multiple times. As for us in this book before us, we are chronicling the creative methodology of the Muslims, and we do not address this transmission except in a general manner, trying only to extract from it some models of methodology from some applied scientists. Many historians of science have held that Arab science did not begin until the medical and scientific teaching council moved from Alexandria during the reign of the Umayyad caliph Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz to Antioch, then to Harran, then to Marw, taking a long journey that ended in Baghdad. Then the scientific assemblies in Baghdad began the translation movement on one hand, and scientific research on the other. The Abbasid caliphs summoned these translators to their palaces, and al-Ma'mun later built the House of Wisdom; experimental research began in various sciences. We can therefore say: The history of science among the Arabs begins with the migration of the Greek Alexandrian library to Baghdad, then it was followed by another scientific migration from the lands of the non-Arabs—ancient Persia—carrying Iranian science to the Arab world, then a third migration came from India and Sindh carrying many of the Indians' opinions in medicine, astronomy, and mathematics. Later we will see a great scholar, al-Biruni, speaking in his book 'Tahqiq ma li al-Hind min maqula' about the sciences of India. Al-Biruni was—as far as we know—a thorough scholar with a comparative methodology. He compared all those Indian sciences that he came to know almost completely—medicine, astronomy, and mathematics—with the Greek scientific heritage, then compared all this with what the Muslims had. Al-Biruni reached the following serious conclusions: The Indians had a large partial science, at a degree of advancement, but no scientific or methodological link bound it; scattered research in medicine, mathematics, astronomy, and natural sciences, but lacking the magnificent methodological framework. The Greeks, on the contrary, had a theory of science, a theory of demonstration (burhan). While we do not find among the Greeks this new number of the Indians' researches in various partial sciences (which the Indians mastered), we do not find among the Indians a theory of science, of demonstration based on certain premises—the pinnacle of Greek thought and a feature of the entire Greek civilization. The Indians stood amazed and awestruck when this latter theory was presented to them; indeed they attributed it to magic.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage is a historical introduction from the book 'Manahij Bahth' (Methods of Research), setting the stage for a discussion of the Muslims' creative methodology. The author begins by stating that this chapter is not a detailed chronological account of how the chemical, natural, medical, and mathematical sciences were transmitted to the Islamic world. He postpones that for a future independent study. He acknowledges the work of earlier scholars like Leclerc and Browne, who have thoroughly studied the transmission of Greek science, but notes that new documents continue to emerge, prompting further writing. The author clarifies that his book focuses on chronicling the Muslims' own methodological creativity, only touching on the transmission of science generally to extract models of methodology from applied scientists.

Next, the author presents a common narrative among historians: Arab science began when the medical and scientific teaching council moved from Alexandria (during the Umayyad caliph Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz) to Antioch, then to Harran, then to Marw, and finally to Baghdad. This journey coincided with the Abbasid caliphs sponsoring translation and the establishment of the House of Wisdom by al-Ma'mun, leading to experimental research.

The author then summarizes three major migrations of knowledge to the Islamic world: first, the Greek Alexandrian library to Baghdad; second, Persian (Iranian) science; third, Indian knowledge in medicine, astronomy, and mathematics. This sets the stage for discussing al-Biruni, a comparative scholar who studied Indian sciences thoroughly. Al-Biruni's crucial conclusion was that Indians had extensive empirical knowledge in various fields but lacked an overarching methodological framework—a theory of science and demonstration (burhan). The Greeks, on the other hand, had this theory but less empirical data. The Indians were reportedly amazed by this Greek theory of demonstration, even attributing it to magic. This contrast underscores the significance of the Greek logical heritage (especially the theory of demonstration) which the Muslims would later integrate with empirical observations to develop their own distinctive methodology.

Key Terms
البرهان
العلم الجزئي
المنهج

Key Takeaway
Al-Biruni's comparison shows that a theory of demonstration is distinct from empirical data, and both are necessary for a robust scientific methodology.

Method of Learning

The Distinctive Empirical Method of Muslim Scientists
Argues that Muslim scientists developed their own empirical method, distinct from Greek and Indian traditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three methodological approaches described (Greek demonstrative, Indian particularistic, Islamic empirical inductive) and explain how the Islamic approach was a synthesis and innovation.

Core Idea
Muslims did not merely copy Greek science; they invented a new empirical inductive method that transformed it.

This passage challenges the common narrative that Islamic science was merely a transmitter of Greek knowledge. The author argues that Muslim scientists developed their own unique methodology—the empirical inductive method—which they applied to both Greek and Indian sciences. Understanding this distinction is crucial for appreciating the originality of Islamic scientific contributions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three methodological categories
The passage contrasts the Greek method (burhan/demonstration), the Indian method (particularistic empiricism), and the Islamic method (empirical inductive). Locate where each is described and note the key terms: البرهان for Greek, the reference to 'particular science' for Indians, and المنهج الإستقرائي التجريبي for Islamic.
The author explicitly states: 'they did not occupy themselves only with particular science, as the Indians did, nor did they occupy themselves with theory — and its tool, demonstration — as the Greeks did, but they arrived at the empirical inductive method.'
2
Note the evidence for the Islamic method's independence
The author mentions three examples to prove that Islamic science had lasting influence independent of Greek sources: Ibn al-Haytham's ideas in Europe, al-Tusi's work on Euclid, and Ibn Sina's Canon as a medical textbook. Identify these in the passage.
These examples show that Muslim scientists did not just transmit but contributed original work that shaped European science.
3
Analyze the role of the 'new spirit' in research
The passage says 'the basic reason for the development of science among the Arabs is that they confronted it with a new spirit in research.' Connect this spirit to the empirical inductive method. What does this 'new spirit' entail?
The author considers this method as the key factor that pushed science forward in the Islamic world.
4
Evaluate the author's counterexample about Galen
The author acknowledges that Greeks like Galen had an empirical component but were held back by Aristotelian logic. How does this strengthen the argument that the Islamic method was more comprehensive?
Galen's partial empiricism is contrasted with the full development of the empirical inductive method among Muslims.

The serious (matters) that this systematic scholar reached, who had a great impact on the development of Islamic science, with his wonderful inductive method, as he had a great merit in the history of sciences in general. But al-Biruni did not pay attention to his own merit and the merit of his predecessors among the Muslim applied scientists, that the method of Muslims was not this nor that. They did not occupy themselves only with particular science, as the Indians did, nor did they occupy themselves with theory — and its tool, demonstration — as the Greeks did, but they arrived at the empirical inductive method as a method, and applied it to the sciences of the Indians and the sciences of the Greeks. And when also Persian science or Indian science came, it found before it a scientific logic, differing most strongly from the naive method that dominated Indian research in mathematics, astronomy, and others. And without this method, science would have fallen in the Islamic world, and ended or at least stopped at the research of the Greeks and the research of the Indians and Persians. Here the perennial question confronts us: Did the Greeks not have an empirical method used in their research? There is no doubt that they had a type of this method, and that the empirical skeptics, especially the school of physicians among them, applied it to some extent, but the empirical skeptics did not achieve any notable success in the Greek world. And we know that Galen's works were transmitted to the Islamic world, and Muslim scholars benefited from his experiments and research, and that Galen was influenced by the empirical aspect of the method of the empirical skeptics, and that the most important of his knowledge is due to the application of this method, or the constructive aspect of the empirical skeptics' scientific doctrine in his research. But despite this, Galen did not completely push forward in this path; Aristotelian logic hindered him to a large extent. There was a Greek medical science, and this science reached the Arab world completely, and the school of Hunayn ibn Ishaq undertook its transmission, specialized in it, and benefited from it, and much of the research of Greek physicians was based on experiment, but it was not...
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage is a historical argument about the origins and nature of the empirical method in Islamic civilization. The author begins by referencing a 'systematic scholar' who had a great impact on Islamic science through his inductive method, but notes that al-Biruni did not recognize that the Muslim method was neither purely particularistic (like the Indians) nor purely theoretical-demonstrative (like the Greeks). Instead, Muslims developed a genuine empirical inductive method and applied it to both Indian and Greek sciences. The passage then critiques the common view that science entered the Islamic world from outside, and that European historians only acknowledge Arab scientific merit as derivative of Greek science. The author provides counterexamples: the lasting influence of Ibn al-Haytham's ideas in Europe, al-Tusi's work on Euclid, and Ibn Sina's Canon as a medical textbook in Europe until the 17th century. The key point is that the real reason for the flourishing of science among Muslims was their new spirit of research, centered on the inductive method, which they discovered independently. The author then discusses the transfer of learning from Alexandria to Baghdad, arguing that when Greek, Persian, and Indian sciences arrived, they encountered a new scientific methodology that transformed them. The passage concludes by addressing the question of whether the Greeks had an empirical method, acknowledging that the empirical skeptics (especially medical schools) applied it partially, but that Galen, despite benefiting from it, was held back by Aristotelian logic. The author implies that the Islamic empirical method was more comprehensive and effective.

المنهج الإستقرائي التجريبي
البرهان

The Empirical Method in Islamic Sciences: Roots in Usul al-Fiqh
Author introduces empirical method as central to Islamic sciences and previews examples from Jabir ibn Hayyan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to trace the Islamic empirical method to the usul al-fiqh concept of dawran and explain how repeated experiment yields certainty (qaṭʿ).

Core Idea
The Islamic empirical method is not imported; it is indigenous, rooted in jurisprudential reasoning.

This passage introduces a chapter on examples of the empirical method among Muslim scholars. The author highlights that the inductive method (manhaj istiqra'i) of jurists is the method of science. Key figures like al-Qarafi, Rida al-Din al-Naysaburi, and Ibn Taymiyya explicitly equate the usul al-fiqh technique of dawran (co-variation) with empirical experiment (tajriba), and note that repeated experiment produces certainty (qaṭʿ). This demonstrates that empirical reasoning was integral to Islamic intellectual tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key claim about the inductive method
The author states that jurists (usuliyyun) realized their inductive method is the method of science. What does this imply about the status of empirical reasoning in Islamic thought?
2
Analyze al-Qarafi's equation of dawran and experiment
Al-Qarafi says 'الدورانات عين التجربة'. What is the significance of equating a legal reasoning tool with empirical experiment?
3
Connect dawran to the concept of certainty (qaṭʿ)
Repeated experiment (tajriba) yields certainty. How does this align with the goal of certainty in Islamic jurisprudence?
4
Recognize the cross-disciplinary application
Rida al-Din al-Naysaburi applies dawran to medicine, and Ibn Taymiyya emphasizes experiment in medicine. How does this show the empirical method's universality?

I am not going into the history of Arab science now, but it suffices for me to say that science was alive here in efficacy and dynamism, while in Europe there were only few, faint, and shrinking researches, and science did not flourish there until the Islamic empirical method was transferred. And it proceeds to present some examples of this empirical method among some Muslim scholars. The Second Chapter: Examples of the Empirical Method among Some Muslim Scholars. The jurists (usuliyyun) realized that their inductive method (manhaj al-istiqra'i) is the method of science. We have already seen al-Qarafi previously. And while discussing the method of al-dawran (co-variation) in usul al-fiqh, he says: 'Al-dawranat (co-variations) are precisely experiment (tajriba). And experiment may be repeated so that it yields certainty (qaṭʿ).' Also, Rida al-Din al-Naysaburi affirms that 'a large number of the rules of medicine were only established by experiment, and it (i.e., experiment) is exactly al-dawran.' And we have seen Ibn Taymiyya – the historian of the Islamic inductive method – delve into empirical matters (tajribiyyat) and declare that they are the path to knowledge, especially in medicine. Thus the method moved from 'theory' (al-qanun) to 'application' (al-tatbiq) and was practiced by Muslim empirical scientists. We are not following the empirical method in its various applications among Muslim scientists in physics, chemistry, medicine, and botany. Rather we present some examples from the thinking of one or two scholars. First: Jabir ibn Hayyan and his Empirical Method. Perhaps the oldest scholar whose scientific works have reached us is Jabir ibn Hayyan (it is probable that he died after 160 AH / 776 CE). But internal criticism of Ibn Hayyan's writings...
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage is an introduction to a chapter on examples of the empirical method among Muslim scholars. The author first contrasts the vibrant Islamic scientific tradition with the decline in Europe, noting that the empirical method was transferred from Islam. Then, in the second chapter heading, the focus shifts to specific examples. The author highlights key figures: al-Qarafi, who in his discussion of al-dawran (co-variation) in usul al-fiqh explicitly equates 'dawranat' with 'tajriba' (experiment) and notes that repeated experiment yields certainty (qaṭʿ). This shows that the inductive method in jurisprudence is seen as empirical. Next, Rida al-Din al-Naysaburi applies the same concept to medicine, affirming that many medical rules are established by experiment, which is exactly al-dawran. Ibn Taymiyya, described as the historian of the Islamic inductive method, also emphasizes experiment as a path to knowledge, especially in medicine. The author then states that the method moved from theory (qanun) to application (tatbiq) and was practiced by Muslim empirical scientists. However, the author will not survey all fields (physics, chemistry, medicine, botany) but will present examples from one or two scholars, beginning with Jabir ibn Hayyan. The passage ends abruptly with a mention of internal criticism of Ibn Hayyan's works, setting up further discussion. Key takeaway: The Islamic empirical method is not limited to natural sciences; it is rooted in jurisprudence (usul al-fiqh) through the method of dawran, which is equated with experiment.

The Empirical Method: Certainty through Repeated Experiment
Author introduces chapter on empirical method, citing al-Qarafi and Ibn Taymiyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how al-Qarafi equated correlation (dawaran) with experiment (tajriba) and why repeated experiment can yield certainty (qat') according to Islamic epistemology.

Core Idea
In Islamic usul, repeated empirical observation yields certainty, not just probability.

This passage from the second chapter establishes that the empirical method was recognized by Muslim scholars as a valid path to certainty. The author cites al-Qarafi and others to show that the usuli method of correlation (dawaran) is identical to scientific experiment. We will explore this epistemological claim and its implications for Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key claim
Find al-Qarafi's statement about correlation and experiment. He says 'الدورانات عين التجربة' (correlations are the very experiment). This equates the legal method of identifying causes (dawaran) with empirical science.
2
Understand frequency and certainty
Al-Qarafi adds that 'تكثر التجربة فتفيد القطع' (experiment may become frequent and yield certainty). Note the claim that empirical repetition can produce absolute certainty (qat'), not just probability.
3
Connect to Islamic sciences
Observe that al-Naysaburi applies this to medicine: medical principles are established by experiment (tajriba), which is correlation. Ibn Taymiyya affirms that experimental matters are a path to knowledge, especially in medicine. This shows interdisciplinary consistency.
4
Recognize the historical transition
The author states that the method moved from 'theory' to 'application' (من القانون إلى التطبيق). Muslim experimental scientists practiced it in physics, chemistry, medicine, botany. This sets the stage for case studies of individual scientists.

الفَصْلُ الثَّانِي
نماذج بين المنهج التجريبي لدى

الطب .
