Chapter Two: Models of the Empirical Method among Some Muslim Scholars. The usulis realized that their inductive method is the method of science. We have seen al-Qarafi previously. While investigating the path of correlation (dawaran) in usul al-fiqh, he says: 'Correlations are the very experiment. Experiment may become frequent and yield certainty (1).' Similarly, Rida al-Din al-Naysaburi affirms that 'a large number of the principles of medicine are established by experiment, which is correlation itself.' And we have seen Ibn Taymiyya – the historian of the Islamic inductive method – engage in experimental matters and affirm that they are a path to knowledge, especially in medicine. The method thus moved from 'theory' to 'application', and Muslim experimental scientists practiced it. We do not here follow the empirical method in its various applications among Muslim scientists in physics, chemistry, medicine, and botany. Rather, we present some models from the thinking of one or two scientists.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage opens Chapter Two by establishing that the empirical method (al-manhaj al-tajribi) was recognized and practiced by Muslim scholars. The author begins by noting that the usulis (scholars of usul al-fiqh) understood their inductive method as the scientific method. He cites al-Qarafi, who explicitly states that correlations (dawarat) in legal reasoning are identical to experiment (tajriba) and that repeated experiment yields certainty (qat'). This is a key epistemological claim: empirical repetition can produce certainty. Next, Rida al-Din al-Naysaburi is quoted, applying the same idea to medicine: medical principles are established by experiment, which is correlation. Then Ibn Taymiyya is mentioned as a historian of the inductive method who affirmed that experimental matters lead to knowledge, especially in medicine. The author then transitions to state that the method moved from theory to application, and Muslim experimental scientists practiced it across various fields. However, the chapter will not cover all applications but will provide models from one or two scientists. This sets the stage for the following discussion of Jabir ibn Hayyan's experimental method.

Jabir ibn Hayyan's Transformation Thesis
Author introduces Jabir's transformation thesis and its conflict with Aristotelian fixed essences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how Jabir ibn Hayyan's concept of istihala (transformation) challenges the Aristotelian notion of fixed essences in Islamic chemical thought.

Core Idea
Essences can change: Jabir overturns Aristotle's fixed categories.

The earliest scientific works we have are attributed to Jabir ibn Hayyan. His central claim is the transformation of metals: one metal can change into another. This contradicts the Aristotelian view that essences are fixed. Let's explore how he supported this empirically.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core concept
Find where the author describes istihala. What does it mean for metals?
Istihala: change of essence (mahiyya) of one metal into another.
2
Contrast with Aristotle
What is the Aristotelian view? How does Jabir differ?
Aristotle: essences fixed; Jabir: essences can transform.
3
Examine the evidence
How does Jabir claim we know natures?
Through weight (mizan) and practice (driba), not qualitative essence.
4
Evaluate the shift
Why is this a departure from Greek philosophy?
Empirical knowledge replaces deductive essence-based reasoning.

First: Jabir ibn Hayyan and His Experimental Method. Perhaps the earliest scientist whose scientific works have reached us is Jabir ibn Hayyan (it is probable that he died after 160 AH). However, internal criticism of Ibn Hayyan's writings confirms that they were written in the late third century AH and early fourth century. Whether the existence of Jabir ibn Hayyan is authentic or not, the chemical works attributed to him are present in our hands, and they clearly indicate the establishment of an Arabic chemical science in the late third century AH and early fourth century. What method, then, did the chemical investigations of this early period follow? The main idea in Jabir ibn Hayyan's chemical investigations is the transformation (istihala) of metals: the change of the essence (mahiyya) of one metal into the essence of another, or more precisely, the change of one nature (tabi'a) into another. Does this agree with the Aristotelian idea of fixed essence qualitatively? Indeed, Jabir ibn Hayyan wrote the book 'al-Hudud' (1), which is in the Aristotelian collection, but he wrote it only as an intellectual exercise, not as a practical book. For him, natures change, and to change, they must lose their qualitative essence in order to transform into another essence or nature. Moreover, we generally do not attain knowledge of the essence — knowledge of the quality — but we only attain the weight (mizan) of natures, i.e., knowing them as: 'The way to know natures is their balance; whoever knows their balance knows everything in them and how they are composed.' As for how we know the quantity, it is through experiment. He says: 'Practice (driba) brings that out. Whoever is practiced is truly knowledgeable, and whoever is not practiced is not knowledgeable. Sufficient for you is practice in all crafts.' (2) Dr. Zaki Najib Mahmud insightfully noted that driba in Jabir ibn Hayyan means experiment (tajriba) (3). And Jabir ibn Hayyan says: 'The practiced craftsman is skilled, while the unpracticed one is useless' (4). But Dr. Zaki Najib Mahmud did not notice that Jabir ibn Hayyan also uses the word 'tajriba'. He used it, as he used the word 'imtihan' (examination) (5). It is also observed that he did not reject...
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage examines the experimental method of Jabir ibn Hayyan, the early chemist. The author begins by noting the historical uncertainty about Jabir's identity but affirms that the chemical works attributed to him exist and indicate a developed Arabic chemical science. The central concept is the transformation (istihala) of metals: elements can change their essences. This challenges Aristotelian fixed essences. Jabir wrote 'al-Hudud' as an Aristotelian exercise but not as applied science. For Jabir, we do not know essences qualitatively; we know natures through their weight (mizan) — a quantitative measure. Knowledge of quantities comes through experiment (tajriba) or practice (driba). He emphasizes that only the practiced person is truly knowledgeable, applying this to all crafts. The author then notes that Dr. Zaki Najib Mahmud correctly identified 'driba' as meaning experiment, but he missed that Jabir also explicitly used the word 'tajriba' and 'imtihan'. The passage ends with an incomplete observation that Jabir did not reject something (likely experimenting on humans). The key takeaway is that Jabir's method was empirical, relying on practice and experiment rather than Aristotelian fixed categories.

Key Terms

الماهية
الميزان
الدربة

Key Takeaway
Jabir ibn Hayyan's transformation thesis asserts that essences are not fixed but can change, and that knowledge comes through quantitative measurement and experiment, not qualitative essence.

Practice and Balance: Jabir's Empirical Method
The author details Jabir's emphasis on balance and practice as the means to know natures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe how Jabir ibn Hayyan uses 'driba' (practice) and 'mizan' (balance) as the foundation for empirical knowledge, and identify its role in Islamic scientific methodology.

Core Idea
Knowledge of natures comes from weight and practice, not fixed essence.

Jabir ibn Hayyan argues that we cannot know essences directly; instead, we must rely on balance (mizan) and practice (driba). He equates practice with experiment, emphasizing that only the practiced person is truly knowledgeable. This lesson unpacks his empirical method and its significance.

Guided Path
1
Locate the key terms
Find 'mizan' and 'driba' in the Arabic. What do they mean?
Mizan = balance/weight; driba = practice/experiment.
2
Understand the epistemological shift
Jabir says we know natures through their balance, not their qualitative essence. Why is this important?
It moves from deduction to measurement.
3
Analyze the quotes about driba
What does Jabir say about the practiced craftsman? How does this relate to scientific knowledge?
Quotes: 'الدربة تخرج ذلك...' and 'الصانع الدرب يحذق...'
4
Connect to tajriba and imtihan
The author notes that Jabir also uses 'tajriba' and 'imtihan'. What does this reveal about his method?
Driba is not just habit but systematic experiment.

استخدمها ، كما استخدم كلمة الامتحان(٥) . ويلاحظ أيضاً أنه لم يرفض
First: Jabir ibn Hayyan and His Experimental Method. Perhaps the earliest scientist whose scientific works have reached us is Jabir ibn Hayyan (it is probable that he died after 160 AH). However, internal criticism of Ibn Hayyan's writings confirms that they were written in the late third century AH and early fourth century. Whether the existence of Jabir ibn Hayyan is authentic or not, the chemical works attributed to him are present in our hands, and they clearly indicate the establishment of an Arabic chemical science in the late third century AH and early fourth century. What method, then, did the chemical investigations of this early period follow? The main idea in Jabir ibn Hayyan's chemical investigations is the transformation (istihala) of metals: the change of the essence (mahiyya) of one metal into the essence of another, or more precisely, the change of one nature (tabi'a) into another. Does this agree with the Aristotelian idea of fixed essence qualitatively? Indeed, Jabir ibn Hayyan wrote the book 'al-Hudud' (1), which is in the Aristotelian collection, but he wrote it only as an intellectual exercise, not as a practical book. For him, natures change, and to change, they must lose their qualitative essence in order to transform into another essence or nature. Moreover, we generally do not attain knowledge of the essence — knowledge of the quality — but we only attain the weight (mizan) of natures, i.e., knowing them as: 'The way to know natures is their balance; whoever knows their balance knows everything in them and how they are composed.' As for how we know the quantity, it is through experiment. He says: 'Practice (driba) brings that out. Whoever is practiced is truly knowledgeable, and whoever is not practiced is not knowledgeable. Sufficient for you is practice in all crafts.' (2) Dr. Zaki Najib Mahmud insightfully noted that driba in Jabir ibn Hayyan means experiment (tajriba) (3). And Jabir ibn Hayyan says: 'The practiced craftsman is skilled, while the unpracticed one is useless' (4). But Dr. Zaki Najib Mahmud did not notice that Jabir ibn Hayyan also uses the word 'tajriba'. He used it, as he used the word 'imtihan' (examination) (5). It is also observed that he did not reject...
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage examines the experimental method of Jabir ibn Hayyan, the early chemist. The author begins by noting the historical uncertainty about Jabir's identity but affirms that the chemical works attributed to him exist and indicate a developed Arabic chemical science. The central concept is the transformation (istihala) of metals: elements can change their essences. This challenges Aristotelian fixed essences. Jabir wrote 'al-Hudud' as an Aristotelian exercise but not as applied science. For Jabir, we do not know essences qualitatively; we know natures through their weight (mizan) — a quantitative measure. Knowledge of quantities comes through experiment (tajriba) or practice (driba). He emphasizes that only the practiced person is truly knowledgeable, applying this to all crafts. The author then notes that Dr. Zaki Najib Mahmud correctly identified 'driba' as meaning experiment, but he missed that Jabir also explicitly used the word 'tajriba' and 'imtihan'. The passage ends with an incomplete observation that Jabir did not reject something (likely experimenting on humans). The key takeaway is that Jabir's method was empirical, relying on practice and experiment rather than Aristotelian fixed categories.

Key Terms
الدربة
الميزان
التجربة
الامتحان

B) Through their balance (mizan)
C) Through divine revelation
D) Through syllogistic reasoning

Key Takeaway
Jabir's empirical method replaces knowledge of essences with knowledge through balance and practice, establishing experiment as the foundation of scientific inquiry.

Jabir's Two Paths of Reasoning: Aristotelian and Mutakallimun
Author presents Jabir's two methods of reasoning and his three types of inference by analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the Aristotelian demonstrative method and the mutakallimun's analogical method as presented by Jabir ibn Hayyan.

Core Idea
Jabir chose the mutakallimun's path because his experimental method required inference from the seen to the unseen.

Jabir ibn Hayyan, the pioneer of experimental chemistry, stood at a crossroads between two methods of reasoning: the Aristotelian categorical syllogism and demonstration (burhān), and the method of the mutakallimun (Islamic theologians) known as qiyās al-ghā'ib 'alā al-shāhid (inference from the unseen to the seen). In this passage, he clearly aligns himself with the latter, as it suits his empirical approach. Understanding this choice is key to appreciating how Islamic logicians adapted Greek logic to their own scientific and theological needs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two paths
Find in the Arabic the names of the two methods: 'طريق المنطق الأرسططاليسي' and 'طريق المتكلمين'. Note their Arabic names.
2
Understand the second path
The mutakallimun's path is 'قياس الغائب على الشاهد' (qiyās al-ghā'ib 'alā al-shāhid). This means drawing analogies from a known, observable thing (shāhid) to an unknown, unseen thing (ghā'ib).
3
Note Jabir's choice
Why does Jabir adopt the second path? The passage says 'منهجه التجريبي يحتم أن يأخذ بالطريق الثاني' (his experimental method required him to take the second path). Explain how experiment relies on observable evidence.
4
Contrast with Aristotelian method
Aristotelian logic relies on categorical syllogisms and demonstrative certainty. The mutakallimun's method is more flexible and analogical, fitting for experimental science.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكان أمام جابر بن حيان طريقان : طريق المنطق الأرسططاليسي : القياس والبرهان : وطريق المتكلمين : وهو قياس الغائب على الشاهد . وكان منهجه التجريبي يحتم أن يأخذ بالطريق الثاني ، بل وأن يستخدم نفس التعبير فيقرر ـ وهو بصدد البحث في كيفية الإستدلال والإستنباط ، أن تعلق شيء بآخر إنما « يكون من الشاهد بالغائب على ثلاثة أوجه وهي : أ ـ المجانسة . ب ـ مجرى العادة . جـ ـ الآثار . ( أ ) دلالة المجانسة : الأنموذج . ويسمي جابر بن حيان دلالة المجانسة بالأنموذج ، لأنها تقوم على استدلال بانموذج جزئي ـ على أنموذج جزئي آخر أو بنماذج جزئية للتوصل إلى حكم كلي وهو ما يقابل « الوقائع المختارة » في المنهج الإستقرائي المعاصر . ويقول جابر ان الرجل يرى صاحبه بعضاً من الشيء ليدل به على أن الكل من ذلك الشيء « مشابه لهذا البعض » ويرى ابن حيان أن دلالة هذا الباب من هذا الوجه ليست دلالة ثابتة صحيحة ، أي أنه يرى أنها ليست دلالة يقينية بل ظنية أو احتمالية ، ولكنه يذكر ـ وهنا يبين مصدره الذي أخذ منه هذه الدلالة ـ أن جماعة من أهل النظر « أي المتكلمون » قد استدلوا من هذا الباب على ما دلالة فيه عليه باضطرار » أي أنهم ذهبوا إلى يقينية هذه الدلالة « أعني أنهم
And before Jabir ibn Hayyan there were two paths: the path of Aristotelian logic: categorical syllogism and demonstration; and the path of the mutakallimun: which is inference from the unseen to the seen. And his experimental method required that he take the second path, and even to use the same expression, he states—while discussing how to reason and deduce—that the connection of one thing to another is 'from the seen to the unseen in three ways: A—homogeneity (al-mujānasa). B—customary course (majra al-‘āda). C—traces (al-āthār). (A) The indication of homogeneity: the model (al-unmudhaj). And Jabir ibn Hayyan calls the indication of homogeneity 'the model' because it is based on inferring from a partial model to another partial model or from partial models to arrive at a universal judgment, which corresponds to 'selected instances' in the contemporary inductive method. And Jabir says that a man sees a part of something from his companion to indicate by it that the whole of that thing 'resembles this part.' And Ibn Hayyan holds that the indication of this type in this respect is not a firm correct indication, meaning he holds that it is not a certain indication but rather presumptive or probable. However, he mentions—and here he shows the source from which he took this indication—that a group of the people of speculation, i.e., the mutakallimun, have inferred from this type what is necessary for them, meaning they held that this indication is certain—I mean that they...
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage presents the methodology of Jabir ibn Hayyan, a pioneering Arabic chemist. The author contrasts two paths of reasoning: the Aristotelian path based on categorical syllogism and demonstration (burhān), and the path of the mutakallimun (Islamic theologians) which employs inference from the unseen (ghā’ib) to the seen (shāhid)—that is, analogical reasoning (qiyās). Jabir's experimental method requires him to adopt the second path. He then breaks down this analogical inference into three types: (1) al-mujānasa (homogeneity), which he calls 'al-unmudhaj' (the model); (2) majra al-‘āda (customary course); and (3) al-āthār (traces). The first type, indication by homogeneity, involves reasoning from a part to the whole or from one particular model to another to reach a universal judgment—similar to 'selected instances' in modern inductive reasoning. Jabir illustrates this with an example: a man sees a part of something belonging to his companion and concludes that the whole resembles that part. Importantly, Jabir considers this type of indication to be non-certain, only presumptive or probable. However, he notes that the mutakallimun consider it certain (yaqīnī). The passage thus shows Jabir's critical stance toward the certainty of analogical inference, aligning it with probability and experience.

Key Terms
قياس الغائب على الشاهد
المنطق الأرسططاليسي
البرهان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What reason does Jabir give for choosing the path of the mutakallimun over Aristotelian logic?
A) He found Aristotelian logic too difficult.
B) His experimental method required inference from the seen to the unseen.
C) The mutakallimun's path is more certain.
D) He was a student of the mutakallimun.

Key Takeaway
Jabir chose the analogical method of the mutakallimun because it was more compatible with his empirical, experimental approach.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In usul al-fiqh, qiyās uses a known case (asl) to infer a ruling for a new case (far'). Write a brief analogical argument using a legal example from Islamic law (e.g., from khamr to other intoxicants). Indicate the 'seen' and 'unseen' parts.

The Three Types of Analogical Inference in Jabir's Methodology
Author presents Jabir's three types of inference by analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list and explain Jabir ibn Hayyan's three types of inference from the seen to the unseen.

Core Idea
Jabir divided analogical inference into three kinds: homogeneity, customary course, and traces.

After committing to the path of qiyās al-ghā'ib 'alā al-shāhid, Jabir ibn Hayyan classifies this inference into three specific types: al-mujānasa (homogeneity or model-based inference), majra al-‘āda (customary course), and al-āthār (traces). Each type has a different basis and level of certainty. This lesson unpacks each type and its role in inductive reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Find the three types in the Arabic
Locate the list: 'على ثلاثة أوجه وهي: أ ـ المجانسة. ب ـ مجرى العادة. جـ ـ الآثار.'
2
Understand the first type: al-mujānasa
This is inference from a partial model to a universal judgment. Note the term 'الأنموذج' (model). It corresponds to selecting representative instances.
Jabir calls it 'indication by homogeneity' and uses 'al-unmudhaj' as a synonym.
3
Understand the second and third types
Majra al-‘āda is inference based on regular recurrence (e.g., natural laws); al-āthār is inference from effects to causes.
4
Connect to modern concepts
Al-mujānasa resembles inductive generalization or sampling; majra al-‘āda resembles natural law induction; al-āthār resembles abductive reasoning.

Source Text

The Epistemological Status of Homogeneity Indication (Mujānasa)
Author presents Jabir's evaluation of the certainty of model-based inference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Jabir considered the indication by homogeneity (mujānasa) to be only probable, and contrast this with the view of the mutakallimun.

Core Idea
Jabir deemed model-based inference probable, not certain—unlike the mutakallimun who considered it necessary.

In a crucial methodological note, Jabir ibn Hayyan evaluates the epistemological strength of the first type of analogical inference—al-mujānasa or model-based inference. He declares that it is 'not a firm correct indication' but only presumptive or probable (dhannī). Yet he notes that some mutakallimun claimed it yields certainty (yaqīnī). This lesson examines the grounds for this disagreement and what it reveals about the nature of inductive inference.

Guided Path
1
Locate Jabir's evaluation in the Arabic
Find the phrase: 'ليست دلالة ثابتة صحيحة' and 'ليست دلالة يقينية بل ظنية أو احتمالية'.
2
Understand Jabir's reasoning
Why does he consider it probable? Because a part may not fully represent the whole, and models are imperfect.
Jabir's own example: seeing a part of a thing and concluding the whole is similar – this can lead to error.
3
Contrast with the mutakallimun's position
The passage says they 'استدلوا من هذا الباب على ما دلالة فيه عليه باضطرار' meaning they infallibly infer from it. Why would they claim certainty? Possibly because of a theological commitment to the coherence of creation.
4
Reflect on the implication for methodology
Jabir's doubt aligns with his empirical approach; he prioritizes experiment over pure analogy. The mutakallimun's certainty may stem from rationalist theology.

Method of Learning
intermediate
7 min
Jabir's Three Types of Analogical Inference
Jabir classifies types of inference from witness to unseen.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of indication from the witnessed to the unseen in Jabir's classification and explain the first type (anmudhaj).

Core Idea
Jabir reduces analogical inference to three patterns: similarity, custom, and effects.

Jabir ibn Hayyan, faced with two methodological paths—Aristotelian syllogism and the theologians' qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid—chose the latter for his empirical work. He then refined it by identifying exactly three ways in which the witnessed can indicate the unseen. This lesson unpacks that classification, especially the first type, which he calls 'anmudhaj' (model).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two paths available to Jabir
Locate in the passage where Jabir contrasts Aristotelian logic (qiyas wa burhan) with the theologians' method (qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid). Why does his experimental method lead him to choose the second?
Jabir's choice reflects his commitment to empirical, observable data.
2
Extract the three types of indication
Find the three Arabic terms Jabir lists for reasoning from the witnessed to the unseen: al-mujanasa, majra al-‘ada, and al-athar. Memorize their meanings.
These correspond to similarity, customary course, and effects.
3
Analyze the first type: dalalat al-mujanasa (anmudhaj)
Jabir calls this 'anmudhaj' (model). Understand his description: inference from a part to the whole, or from one partial model to another, to reach a universal judgment. Connect this to modern inductive reasoning ('selected facts').
The example given: a man sees part of something from his companion and infers the whole is similar.
4
Test Jabir's example
Imagine seeing a sample (anmudhaj) of a substance. Does its property guarantee the same property in all other samples of the same kind? Jabir says no—it is only probable.
This step reveals the epistemological heart of his critique.

( أ ) دلالة المجانسة : الأنموذج . ويسمي جابر بن حيان دلالة المجانسة بالأنموذج ، لأنها تقوم على استدلال بانموذج جزئي ـ على أنموذج جزئي آخر أو بنماذج جزئية للتوصل إلى حكم كلي وهو ما يقابل « الوقائع المختارة » في المنهج الإستقرائي المعاصر . ويقول جابر ان الرجل يرى صاحبه بعضاً من الشيء ليدل به على أن الكل من ذلك الشيء « مشابه لهذا البعض » ويرى ابن حيان أن دلالة هذا الباب من هذا الوجه ليست دلالة ثابتة صحيحة ، أي أنه يرى أنها ليست دلالة يقينية بل ظنية أو احتمالية ، ولكنه يذكر ـ وهنا يبين مصدره الذي أخذ منه هذه الدلالة ـ أن جماعة من أهل النظر « أي المتكلمون » قد استدلوا من هذا الباب على ما دلالة فيه عليه باضطرار » أي أنهم ذهبوا إلى يقينية هذه الدلالة « أعني أنهم أثبتوا من أجل هذا الشيء ، الذي هو الأنموذج مثلاً ، وهو من جنسه ، شيئاً آخر هو أكثر منه » ولكن جابر بن حيان لا يوافق على هذا ويرى أن هذه الدلالة غير اضطرارية ولا ثابتة في كل حال ، « وذلك أن هذا الشيء الذي هو الأنموذج مثلاً لا يوجب وجود شيء آخر من جنسه ، حكمه في الجوهر والطبيعة حكمه(۱) . تنبه جابر بن حيان إذن إلى وجود قياس الغائب على الشاهد عند المتكلمين ، ونادى بتطبيقه في التجربة ، غير أن المتكلمين ذهبوا إلى أن نتائج هذا القياس يقينية إذا طبق في الإلهيات ، ولكنه متوافقاً مع روح منهجه التجريبي - وهو هنا يتناقض مع نفسه - أنه إذا جمعت تجاربه ودرست أسبابها « على الولاء والدوام . خرج العلم منها وانقدح » ولعله قصد أنه قد وصل إلى الدقة في
Witness of others - as Dr. Zaki Najib Mahmud mistakenly thought in his wonderful book about Jabir ibn Hayyan, that he decides that he will mention in the Book of Properties - what he reached through his personal experiment: what is correct he records, and what is invalid he rejects, and that what he extracts he will compare with the sayings of others. He decides that philosophers and non-scientists are equal in their experience of particular things, then he asks that what he has reached through his experiment be compared with the experiments of others (1). Jabir ibn Hayyan had two paths: the path of Aristotelian logic: syllogism and demonstration; and the path of the theologians (mutakallimun): which is qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid (analogical reasoning from the witnessed to the unseen). His experimental method necessitated that he take the second path, and moreover use the same expression. He decides - while investigating the manner of inference and deduction - that the attachment of one thing to another is 'from the witnessed to the unseen in three aspects, namely: a) Similarity (mujanasa). b) Customary course (majra al-'ada). c) Effects (athar). (a) Indication of similarity: the model (anmudhaj). Jabir ibn Hayyan calls the indication of similarity 'anmudhaj', because it is based on inference from a partial model to another partial model, or from partial models to arrive at a universal judgment, which corresponds to 'selected facts' in the modern inductive method. Jabir says: 'A man sees from his companion a part of something to indicate by it that the whole of that thing is similar to this part.' Ibn Hayyan holds that the indication of this category in this aspect is not a fixed correct indication, i.e. he holds that it is not a certain indication but rather probable or possible. But he mentions - and here he explains the source from which he took this indication - that a group of 'the people of theoretical inquiry' i.e. the mutakallimun, have inferred from this category what they have inference upon it necessarily, i.e. they held the certainty of this indication. 'I mean that they established, on account of this thing which is the model, for example, and is of its genus, another thing which is more than it.' But Jabir ibn Hayyan does not agree with this and holds that this indication is neither necessary nor fixed in every case, 'because this thing which is the model, for example, does not necessitate the existence of another thing of its genus, whose judgment in substance and nature is its judgment (1).' Jabir ibn Hayyan thus became aware of the existence of qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid among the mutakallimun, and called for its application in experiment. However, the mutakallimun held that the results of this qiyas are certain if applied in theology, but he - in agreement with the spirit of his experimental method - does not hold this. Rather, he holds that the Manichaeans, in their debate with the mutakallimun, used the qiyas of these latter. They said: 'If in the world there is light and darkness, good and evil, beautiful and ugly, then there must be outside this world also light and darkness and all that they mentioned, being universals for these.' Jabir ibn Hayyan holds that this inference is not necessary, not apodictically necessary, until they prove that what is in the world of these are 'parts and fractions'. But before that, it is not necessitated by what they made necessary. It is possible that in the world these are universals in themselves, not parts. Therefore, this indication is not valid until we necessarily demonstrate that what is in the world of these are fractions and parts. So there is no certainty in this inference until we reach the quantity of these fractions. He says: 'Do you not see that the model is not established with him by certain knowledge, i.e. he has of that nothing other than what I see (2).' This is valuable speech on method, in which he affirms the probability and conjectural nature of experiment, and he shows that he has clarified experimentally these facts that he mentioned by an applied demonstration in his books on medicine, the four stones, al-Tajmi', al-Midan, and al-Mizan. And he holds - and here he contradicts himself - that if his experiments are gathered and their causes studied 'in succession and permanence, knowledge will come out of them and kindle'. Perhaps he meant that he has attained precision in
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage discusses the methodology of Jabir ibn Hayyan, a prominent early Muslim alchemist and scientist. It opens with a critical remark about Dr. Zaki Najib Mahmud's interpretation, then introduces the two intellectual paths available to Jabir: Aristotelian logic (qiyas and burhan) and the theologians' method (qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid). Because of his empirical approach, Jabir adopts the second path. He then classifies the types of inference from the witnessed (shahid) to the unseen (gha'ib) into three categories: similarity (mujanasa), customary course (majra al-'ada), and effects (athar). The focus is on the first: dalaalat al-mujanasa, which he calls anmudhaj (model). Jabir explains that this type infers from a part to the whole or from one particular to another, similar to modern induction. However, he argues that this indication is not certain but only probable. He contrasts this with the mutakallimun, who claimed that such inference yields certainty. Jabir provides a counterexample: if we see a model of a thing, it does not necessitate the existence of another thing of the same kind. He then discusses the Manichaean argument that from the existence of opposing qualities in the world, one can infer similar universals outside the world. Jabir rejects this as not necessary until it is proven that the worldly instances are parts and not universals themselves. The passage concludes by noting Jabir's insistence on the probability of experimental knowledge, yet he paradoxically suggests that repeated experiments might yield certainty—a point the author highlights as a contradiction. Overall, the passage clarifies the nature of analogical reasoning as understood by Jabir and his epistemological position that such reasoning is at best probable, not certain, especially when applied beyond the empirical domain.

Checkpoint
According to Jabir ibn Hayyan, how many types of indication from the witnessed to the unseen does he identify?
A) Two: similarity and effects
B) Three: similarity, customary course, and effects
C) Four: genus, species, custom, and effects
D) One: similarity only

Key Takeaway
Jabir classified analogical inference into three distinct patterns, with the first (anmudhaj) being the foundation of inductive reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify which of Jabir's three types of indication is being used in the following example: A doctor observes that a specific herb cures fever in several patients and concludes it will cure fever in all similar cases. Explain your reasoning based on the passage.
?
Construct a brief analogical argument from your own knowledge of Islamic rulings (e.g., using the prohibition of khamr as the 'illa) following the pattern of anmudhaj as described by Jabir.

Method of Learning
advanced
10 min
Jabir's Critique of Certainty in Analogical Inference
Jabir critiques the certainty of analogical inference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate Jabir's argument that analogical inference (especially anmudhaj) yields only probability, not certainty, and contrast his view with the mutakallimun.

Core Idea
Analogy from the witnessed to the unseen is probable, not certain—Jabir's empirical humility.

Jabir ibn Hayyan did not merely adopt the theologians' method; he critically examined its epistemic yield. While the mutakallimun claimed that qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid produces certainty (yaqin), Jabir—consistent with his experimental approach—insisted it is only probable (zanniyya). He supported this with a logical argument and a concrete example from the Manichaeans.

Guided Path
1
Extract Jabir's thesis on the epistemic status of anmudhaj
Locate where Jabir states that the indication of the model is not fixed (thabita) but rather probable (zanniyya). Contrast this with the mutakallimun who claim it yields certainty.
Jabir says: 'لا دلالة ثابتة صحيحة' and 'ليست دلالة يقينية بل ظنية أو احتمالية'.
2
Understand Jabir's counterexample to the mutakallimun
Jabir argues that seeing a model (anmudhaj) of a thing does not necessitate the existence of another thing of its genus. Why? Because the model could be unique or could be a universal itself, not a part.
The key phrase: 'لا يوجب وجود شيء آخر من جنسه'.
3
Analyze the Manichaean argument and Jabir's rebuttal
The Manichaeans argued: since the world contains light and darkness, good and evil, there must be universals of these outside the world. Jabir rejects this as non-necessary. Identify his reason: perhaps the worldly instances are themselves universals, not parts.
Jabir's condition: one must first prove that the worldly instances are 'parts and fractions' (أجزاء وأبعاض) before inferring external universals.
4
Evaluate the strength of Jabir's critique
Consider whether Jabir's objection actually refutes the Manichaean argument or merely shows it is not demonstrative. He says the inference is not 'daruri' (necessary) until the part-whole relationship is established.
This step highlights the logical structure of his critique: he is demanding a missing premise.

This passage discusses the methodology of Jabir ibn Hayyan, a prominent early Muslim alchemist and scientist. It opens with a critical remark about Dr. Zaki Najib Mahmud's interpretation, then introduces the two intellectual paths available to Jabir: Aristotelian logic (qiyas and burhan) and the theologians' method (qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid). Because of his empirical approach, Jabir adopts the second path. He then classifies the types of inference from the witnessed (shahid) to the unseen (gha'ib) into three categories: similarity (mujanasa), customary course (majra al-'ada), and effects (athar). The focus is on the first: dalaalat al-mujanasa, which he calls anmudhaj (model). Jabir explains that this type infers from a part to the whole or from one particular to another, similar to modern induction. However, he argues that this indication is not certain but only probable. He contrasts this with the mutakallimun, who claimed that such inference yields certainty. Jabir provides a counterexample: if we see a model of a thing, it does not necessitate the existence of another thing of the same kind. He then discusses the Manichaean argument that from the existence of opposing qualities in the world, one can infer similar universals outside the world. Jabir rejects this as not necessary until it is proven that the worldly instances are parts and not universals themselves. The passage concludes by noting Jabir's insistence on the probability of experimental knowledge, yet he paradoxically suggests that repeated experiments might yield certainty—a point the author highlights as a contradiction. Overall, the passage clarifies the nature of analogical reasoning as understood by Jabir and his epistemological position that such reasoning is at best probable, not certain, especially when applied beyond the empirical domain.

A) The witness could be lying

Key Takeaway
Jabir ibn Hayyan refuted the claim that analogical reasoning yields certainty, establishing it as a probabilistic method suitable for empirical investigation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find a contemporary argument in Islamic theology that uses analogical reasoning (e.g., from the perfection of the universe to the perfection of the Creator). Apply Jabir's critique to assess whether the argument achieves certainty or only probability.
?
Construct your own brief analogical argument from a fiqh example (e.g., qiyas for a new financial contract) and then add Jabir's condition: you must first prove that the original case and the new case share the same underlying cause ('illa) and that the cause is not unique to the original.

Method of Learning
intermediate

Ibn al-Haytham's Method: Induction vs. Aristotelian Syllogistic
Author introduces Ibn al-Haytham as a model of inductive method and poses methodological question.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the inductive method from the Aristotelian syllogistic method and analyse Ibn al-Haytham's approach as presented by the author.

Core Idea
The question is not whether Ibn al-Haytham used induction, but whether he broke with Aristotle's deductive framework.

This passage introduces Ibn al-Haytham as a second model of the inductive method adopted by Muslim scholars. The author, however, raises a critical methodological question: did Ibn al-Haytham follow the Aristotelian syllogistic method or depart from it? This is not merely historical; it forces us to clarify the relationship between inductive observation and deductive reasoning in Islamic science.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two methods contrasted
Find the phrases 'inductive method' (al-manhaj al-istiqrāʾī) and 'Aristotelian syllogistic method' (al-manhaj al-qiyāsī al-arīsṭūṭālīsī). Define each method in your own words: one moves from specific observations to general principles, the other from universal premises to specific conclusions.
The author sets up a dichotomy between these two approaches.
2
Locate the central question
The author asks: 'Did he follow the Aristotelian syllogistic method or depart from it?' Underline this question. Consider why the author considers this important for understanding Ibn al-Haytham's methodology.
This question frames the entire discussion.
3
Note the role of Mustafa Nazif's study
The author credits Mustafa Nazif with highlighting Ibn al-Haytham's originality, even arguing that later European scientists should be credited to him. Reflect on how modern scholarship can reshape our understanding of historical methods.
Nazif's work provides the evidence for the author's inquiry.
4
Reflect on the author's restriction
The author says he is only discussing method, not the history of theories. Consider how separating method from content allows a focused epistemological analysis.
This clarifies the scope of the lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثانياً الحسن بن الهيثم ومنهجه التجريبي . أما النموذج الثاني من نماذج أخذ علماء المسلمين بالمنهج الإستقرائي . فهو نموذج الحسن بن الهيثم ( المتوفي عام ٤١١هـ ـ ١٠٢٠م ) . وقد كان الحسن بن الهيثم أكبر عالم رياضي وطبيعي في العصور الوسطى . وما زال لآرائه ونظرياته في الرياضة وفي البصريات مكانها حتى الآن ، وقد كان الفضل الأكبر في إبراز معالم نظريات ابن الهيثم وآرائه وبخاصة بحوثه وكشوفه النظرية لعالم عربي مصري هو الأستاذ مصطفى نظيف . وقد بين الأستاذ مصطفى نظيف أصالة هذا العالم العظيم في نظرياته وآرائه ، بحيث يقرر في مقدمة كتابه أنه ينبغي أن يستبدل أسماء روجر بيكون ، ومورليكوس وليوناردو دافنشي ودلا بورتا وكبلر باسم الحسن بن الهيثم(١) . وإنني لا أعرض في هذا الكتاب لتاريخ النظريات والآراء العلمية عند المسلمين ، وانما أتكلم فقط عن المنهج . وسأحاول أن أعرض لمنهج الحسن بن الهيثم ، هل تابع المنهج القياسي الأرسططاليسي ، أم خرج عليه . وقد عرض الأستاذ مصطفى نظيف في عبقرية نادرة لمنهج الحسن بن الهيثم في الفصل الأول من الباب الأول من كتابه ، وتوصل الى نتائج نادرة خلال دراسته لكتاب المناظر لابن الهيثم دراسة علمية نافذة .
Secondly: Hasan ibn al-Haytham and his experimental method. The second model from the models of Muslim scholars adopting the inductive method is the model of Hasan ibn al-Haytham (died 411 AH / 1020 CE). He was the greatest mathematician and natural scientist of the Middle Ages. His opinions and theories in mathematics and optics still hold their place today. The greatest credit for highlighting the features of Ibn al-Haytham's theories and opinions, especially his theoretical research and discoveries, goes to an Egyptian Arab scholar, Professor Mustafa Nazif. Professor Mustafa Nazif has demonstrated the originality of this great scholar in his theories and opinions, such that he states in the introduction of his book that the names of Roger Bacon, Morolicus, Leonardo da Vinci, Della Porta, and Kepler should be replaced with the name of Hasan ibn al-Haytham. I am not presenting in this book the history of scientific theories and opinions among Muslims; rather, I speak only about the method. I will attempt to present the method of Hasan ibn al-Haytham: did he follow the Aristotelian syllogistic method or depart from it? Professor Mustafa Nazif presented, with rare genius, the method of Hasan ibn al-Haytham in the first chapter of the first part of his book, and arrived at rare results during his penetrating scientific study of Ibn al-Haytham's Book of Optics.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage introduces Hasan ibn al-Haytham as a second exemplar of the inductive method in Islamic science. The author states that Muslim scholars adopted the inductive method, and Ibn al-Haytham is a prime example. He was a leading mathematician and natural scientist whose work in optics remains influential. The author acknowledges the contribution of the modern scholar Mustafa Nazif in highlighting Ibn al-Haytham's originality, even arguing that several European scientists should be credited to Ibn al-Haytham. However, the author clarifies that the focus here is not on the history of theories but on methodology. The central question is whether Ibn al-Haytham followed the Aristotelian syllogistic method or departed from it. The author references Mustafa Nazif's study of Ibn al-Haytham's Book of Optics as providing rare insights into his method.

Key Terms
المنهج الإستقرائي
المنهج القياسي الأرسططاليسي

Key Takeaway
The passage sets up the question of whether Ibn al-Haytham's empirical method represents a departure from Aristotelian deductive syllogistic, a key epistemological issue in Islamic science.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a claim in Islamic theology: 'The world is temporally originated because it is composed of atoms that are contingent.' Identify whether this argument is more deductive (Aristotelian syllogistic) or inductive. Explain your reasoning.
?
Imagine a brief description of Ibn al-Haytham's experiments on light reflection: He observed that light reflects at equal angles and formulated a general law. Classify the reasoning steps as inductive or deductive, and note any deductive elements (e.g., mathematical derivation).
